Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
Definition and Division of Spirits Attached to Bodies
Author defines spirits attached to bodies and divides them into celestial and elemental directors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of spirits attached to bodies and their two main categories: celestial directors and elemental directors.

Core Idea
Spirits attached to bodies are immaterial directors of celestial and elemental realms.

In this lesson, we examine the second kind of non-spatial beings: spirits that are immaterial but manage bodies. The author clarifies their nature and then divides them into two fundamental types based on the bodies they direct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Let the author's own definitions come through.
Notice the careful phrasing: 'مجردة في ذواتها عن الجسمية والحلول في الجسمية'.
2
Identify the definition
Look for the description of these spirits: they are substances that are immaterial in essence but attached to bodies for direction and management.
They are not bodies nor do they inhere in bodies, but they have a relation of management.
3
Understand the division
The author divides these spirits into two: those directing celestial bodies and those directing elemental bodies. Note the distinction.
Celestial bodies refer to the spheres and stars; elemental bodies refer to earthly matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني فهو الجواهر التي تكون مجردة في ذواتها عن الجسمية والحلول في الجسمية إلا أنها متعلقة بالأجسام على سبيل التدبير والتصرف. وهذا القسم ينقسم إلى قسمين: لأنها إما أن تكون مدبرة للأجسام الفلكية، أو الأجسام العنصرية. ولما ثبت بالدليل أنه حصل خارج العالم خلاء لا نهاية له، ولم يثبت بالدليل أن العالم واحد، بل ثبت أنه لا يمتنع وجود عوالم غير هذا العالم. وبتقدير ثبوتها، فيحصل هناك من زمر الأرواح المقدسة ما لا يعلمها إلا الله تعالى، ولا يعلمها البشر البتة. إلا أن البحث عن تلك الأحوال غير ممكن. فلهذا السبب اقتصرت العقول على البحث عن الأرواح المدبرة للأجسام الفلكية، أو الأرواح المدبرة للأجسام العنصرية.
As for the second division, it consists of the substances that are immaterial in their essences from corporeality and from inherence in corporeality, except that they are attached to bodies in the manner of direction and management. This division is divided into two parts: because they either direct the celestial bodies or the elemental bodies. And since it has been established by proof that there exists outside the world a void without end, and it has not been established by proof that the world is one; rather it has been established that the existence of worlds other than this world is not impossible. And if their existence is assumed, then there will come to be there from the troops of holy spirits what only Allah knows, and humans do not know at all. However, inquiry into those states is not possible. For this reason, intellects restricted themselves to inquiry about the spirits that direct celestial bodies, or the spirits that direct elemental bodies.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage describes the second kind of non-spatial beings: those that are immaterial in essence but attached to bodies as directors and managers. They are divided into two types: those that direct celestial bodies (like the spheres and stars) and those that direct elemental bodies (earthly matters). The author then adds a note: it has been proven that there is an infinite void outside the world, and it has not been proven that the world is one; rather, multiple worlds are not impossible. If such worlds exist, they would have their own holy spirits unknown to humans. However, inquiry into those is not possible, so intellects restrict themselves to studying the spirits that direct celestial and elemental bodies. This shows the author's methodological restraint.

Key Terms
مدبرة للفلكية
مدبرة للعنصرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the nature of the spirits in the second division?
A) They are material.
B) They are immaterial but attached to bodies for direction.
C) They are wholly spiritual with no relation to bodies.
D) They are composite.

Key Takeaway
Spirits attached to bodies are immaterial directors, divided into those that manage celestial bodies and those that manage elemental bodies.

The Scope of Inquiry: Why Only Celestial and Elemental Spirits?
Author explains why research is limited to celestial and elemental spirits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author restricts inquiry to spirits that direct celestial and elemental bodies, excluding spirits from hypothetical other worlds.

Core Idea
Inquiry is limited to what is possible to investigate; other worlds are not impossible but unknowable.

After defining spirits attached to bodies, the author addresses a broader question: why do scholars only study spirits that direct celestial and elemental bodies? The reason is methodological: other worlds may exist, but their spirits are beyond human knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Pay attention to the logical flow.
The author uses 'ولما ثبت' to indicate established proofs.
2
Identify the two established points
The author mentions that an infinite void exists outside the world and that multiple worlds are not impossible. Note these as background.
These are not assumptions but conclusions from proofs.
3
Understand the implication
If other worlds exist, their holy spirits are known only to Allah. Humans cannot know them.
This leads to the conclusion that inquiry into them is impossible.
4
See the final restriction
Thus, intellects restrict themselves to the spirits that direct celestial and elemental bodies, which are accessible to human reason.
This is a principle of methodological restraint.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني فهو الجواهر التي تكون مجردة في ذواتها عن الجسمية والحلول في الجسمية إلا أنها متعلقة بالأجسام على سبيل التدبير والتصرف. وهذا القسم ينقسم إلى قسمين: لأنها إما أن تكون مدبرة للأجسام الفلكية، أو الأجسام العنصرية. ولما ثبت بالدليل أنه حصل خارج العالم خلاء لا نهاية له، ولم يثبت بالدليل أن العالم واحد، بل ثبت أنه لا يمتنع وجود عوالم غير هذا العالم. وبتقدير ثبوتها، فيحصل هناك من زمر الأرواح المقدسة ما لا يعلمها إلا الله تعالى، ولا يعلمها البشر البتة. إلا أن البحث عن تلك الأحوال غير ممكن. فلهذا السبب اقتصرت العقول على البحث عن الأرواح المدبرة للأجسام الفلكية، أو الأرواح المدبرة للأجسام العنصرية.
As for the second division, it consists of the substances that are immaterial in their essences from corporeality and from inherence in corporeality, except that they are attached to bodies in the manner of direction and management. This division is divided into two parts: because they either direct the celestial bodies or the elemental bodies. And since it has been established by proof that there exists outside the world a void without end, and it has not been established by proof that the world is one; rather it has been established that the existence of worlds other than this world is not impossible. And if their existence is assumed, then there will come to be there from the troops of holy spirits what only Allah knows, and humans do not know at all. However, inquiry into those states is not possible. For this reason, intellects restricted themselves to inquiry about the spirits that direct celestial bodies, or the spirits that direct elemental bodies.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage describes the second kind of non-spatial beings: those that are immaterial in essence but attached to bodies as directors and managers. They are divided into two types: those that direct celestial bodies (like the spheres and stars) and those that direct elemental bodies (earthly matters). The author then adds a note: it has been proven that there is an infinite void outside the world, and it has not been proven that the world is one; rather, multiple worlds are not impossible. If such worlds exist, they would have their own holy spirits unknown to humans. However, inquiry into those is not possible, so intellects restrict themselves to studying the spirits that direct celestial and elemental bodies. This shows the author's methodological restraint.

Key Terms
خلاء لا نهاية له
عوالم
أرواح مقدسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author asserts that multiple worlds definitely exist.
Correction: The author only says multiple worlds are not impossible, not that they exist.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the reason intellects restrict research to celestial and elemental spirits?
A) Other worlds are impossible.
B) Inquiry into other worlds is not possible.
C) Celestial spirits are more important.
D) Elemental spirits are easier to study.

Key Takeaway
The author limits theological inquiry to spirits that can be investigated, avoiding speculation about hypothetical other worlds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows scholars limiting themselves to what is knowable. Where in your life are you spending mental energy on questions about the unseen that Allah has not commanded you to investigate? How can you redirect that energy to acting on what you do know from revelation?
?
Do you truly trust that Allah alone knows the full reality of other worlds and spirits, or do you secretly feel uneasy leaving those matters unknown? What would it look like for you to surrender that unease to Him today?
?
The author says 'inquiry into those states is not possible.' What is one area of your faith where you have been trying to force certainty or answers where Allah has not provided them? Will you commit to stopping that search and instead focus on the clear commands He has given you?

Argument by Analogy for Souls Administering the Sublunar Sphere
Analogical argument for spirits managing sublunar realm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy of innate heat in the heart is used to argue for the existence of souls governing the sublunar realm.

Core Idea
If tiny heart heat needs a soul, vast lunar heat must need souls too.

This passage presents a fascinating analogical argument from Islamic theology. It compares the innate heat in the human heart, which requires a soul to manage it, to the subtle heat pervading the region under the moon. The author argues that just as the heart's heat is not without a soul, the greater heat of the sublunar sphere must also be administered by spirits. We'll examine the logic and the additional support from observable phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Notice the key terms and the structure of the analogy. Pay attention to how the author moves from the known (heart heat) to the unknown (sublunar heat).
The Arabic uses a conditional 'if...then' structure.
2
Identify the analogy components
Find the two parts: the innate heat in the heart requiring a soul, and the fine heat under the moon. Trace how the analogy is drawn.
3
Consider the additional arguments
The passage also mentions many spirits attaching to other spheres and uses the example of worms in snowy mountains. Reflect on how these support the main analogy.
This strengthens the plausibility of the claim.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Ask yourself why this analogy matters for understanding causality and the role of spirits in the natural world.
It shows that even physical processes may have spiritual agents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تلك الحرارة تشبه الحرارة الغريزية [ فإذا كانت الحرارة الغريزية (١) ] التي في القلب مع قلتها ، لم تخل من نفس تدبرها ، وتتعلق بها ، فالحرارة اللطيفة الموجودة تحت فلك القمر ، كيف يمكن خلوها عن نفوس تتعلق بها وتديرها ؟ ومنهم من قال : لا يمتنع أيضاً تعلق الأرواح الكثيرة [ بها(٢) ، و] بكرة النسيم ، وبكرة الزمهرير. فإنا نشاهد أن الجبال التي تكثر فيها الثلوج ، قد يتولد فيها ديدان عظيمة ، تشبه زقاقاً رقيقة مملوءة من الماء العذب البارد ، فإذا لم يمتنع تعلق الأرواح بها ، فكذلك لا يمتنع تعلق الأرواح بهذه الكرات . فهذا تفصيل الكلام في هذا الباب
That heat resembles the innate heat. For if the innate heat that is in the heart, despite its smallness, is not devoid of a soul that manages it and is attached to it, then the fine heat existing under the sphere of the moon—how can it be devoid of souls attached to it and managing it? And some of them said: It is also not impossible for many spirits to attach to it and to the sphere of the gentle breeze (al-nasim) and the sphere of extreme cold (al-zamharir). For we observe that mountains in which snow abounds sometimes generate great worms resembling thin skins filled with cold sweet water. So if the attachment of spirits to them is not impossible, then likewise the attachment of spirits to these spheres is not impossible. This is the detailed discussion on this topic.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage presents an argument by analogy to prove the existence of souls administering the sublunar region (the area under the moon's sphere). The reasoning: the innate heat in the human heart, although small, requires a soul to manage it and be attached to it. By analogy, the fine, subtle heat present in the whole region under the moon—vast and pervasive—must also require souls to manage it. The passage then adds another argument from authority: some scholars hold that many spirits can attach themselves even to the spheres of the gentle breeze (al-nasim) and extreme cold (al-zamharir). An empirical observation is cited: mountains with snow produce worms that are like thin skins filled with cold water. Since spirits can attach to these worms, it is not far-fetched that they attach to those celestial spheres. Thus the author concludes that the existence of such spirits is plausible and the matter is detailed here.

Key Terms
الحرارة الغريزية
فلك القمر
كرة النسيم
كرة الزمهرير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about physical heat and physical causes.
Correction: The heat is a metaphor for a subtle substance that requires spiritual administration, not just physical explanation.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the analogy used to argue for spirits under the moon?
A) The heat of the sun.
B) The innate heat in the heart requiring a soul.
C) The cold of the mountains.
D) The movements of the planets.

Key Takeaway
The analogy of innate heat serves as a key argument for the existence of spiritual administrators in the sublunar realm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that even your heart's heat requires a soul to manage it. Have you ever truly considered that your own body's functioning depends on a spiritual reality? How does that change the way you treat your physical health?
?
If the entire sublunar realm is administered by spirits, do you live with the awareness that unseen forces are at work behind natural events? What would change in your daily life if you constantly remembered that?
?
The author uses an example of worms in mountains to show that spirits can attach to even the most humble creations. Are there 'small' or overlooked aspects of your life that you neglect spiritually? How can you invite God's administration into those areas?

beginner
5 min
Proof for Celestial Souls via Analogy
The author proves celestial souls via analogy with heart's innate heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for the existence of celestial souls based on an analogy with the innate heat of the heart.

Core Idea
Celestial heat needs souls as much as the heart's heat does.

The author begins by noting that certain spirits like angels of provisions and war are affirmed by the Prophet and by talisman practitioners, so their existence demands investigation. He then presents a careful analogy: just as the small innate heat in the heart requires a governing soul, the vast subtle heat under the moon must also be directed by souls. This lesson unpacks that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the author's progression from authority to analogy.
2
Identify the key analogy
Understand the comparison between heart's heat and celestial heat.
3
Consider the additional evidence
Note the example of worms in snowy mountains showing spirits can attach to natural phenomena.
4
Reflect on the structure
See how the proof builds from observation to conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وملك الأرزاق ، وملائكة الحرب ، فهذا كلام أثبته رسول الله . وأقر به . وجمهور أصحاب الطلسمات يثبتون هذا النوع من الأرواح ، ويقرون به ، فيجب البحث عن وجود هذا النوع . تلك الحرارة تشبه الحرارة الغريزية فإذا كانت الحرارة الغريزية التي في القلب مع قلتها ، لم تخل من نفس تدبرها ، وتتعلق بها ، فالحرارة اللطيفة الموجودة تحت فلك القمر ، كيف يمكن خلوها عن نفوس تتعلق بها وتديرها ؟ ومنهم من قال : لا يمتنع أيضاً تعلق الأرواح الكثيرة بها ، وبكرة النسيم ، وبكرة الزمهرير. فإنا نشاهد أن الجبال التي تكثر فيها الثلوج ، قد يتولد فيها ديدان عظيمة ، تشبه زقاقاً رقيقة مملوءة من الماء العذب البارد ، فإذا لم يمتنع تعلق الأرواح بها ، فكذلك لا يمتنع تعلق الأرواح بهذه الكرات . فهذا تفصيل الكلام في هذا الباب والحاصل : أن مراتب المفارقات عند الفلاسفة المتأخرين كأبي نصر الفارابي و أبي علي بن سينا أربعة . فأعلاها وأشرفها : واجب الوجود لذاته ، ثم العقول ، ثم النفوس السماوية ، ثم النفوس البشرية . ولا يعترفون بالجن والشياطين . وأما أرباب الملل والنحل ، وأصحاب الطلسمات . فإنهم يثبتون الجن والشياطين ، وسائر الأقسام . والله أعلم .
And the angel of provisions and the angels of war — this is a statement affirmed by the Messenger of God and he acknowledged it. And the majority of talisman practitioners affirm this type of spirits and acknowledge them, so it is necessary to investigate the existence of this type. That heat resembles the innate heat. For if the innate heat that is in the heart, despite its smallness, is not devoid of a soul that manages it and is attached to it, then the subtle heat existing under the sphere of the moon — how can it be devoid of souls attached to it and directing it? And among them is one who said: It is also not impossible for many spirits to be attached to it, to the sphere of the breeze, and to the sphere of the extreme cold. For we observe that mountains in which snow abounds sometimes produce great worms resembling thin skins filled with sweet cold water. So if it is not impossible for spirits to be attached to them, then likewise it is not impossible for spirits to be attached to these spheres. This is the detailed discussion on this topic. And the conclusion: That the ranks of separate entities according to the later philosophers, such as Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Abu Ali ibn Sina, are four. The highest and most noble of them is the Necessary Existence in Itself, then the intellects, then the celestial souls, then the human souls. And they do not acknowledge jinn and devils. As for the people of religions and sects, and the talisman practitioners, they affirm jinn and devils and all the other categories. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is both a proof for the existence of celestial souls and a classification of separate entities. The author begins by stating that certain spiritual beings—angels of provisions and war—are affirmed by the Prophet and by talisman practitioners, so their existence must be investigated. He then presents a proof by analogy: just as the small innate heat in the heart cannot exist without a soul that manages it, the subtle heat under the lunar sphere cannot be devoid of souls that manage it. This analogy argues that celestial heat—the principle of motion in the sublunary region—requires directing souls, i.e., celestial souls. Next, he reports an additional claim: it is not impossible for many spirits to be attached to the celestial spheres, using the example of mountains producing worms from snow. This suggests that even inanimate or natural phenomena can have spiritual attachments. Finally, he summarizes the hierarchy of separate entities according to later philosophers like al-Farabi and Ibn Sina: the Necessary Existent, intellects, celestial souls, and human souls. He notes that these philosophers deny jinn and devils, whereas religious communities and talisman practitioners affirm them. The passage thus establishes a rational proof for celestial souls while distinguishing between philosophical and religious views on spiritual hierarchies.

Key Terms
أرواح
حرارة غريزية
نفوس
فلك القمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is about the human soul or the soul of the heart.
Correction: The analogy uses the heart's innate heat only as an example to argue for celestial souls, not to discuss the human soul itself.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the author's proof for the existence of celestial souls?
A) The authority of the Prophet and talisman practitioners
B) The analogy of the heart's innate heat and the subtle heat under the moon
C) The observation of worms in snowy mountains
D) The philosophical classification of separate entities

Key Takeaway
The subtle heat under the moon requires celestial souls to direct it, just as the heart's heat requires a soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel the warmth of the sun or the chill of winter, do you consciously remember that these are directed by celestial souls? How does this awareness change your sense of connection to the unseen world? What is one practical step you can take today to live more mindful of the spiritual management of the universe?
?
If the innate heat in your heart requires a soul to manage it, do you ever pause to reflect on the fact that your very heartbeat is sustained by a command from Allah? How does this realization affect your gratitude and reliance on Him? Will you take a moment today to silently thank Him for that constant management?

Classification of Separate Entities: Philosophers vs Religious Views
The author classifies separate entities into four ranks and contrasts philosophical and religious views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical hierarchy of separate entities and the contrast with religious views on jinn and devils.

Core Idea
Philosophers rank four separate entities; they deny jinn, while religion affirms them.

After establishing the proof for celestial souls, the author concludes with a classification of separate entities according to later philosophers. He presents a hierarchy from the Necessary Existent down to human souls, and notes that philosophers reject jinn and devils, unlike religious communities and talisman practitioners. This lesson outlines that hierarchy and the sectarian difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Focus on the four ranks and their order.
2
Understand the denial of jinn
Note that philosophers exclude jinn and devils.
3
Compare with religious view
See how religious groups affirm additional spiritual entities.
4
Reflect on the implications
Why does this classification matter for theology?

As for the people of religions and sects, and the talisman practitioners, they affirm jinn and devils and all the other categories. And God knows best.

The author begins by stating that certain spiritual beings—angels of provisions and war—are affirmed by the Prophet and by talisman practitioners, so their existence must be investigated. He then presents a proof by analogy: just as the small innate heat in the heart cannot exist without a soul that manages it, the subtle heat under the lunar sphere cannot be devoid of souls that manage it. This analogy argues that celestial heat—the principle of motion in the sublunary region—requires directing souls, i.e., celestial souls. Next, he reports an additional claim: it is not impossible for many spirits to be attached to the celestial spheres, using the example of mountains producing worms from snow. This suggests that even inanimate or natural phenomena can have spiritual attachments. Finally, he summarizes the hierarchy of separate entities according to later philosophers like al-Farabi and Ibn Sina: the Necessary Existent, intellects, celestial souls, and human souls. He notes that these philosophers deny jinn and devils, whereas religious communities and talisman practitioners affirm them. The passage thus establishes a rational proof for celestial souls while distinguishing between philosophical and religious views on spiritual hierarchies.

Correction: Later philosophers like al-Farabi and Ibn Sina deny the existence of jinn and devils, unlike religious traditions.

Hyle: Pure Receptivity Without Activity
Al-Razi defines the second category of existents: matter that receives influence but does not act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of hyle as passive matter, its two definitions (kalam and philosophical), and its nature as pure potentiality.

Core Idea
Hyle is pure passivity—it receives all qualities but has none of its own.

Al-Razi continues his classification of existents by turning to the second category: that which is affected but does not affect. This category is hyle, or prime matter. The passage introduces two competing definitions—from the mutakallimun and from the philosophers—and then clarifies that hyle has no inherent qualities; it is mere receptivity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the full translation. Notice the structure: classification, definitions, then conclusion about hyle's lack of qualities.
The passage ends mid-sentence, so we focus on what is provided.
2
Identify the two definitions of hyle
Find where Al-Razi gives the view of the mutakallimun (indivisible parts) and of the philosophers (non-spatially-extended substrate). Compare how each defines the fundamental matter of the corporeal world.
These definitions reflect different ontological commitments.
3
Understand the significance of hyle's lack of qualities
Notice that Al-Razi says hyle in itself is not hot, cold, moist, dry, joined, or separated. This means hyle is pure potentiality—it can receive any quality but is not determined by any.
This distinguishes hyle from substances with fixed attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو الموجود الذي يتأثر ولا يؤثر فهو « الهيولى » وقد عرفت في كتاب « الهيولى ) : أن هيولى العالم الجسماني ، هي الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ وعند الفلاسفة : هيولى عالم الأجسام [ موجود ] ليس بمتحيز ، وصورتها هي الحجمية والتحيز . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : تلك الأجزاء من حيث هي هي ، ليست حارة ولا باردة ، ولا رطبة ولا يابسة ، ولا مجتمعة ولا مفترقة ، بل هي قابلة لهذه

And according to the philosophers: the hyle of the world of bodies is a existent that is not spatially extended, and its form is magnitude and spatial extension. If you have known this, we say: those parts, in themselves, are neither hot nor cold, nor moist nor dry, nor joined nor separated, but rather they are receptive to these.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage describes the second category from the earlier classification: that which is affected but does not affect. The author identifies this as 'hyle' (matter). He refers to a previous work 'al-Hayula' for details. Two views are given: First, that the hyle of the corporeal world consists of indivisible parts (atoms). Second, according to philosophers (falasifa), hyle is a non-spatially-extended substrate whose form is magnitude and spatial extension. He then explains that these parts in themselves have no determinate qualities—not hot, cold, moist, dry, joined, or separated—but are merely receptive to such qualities. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating that hyle is pure potentiality.

Spirits as Affected Beings: The First Proof
Al-Razi introduces the third division and proves spirits are affected.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's classification of existents into three groups and the rational proof that spirits are affected (dependent on God) because they are possible existents.

Core Idea
Every possible being, including spirits, needs a cause to exist.

Al-Razi begins by identifying the third class of existents—those that both act and are acted upon. This includes the world of spirits and souls. To establish their dependency on God, he provides a concise proof based on the nature of possibility and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the concise logical structure.
2
Identify the two investigations
Al-Razi announces two investigations needed regarding this third division. The passage covers only the first: proving spirits are affected.
3
Follow the logical chain of the proof
Trace each step: Necessary Existent is one → everything else is possible → spirits are possible → possible needs a cause → spirits are affected.
This is a straightforward deduction.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider that being 'affected' here means receiving existence from God. It does not imply passivity in a negative sense, but rather dependence.
The common misunderstanding is clarified below.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث من أقسام الموجودات: وهو الذي يؤثر ويتأثر معاً. فهو عالم الأرواح والنفوس، ويجب البحث ههنا عن أمرين: الأول: إثبات أنها متأثرة. والدليل عليه: أن واجب الوجود لذاته: واحد، فيكون كل ما سواه: ممكناً لذاته. فهذه الأرواح والنفوس ممكنة لذواتها. والممكن لذاته لا يوجد إلا بمؤثر، فثبت أن هذه الأرواح قابلة للأثر.
As for the third division of divisions of existents: it is that which affects and is affected together. It is the world of spirits and souls. And here we must investigate two matters: the first: proving that they are affected. And the proof for it: that the Necessary of Existence in Himself is one, so everything other than Him is possible in itself. So these spirits and souls are possible in themselves. And the possible in itself does not exist except through an efficient cause. So it is established that these spirits are receptive to effect.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage introduces the third division of existents: beings that both affect and are affected. This is the world of spirits (arwah) and souls (nufus). Al-Razi then announces two investigations needed. The first investigation is to prove that these beings are affected (muta'aththira). The proof is straightforward: the Necessary Existent (God) is one, so everything else is possible in itself. Spirits and souls are among those possible beings, and every possible being requires an efficient cause to bring it into existence. Therefore, spirits are receptive to effect (it establishes that they are affected by God).

?

Proof that Only the One (God) is Effective
Al-Razi presents the first proof that only the Necessary Existent is truly effective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that only the Necessary Existent can be truly effective, based on the impossibility of one essence producing two effects.

Core Idea
One essence cannot produce two distinct effects — only God acts independently.

Al-Razi begins the second investigation by presenting a key theological debate: can created beings truly be effective agents? He introduces a proof that only the One (God), who is Necessary in Himself, can be an independent cause. The proof rests on the nature of possible beings and the principle that a single essence cannot necessitate two distinct things. Let's examine this argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a claim, then a proof, then a reference to philosophical principles.
The proof is self-contained within the first part.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on terms like 'مؤثرة' (effective), 'ماهية' (essence), 'مقتضية للإمكان' (necessitates possibility), 'أثرين' (two effects), 'محال' (impossible). Understand their meanings in context.
These terms form the backbone of the argument.
3
Understand the premise
Every possible being has an essence that necessitates possibility (imkan). This is its defining characteristic.
Possibility is a necessary concomitant of the essence.
4
Follow the logical consequence
If that same essence were to produce an effect, it would necessitate two distinct things: possibility (from its own nature) and the effect. This is impossible because a single essence cannot have two distinct necessary concomitants.
The proof thus shows that no possible being can be an independent cause.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Therefore, only the Necessary Existent (God) can be truly effective. This aligns with the philosophical principle 'the one does not issue from it except one'.
This proof is one of several supporting the same claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: إثبات أنها مؤثرة، وههنا يحصل البحث العظيم. فمن الثاني: من زعم: أنه لا يؤثر إلا الواحد، الذي هو واجب الوجود لذاته. واحتج عليه بوجوه: الحجة الأولى: إن كل ما كان ممكناً لذاته، فإن ماهيته مقتضية للإمكان، فلو كانت مؤثرة في وجود غيرها، لكانت الماهية الواحدة اقتضت أثرين، وهذا محال. وهذه الحجة مستقيمة على أصول الفلاسفة من وجهين: الأول: إن عندهم الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد.
The second: proving that they are effective. And here occurs the great research. So from the second [group]: there is he who claims that none affects except the One, who is the Necessary of Existence in Himself. And he argued for it with proofs: the first proof: that every possible in itself, its essence necessitates possibility. So if it were effective in the existence of another, then the one essence would necessitate two effects, and this is impossible. And this proof is consistent with the principles of the philosophers in two ways: the first: that according to them, the one does not issue from it except one.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents the second investigation concerning spirits: proving that they are effective (mu'aththira). Al-Razi notes that this is a great research. He then introduces a position held by some: that only the One (God) can affect, and no other being can truly be effective. The first proof for this position is given: every possible being has an essence (mahiyya) that necessitates possibility (imkan). If that essence were to produce an effect (i.e., cause something else to exist), then that single essence would necessitate two distinct effects: possibility and the effect. This is impossible because a single essence cannot have two necessary concomitants that are distinct. The proof is said to align with the philosophers' principle that from one thing only one thing proceeds (al-wahid la yasduru 'anhu illa al-wahid).

أثرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies any causality from created beings.
Correction: The proof only denies independent efficient causality; created beings can be instrumental causes.

Checkpoint

A) A possible essence cannot produce an effect because it is contingent.
B) A possible essence necessitates possibility, so if it produced an effect, one essence would necessitate two effects, which is impossible.
C) The Necessary Existent cannot have any effect.
D) Multiple effects require multiple causes.

Key Takeaway
Only the Necessary Existent can be truly effective; a possible being's essence precludes independent causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Think of a situation where you felt proud of an achievement. Does this lesson expose that pride as a form of shirk in causality? Are you willing to repent and ascribe all effective power to God alone, starting with the next thought you have today?

First Proof: One Essence Cannot Yield Two Effects
First proof: one possibility-necessitating essence cannot cause another effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against spirits being effectual causes: a single essence cannot necessitate two distinct effects.

Core Idea
A single simple essence cannot necessitate two different outcomes.

The first proof challenges the idea that possible beings can act as causes. It shows that a possible thing's own nature already determines one effect (its possibility), so it cannot also produce another. This argument is rooted in the philosophical principle that from one thing only one proceeds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the structure: the core argument followed by two supporting principles.
2
Identify the core argument
The proof says: every possible-in-itself has a quiddity that necessitates possibility. If it also caused an effect, the quiddity would necessitate two effects. This is impossible because the quiddity is simple.
Focus on the logical contradiction: one thing cannot require two different results.
3
Understand the two supporting principles
The author notes that the proof is valid according to the philosophers: (1) from one simple thing only one can proceed, (2) a simple entity cannot be both receptive and active simultaneously.
These principles reinforce why a single quiddity cannot cause an effect while being possible itself.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this argument refutes the idea that spirits (or any possible existent) can be effectual causes. If they were, their essence would have to do two things at once, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : إن كل ما كان ممكناً لذاته ، فإن ماهيته مقتضية للإمكان ، فلو كانت مؤثرة في وجود غيرها، لكانت الماهية الواحدة اقتضت أثرين ، وهذا محال . وهذه الحجة مستقيمة على أصول الفلاسفة من وجهين : الأول : إن عندهم الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد . الثاني : إن عندهم البسيط لا يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً معاً .
The first proof: Every thing that is possible in itself, its quiddity necessitates possibility. So if it were effectual in the existence of another, then the single quiddity would have necessitated two effects, and this is impossible. And this proof is sound according to the principles of the philosophers in two respects: First: For them, the one does not issue from it except the one. Second: For them, the simple cannot be both receptive and active together.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against the claim that spirits (or any possible existent) can be effectual causes. The argument is: A possible existent has a quiddity (mahiyya) that necessitates possibility in itself (i.e., its essence entails that it is possible, not necessary). If that same quiddity were also to necessitate an effect in another thing, then the single quiddity would necessitate two distinct effects: its own possibility and the effect. This is impossible because one simple thing cannot produce two distinct effects. The author then notes that this proof aligns with two principles held by the philosophers: 1) from the one, only one can proceed (al-wahid la yasduru illa al-wahid); 2) a simple thing cannot be both receptive and active simultaneously. Thus, from a philosophical standpoint, the proof is valid.

Key Terms
ماهية
إمكان
أثرين
واحد
بسيط
قابل
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'two effects' refers to two independent actions, but here it means two different necessitations (being possible and causing another) from the same essence.
Correction: The proof is about necessitations, not actions. The quiddity already necessitates one thing (possibility); adding another necessitates a second, which is impossible for a simple essence.

Checkpoint

B) Because its quiddity would then necessitate two effects, which is impossible
C) Because it is composite
D) Because it is temporal

Key Takeaway
A possible essence cannot be an effectual cause because its simple nature can only necessitate one thing (its possibility), not two.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If not, why do you still place so much trust in them? How would your day change if you consciously remembered that all effects come from Allah alone?
?
This proof shows that a single simple essence cannot necessitate two effects. You are not simple; you are made of many parts. But your very existence is a contingency. Do you live as if your existence is self-grounded, or as if it is constantly being given by the One who alone can cause? What is one concrete change you can make today to acknowledge your utter dependence on Him?

The philosophers' principle 'from one only one issues' is used here to highlight the impossibility of multiple effects from one essence. How does this challenge you when you try to ascribe multiple independent causes to events in your life (like your success, your provision, your safety)? Are you willing to see all of them as coming from Allah's single, undivided will? What would it cost you to truly believe that?

First Branch: If Multiple Effects Are Possible, Worship the Known God
Sanusi presents the first horn of a dilemma, concluding that reason requires worshipping the known God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first branch of the dilemma establishes that if a single agent can produce multiple effects, then reason requires worshipping only the known God.

Core Idea
If multiple effects are not impossible, the known God is sufficient, and the unknown is doubtful.

This lesson examines the first horn of the dilemma: assuming that one agent can produce many effects, the argument shows that since God is known as the effectual cause, we have no reason to worship anything else. The author supports this with a Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text for the first branch carefully, noting how the author sets up the condition and the conclusion.
The passage includes a Quranic verse (23:117) which the author uses as support.
2
Understand the reasoning
Identify the key steps: we know God exists, He is effectual, and if multiple effects are possible, then all effects can be from Him. Since He is known and others are doubtful, reason dictates worshipping Him alone.
3
Reflect on the Quranic support
Consider how the verse is used to reinforce the logic: why worshipping another without proof is like going to an unknown object of worship.
The verse condemns invoking another god without proof, aligning with the argument's conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أن نقول : إن الشيء الواحد . إما أن لا يمتنع كـونـه مصدراً للأثرين (۱) أو يمتنع . فإن لم يمتنع ذلك ، فنقول : لا شك أن واجب الوجود لذاته : موجود . ولا شك أنه مؤثر في وجود غيره . والتقدير في هذا القسم : أنه لا يمتنع صدور الآثار الكثيرة عن المؤثر الواحد . وإذا كان لم يمتنع إسناد جميع موجودات العالم إليه . وعلى هذا التقدير ، فإثبات المؤثر الواحد : معلوم ، وإما إثبات ما عداه : فمشكوك فيه . والتمسك بطاعة المعبود المعلوم، أولى من التمسك بطاعة المعبود المجهول . فثبت : أن العقل يقتضى التوجه إلى عبادة الله تعالى ، والإعراض عن كل ما سواه . وهذا الدليل هو المذكور في الكتاب الإلهي ، حيث قال تعالى : ( ومن الله إلهاً آخر ، لا برهان له به. فإنما حسابه عند ربه (۲) ) والمعنى : أن يدع مع المعبود الأول معلوم الثبوت ، وما سواه غير معلوم. والاقتصار على المعلوم أولى من الذهاب إلى غير المعلوم . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يكون التقدير ، هو أنه يمتنع صدور الآثار الكثيرة عن المؤثر الواحد ، فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير ، يمتنع كون الممكن لذاته ، مصدراً للأثر ، لأن للممكن لذاته ماهية ، تقتضي الإمكان فلو اقتضت تلك الماهية أثراً آخر ، لزم صدور الآثار الكثيرة عن المؤثر الواحد ، وهو محال .
The second proof: We say: The single thing either is not impossible for it to be a source of two effects, or it is impossible. If it is not impossible, then we say: There is no doubt that the Necessary of Existence in itself exists. And there is no doubt that He is effectual in the existence of another. And the presumption in this case is that the emanation of many effects from a single effectual cause is not impossible. And if it is not impossible to attribute all the existents of the world to Him, then on this presumption, the existence of the one effectual cause is known, while the existence of anything else is doubtful. And adhering to obedience of the known object of worship is more appropriate than adhering to obedience of the unknown object of worship. So it is established that reason requires directing oneself to worship God, may He be exalted, and turning away from everything other than Him. And this proof is mentioned in the Divine Book, where He, may He be exalted, said: 'And whoever invokes besides God another god for which he has no proof, his account is with his Lord' (23:117). And the meaning is that one should leave the first object of worship whose existence is known, while everything else is unknown. And confining oneself to the known is more appropriate than going toward the unknown. As for the second case, which is that the presumption is that the emanation of many effects from a single effectual cause is impossible, then we say: On this presumption, it is impossible for a possible in itself to be a source of effect, because the possible in itself has a quiddity that necessitates possibility. So if that quiddity necessitated another effect, it would necessitate the emanation of many effects from a single effectual cause, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents a second, more complex proof structured as a dilemma. The opponent claims that only the One (God) can be an effectual cause. The author examines two possibilities:

1. If it is not impossible for a single thing to be the source of multiple effects, then we already know that God (the Necessary Existent) is an effectual cause, and we are certain of His existence. Since it is not impossible that all effects come from Him, we have no need to posit other effectual causes. In fact, positing other effectual causes is doubtful, while belief in God is certain. Thus reason demands that we worship only the known God and ignore all others. The author cites Quran 23:117 to support this, arguing that the verse condemns worshipping another god without proof, and by analogy, worshipping any other effectual cause is baseless.

2. If, on the other hand, it is impossible for a single thing to produce multiple effects, then a possible existent (like a spirit) cannot be an effectual cause. This is because its quiddity already necessitates possibility (one effect); if it also produced another effect, that would be multiple effects from one agent, which is impossible under this assumption. Thus the dilemma concludes that either way, the claim that spirits are effectual is refuted: either they are unnecessary (since God suffices) or impossible (due to the impossibility of multiple effects from one).

أثرين
آثار كثيرة
مؤثر
معبود
معلوم
مجهول

Checkpoint

A) Because nothing else exists
B) Because God is known while others are doubtful
C) Because multiple effects are impossible
D) Because the Quran says so without reason

Key Takeaway
If multiple effects from one agent are possible, the known God is the only certain object of worship, and reason requires turning away from all else.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument ends: 'Adhering to obedience of the known object of worship is more appropriate than adhering to obedience of the unknown object of worship.' Are there any 'unknown objects of worship' in your life—people, goals, or desires—that you are currently prioritizing over the God you know exists? What would it take to turn from them today?
?
This proof says the known God is sufficient; everything else is doubtful. Yet you live as if the world's pleasures are certain. Are you willing to confess that you have treated doubtful things as if they are more worthy than the Certain? What concrete step can you take this week to reorder your priorities?
?
Sanusi cites Quran 23:117: 'Whoever invokes besides God another god for which he has no proof, his account is with his Lord.' This is not just about idols—it's about anything you rely on as a source of ultimate security or meaning without divine warrant. What in your life functions as a 'god without proof'? Will you repent now?

Second Branch: If Multiple Effects Are Impossible, a Possible Cannot Cause
Sanusi presents the second horn of the dilemma, concluding that a possible being cannot be an effectual cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second branch of the dilemma shows that if multiple effects from one agent are impossible, then no possible existent (like a spirit) can be an effectual cause.

Core Idea
If one agent cannot produce many effects, then a possible's quiddity prevents it from being a cause.

This lesson covers the second horn of the dilemma: assuming that multiple effects from a single agent are impossible. Sanusi argues that in that case, a possible being (such as a spirit) cannot be an effectual cause because its quiddity already constitutes one effect (its own possibility), and producing another would require multiple effects from one, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text for the second branch, noting the logical flow from the impossibility assumption to the conclusion about possibles.
The argument is short but contains a key distinction between the quiddity's own effect (possibility) and additional effects.
2
Understand the reasoning
The impossibility assumption means one agent cannot produce multiple effects. A possible in itself has a quiddity that necessitates possibility; this is one effect. If that same quiddity also produced another effect, that would be multiple effects from one, contradicting the assumption.
3
Apply to the spirit claim
Consider the broader context: the opponent claims spirits are effectual. This argument shows they cannot be, because as possibles, their quiddity already prevents them from causing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أن نقول : إن الشيء الواحد . إما أن لا يمتنع كـونـه مصدراً للأثرين (۱) أو يمتنع . فإن لم يمتنع ذلك ، فنقول : لا شك أن واجب الوجود لذاته : موجود . ولا شك أنه مؤثر في وجود غيره . والتقدير في هذا القسم : أنه لا يمتنع صدور الآثار الكثيرة عن المؤثر الواحد . وإذا كان لم يمتنع إسناد جميع موجودات العالم إليه . وعلى هذا التقدير ، فإثبات المؤثر الواحد : معلوم ، وإما إثبات ما عداه : فمشكوك فيه . والتمسك بطاعة المعبود المعلوم، أولى من التمسك بطاعة المعبود المجهول . فثبت : أن العقل يقتضى التوجه إلى عبادة الله تعالى ، والإعراض عن كل ما سواه . وهذا الدليل هو المذكور في الكتاب الإلهي ، حيث قال تعالى : ( ومن الله إلهاً آخر ، لا برهان له به. فإنما حسابه عند ربه (۲) ) والمعنى : أن يدع مع المعبود الأول معلوم الثبوت ، وما سواه غير معلوم. والاقتصار على المعلوم أولى من الذهاب إلى غير المعلوم . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يكون التقدير ، هو أنه يمتنع صدور الآثار الكثيرة عن المؤثر الواحد ، فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير ، يمتنع كون الممكن لذاته ، مصدراً للأثر ، لأن للممكن لذاته ماهية ، تقتضي الإمكان فلو اقتضت تلك الماهية أثراً آخر ، لزم صدور الآثار الكثيرة عن المؤثر الواحد ، وهو محال .
The second proof: We say: The single thing either is not impossible for it to be a source of two effects, or it is impossible. If it is not impossible, then we say: There is no doubt that the Necessary of Existence in itself exists. And there is no doubt that He is effectual in the existence of another. And the presumption in this case is that the emanation of many effects from a single effectual cause is not impossible. And if it is not impossible to attribute all the existents of the world to Him, then on this presumption, the existence of the one effectual cause is known, while the existence of anything else is doubtful. And adhering to obedience of the known object of worship is more appropriate than adhering to obedience of the unknown object of worship. So it is established that reason requires directing oneself to worship God, may He be exalted, and turning away from everything other than Him. And this proof is mentioned in the Divine Book, where He, may He be exalted, said: 'And whoever invokes besides God another god for which he has no proof, his account is with his Lord' (23:117). And the meaning is that one should leave the first object of worship whose existence is known, while everything else is unknown. And confining oneself to the known is more appropriate than going toward the unknown. As for the second case, which is that the presumption is that the emanation of many effects from a single effectual cause is impossible, then we say: On this presumption, it is impossible for a possible in itself to be a source of effect, because the possible in itself has a quiddity that necessitates possibility. So if that quiddity necessitated another effect, it would necessitate the emanation of many effects from a single effectual cause, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents a second, more complex proof structured as a dilemma. The opponent claims that only the One (God) can be an effectual cause. The author examines two possibilities:

1. If it is not impossible for a single thing to be the source of multiple effects, then we already know that God (the Necessary Existent) is an effectual cause, and we are certain of His existence. Since it is not impossible that all effects come from Him, we have no need to posit other effectual causes. In fact, positing other effectual causes is doubtful, while belief in God is certain. Thus reason demands that we worship only the known God and ignore all others. The author cites Quran 23:117 to support this, arguing that the verse condemns worshipping another god without proof, and by analogy, worshipping any other effectual cause is baseless.

2. If, on the other hand, it is impossible for a single thing to produce multiple effects, then a possible existent (like a spirit) cannot be an effectual cause. This is because its quiddity already necessitates possibility (one effect); if it also produced another effect, that would be multiple effects from one agent, which is impossible under this assumption. Thus the dilemma concludes that either way, the claim that spirits are effectual is refuted: either they are unnecessary (since God suffices) or impossible (due to the impossibility of multiple effects from one).

The Cause of Need for an Effectual Is Possibility
The third proof: cause of need is possibility, argued via elimination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that the cause of a thing's need for an effectual cause is its possibility, and why this cause is one.

Core Idea
Possibility alone creates need; necessity and impossibility need no cause.

This passage presents the third proof against the notion that spirits could be effectual causes. The argument centers on the concept of need: what makes something need an external cause? Through a simple elimination, the author demonstrates that only possibility can be the cause of need. Since possibility is a single concept, the need for an effectual is also a single thing, which has implications for the uniqueness of the effectual cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical flow: the author starts with a claim, then proves it by elimination, and draws a conclusion.
The passage is incomplete; the argument continues beyond the available text.
2
Understand the elimination argument
The author asks: what if we remove possibility from our mental consideration? Only two options remain: the thing is either necessary in itself or impossible in itself. Both of these preclude any need for an external cause.
Necessary beings exist by themselves; impossible beings cannot exist; neither needs a cause.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Since necessity and impossibility do not create need, the only remaining candidate is possibility. Thus the cause of need is possibility itself.
This is a foundational principle in kalam: possibility is the sole reason something requires a cause.
4
Reflect on 'one cause of need'
Because possibility is a single concept, the cause of need is one. This sets up the next stage of the argument (not fully present here) that this one cause must lead to a specific effectual, not just any.
The incomplete sentence asks whether this cause is for a specific effectual or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر، هي الإمكان . والدليل عليه : إنا إذا رفعنا الإمكان عن الوهم ، بقي الوجوب بالذات ، أو الامتناع بالذات ، وكل واحد منهما ، يحيل الحاجة إلى المؤثر. فثبت : أن علة الحاجة ليست إلا الإمكان . والمفهوم من الإمكان أمر واحد ، فعلة الحاجة إلى المؤثر أمر واحد . وذلك الأمر الواحد ، إما أن يكون علة للحاجة إلى مؤثر معين ، أو
The third proof: The cause of need for an effectual cause is possibility. The proof for it is: When we remove possibility from the mind, there remains either necessity in itself or impossibility in itself, and each of them precludes need for an effectual cause. So it is established that the cause of need is nothing other than possibility. And the concept of possibility is one thing, so the cause of need for an effectual cause is one thing. And that one thing either is a cause for need for a specific effectual cause, or...
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage begins the third proof against spirits being effectual causes. The argument is based on the concept of need. The author first establishes that the cause (illa) of something's need for an effectual cause is its possibility (imkan). The proof: if we mentally remove possibility from a thing, only two possibilities remain: either it is necessary in itself (wajib al-wujud bi-dhatih) or impossible in itself (mumtani' al-wujud bi-dhatih). Both of these preclude any need for an external cause: the necessary does not need a cause, and the impossible cannot exist. Therefore, the only thing that creates need is possibility. Since possibility is a single concept (amr wahid), the need for an effectual cause is also a single thing. The argument then begins to consider whether this single cause of need necessitates need for a specific effectual cause or... (the text cuts off). The complete argument would likely conclude that the need cannot be for just any effectual cause but for a specific one, thus further restricting the possibility of multiple effectual causes. The passage is unfinished in the provided pages.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that non-existence or temporality is the cause of need for a cause.
Correction: The argument shows that only possibility creates need; necessity and impossibility do not. Non-existence is not a real attribute, and temporality is a kind of possibility.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The need for an effectual cause is entirely due to a thing's possibility, and because possibility is one concept, the need is also one.

?

The Majority View: Multiple Effective Causes and Spiritual Intermediation
Razi presents the majority's affirmation of many effective causes and the intermediate role of spirits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority's argument for multiple effective causes and the role of spirits as intermediaries between the divine and material worlds.

Core Idea
Spirits are real causes that mediate between Allah and the physical world

Before we examine the full tawheed-based critique, al-Razi first presents the prevailing view that recognizes many causes. This passage outlines how the majority not only accepts physical causes like fire and water but also elevates spirits as intermediate agents. Understanding this view is essential because it sets up the theological problem that the author will eventually resolve.

Guided Path
1
Identify the empirical basis
Read the opening lines where the majority cites observable phenomena: fire burns, the sun illuminates, bread satiates, water quenches. Notice how they argue from everyday experience.
2
Examine the claim about spirits
The passage then extends causality to spirits, stating they manage and dispose bodies. Look for the shift from physical to spiritual causation.
3
Note the sources of proof
The majority uses both philosophical reasoning and prophetic symbols—specifically Quranic verses about angels. Identify these dual sources.
4
Understand the intermediary rank
Spirits are said to be intermediate between the divine world and the material world—not in place but in nobility. Razi explains how they are both affected (lower) and effecting (higher).
This intermediate role is key to their causal status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجمهور الأعظم من أهل العالم. فقد أثبتوا مؤثرات كثيرة . قالوا : وكيف يمكننا إنكار ذلك ، ونرى النار مؤثرة في الإحراق ، والشمس في الإشراق ، والخبز في الشبع ، والماء في الري ؟ ثم قالوا : والأرواح مؤثرة في عالم الأجسام ، ومديرة لها ، ومتصرفة فيها، وأكدوا أقوالهم تارة بوجوه فلسفية ، وأخرى برموز نبوية. ألا ترى : أنه جاء في الكتاب الإلهي في صفات الملائكة : فالمقسمات أمراً ؟ وقال أيضاً : فالمديرات أمراً ) وإذا ثبت هذا ، فقد ظهر أن عالم الأرواح متوسط بين العالم الإلهي ، وبين العالم الجسماني ، لا توسطاً بالحيز والمكان ، بل توسبطاً بالشرف والرتبة . فهي من حيث إنها متأثرة عن العالم الإلهي ، كانت أدون منه ، ومن حيث إنها مؤثرة في عالم الأجسام ، كانت أعلى وأجل منها ، فلا جرم كانت درجة الأرواح متوسطة بين الدرجتين
As for the great majority of the people of the world, they have affirmed many effecters. They said: How can we deny that, while we see fire effecting burning, the sun effecting illumination, bread effecting satiety, and water effecting quenching? Then they said: Spirits are effecting in the world of bodies, managing them, and disposing in them. And they emphasized their statements sometimes with philosophical modes, and other times with prophetic symbols. Do you not see that it came in the divine book regarding the attributes of angels: 'And those who distribute by command'? And He also said: 'And those who manage affairs'? And if this is established, then it appears that the world of spirits is intermediate between the divine world and the bodily world – not an intermediation in space and place, but an intermediation in nobility and rank. Thus, insofar as they are affected by the divine world, they are lower than it, and insofar as they are effective in the world of bodies, they are higher and more exalted than it. So necessarily, the rank of spirits is intermediate between the two ranks.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the great majority of people who affirm the existence of many effective causes (mu'aththirat kathira). They argue from empirical observation: fire causes burning, the sun causes illumination, bread causes satiety, water causes quenching. Furthermore, they assert that spirits (arwah) are effective in the material world, managing and disposing of bodies. They support their position with both philosophical arguments and prophetic symbols, citing Quranic verses describing angels who 'distribute by command' (al-muqassimat amra) and 'manage affairs' (al-mudabbirat amra). From this they conclude that the world of spirits is intermediate between the divine world and the material world – not in physical location but in nobility and rank. Because spirits receive influence from the divine world, they are inferior to it; because they influence the material world, they are superior to it. Hence, spirits occupy an intermediate rank.

Key Terms
جمهور (majority)
مؤثرات (effecters)
نار (fire)
شمس (sun)
خبز (bread)
ماء (water)
أرواح (spirits)
ملائكة (angels)
توسط (intermediation)
شرف (nobility)
رتبة (rank)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many readers assume that because al-Razi presents this view in detail, he endorses it.
Correction: Al-Razi is merely reporting the majority position to later refute it; the lesson is that acknowledging many causes requires careful theological analysis.

Checkpoint
What evidence do the majority use to affirm multiple effective causes?
A) Only philosophical arguments.
B) Only observable phenomena.
C) Only prophetic symbols.
D) Both philosophical arguments and prophetic symbols, along with observable phenomena.

Key Takeaway
The majority view recognizes fire, sun, bread, water, and spirits as real causes, with spirits playing an intermediary role between Allah and the physical world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see fire burning or bread satisfying hunger, do you truly acknowledge these as real causes, or do you slip into thinking they are mere illusions? What would change in your daily life if you fully believed in the causality Allah has placed in creation?
?
The passage says spirits are intermediate in rank – lower than Allah but higher than bodies. If you truly believed that angels and spirits are managing the world around you right now, would you live in greater awareness and reverence? Why do you often forget this unseen reality?
?
You know that Allah is the ultimate cause, but do you live as if secondary causes matter? When you rely on medicine, food, or your own efforts, do you thank Allah but also respect the means He appointed? Where have you been negligent in honoring the means while trusting the Provider?

The Majority View on Multiple Influences
The author presents the majority view affirming multiple influences with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority view that affirms multiple efficient causes and the empirical and prophetic evidence they cite.

Core Idea
The majority see multiple influences in the world, from fire to spirits.

The author begins by presenting the position of the vast majority of thinkers who affirm many determiners. They argue from everyday experience—fire burns, the sun gives light—and extend this to spiritual beings. This lesson examines their reasoning, which the author will later qualify.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the majority view
Carefully read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the confident tone: 'How can we deny that?'
2
Identify the empirical examples
List the four examples given: fire causing burning, sun giving light, bread causing satiety, water quenching thirst. Why do you think they chose these?
These are everyday experiences.
3
Notice the two modes of support
The author mentions that they support their view with philosophical arguments and prophetic symbols. What might be examples of each?
Prophetic symbols could refer to Quranic verses about angels.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: why does the author present this view? Is he endorsing it or setting up a critique? The next lessons will reveal.
This sets the stage for a more nuanced discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجمهور الأعظم من أهل العالم. فقد أثبتوا مؤثرات كثيرة . قالوا : وكيف يمكننا إنكار ذلك ، ونرى النار مؤثرة في الإحراق ، والشمس في الإشراق ، والخبز في الشبع ، والماء في الري ؟ ثم قالوا : والأرواح مؤثرة في عالم الأجسام ، ومديرة لها ، ومتصرفة فيها، وأكدوا أقوالهم تارة بوجوه فلسفية ، وأخرى برموز نبوية. ألا ترى : أنه جاء في الكتاب الإلهي في صفات الملائكة : فالمقسمات أمراً ؟ وقال أيضاً : فالمديرات أمراً ( وإذا ثبت هذا ، فقد ظهر أن عالم الأرواح متوسط بين العالم الإلهي ، وبين العالم الجسماني ، لا توسطاً بالحيز والمكان ، بل توسطاً بالشرف والرتبة . فهي من حيث إنها متأثرة عن العالم الإلهي ، كانت أدون منه ، ومن حيث إنها مؤثرة في عالم الأجسام ، كانت أعلى وأجل منها ، فلا جرم كانت درجة الأرواح متوسطة بين الدرجتين. إذا عرفت هذا فتقول : الروحانيات لها مراتب و درجات : فالمرتبة الأولى : وهي أعلى مرتبة ، وأجلها رتبة : الذين يكونون مستغرقين في نور جلال الله تعالى، استغراقاً تاماً ، بحيث لا يتفرغون مع ذلك الاستغراق ، لتدبير عالم الأجسام ، فطعامهم التوحيد ، وشرابهم التقديس ، وأنسهم التنزيه . استغرقوا في نور جلال الله تعالى ، ولم يتفرغوا إلى شيء سوى الله تعالى . وهؤلاء هم الملائكة المقربون . وقد عبر الكتاب الإلهي عن هذه المرتبة بقوله : ( ومن عنده لا يستكبرون عن عبادته ولا يستحسرون ، يسبحون الليل والنهار ، لا يفترون )
As for the vast majority of the people of the world, they have affirmed many determiners. They said: How can we deny that, while we see fire affecting burning, the sun affecting illumination, bread affecting satiety, and water affecting quenching? Then they said: Spirits are affecting the world of bodies, managing them, and disposing in them. They emphasized their statements sometimes with philosophical arguments and other times with prophetic symbols. Do you not see that it came in the divine book regarding the attributes of the angels: 'And those who distribute by command'? And also said: 'And those who manage by command'? And when this is established, it becomes clear that the world of spirits is an intermediary between the divine world and the corporeal world, not an intermediacy in spatial locus, but an intermediacy in nobility and rank. So they, insofar as they are affected by the divine world, are lower than it, and insofar as they affect the world of bodies, are higher and nobler than them. Therefore, the rank of spirits is intermediate between the two ranks. If you have understood this, then say: Spiritual beings have ranks and degrees. The first rank, which is the highest rank and the most noble in station, are those who are completely immersed in the light of the majesty of Allah Almighty, a complete immersion, such that they do not occupy themselves with that immersion to manage the world of bodies. Their food is tawḥīd, their drink is taqdīs, and their intimacy is tanzīh. They are immersed in the light of the majesty of Allah Almighty, and they do not occupy themselves with anything other than Allah Almighty. These are the near angels (al-malā'ika al-muqarrabūn). And the divine book expressed this rank in His saying: 'And those who are with Him are not arrogant to worship Him nor do they grow weary; they glorify night and day, never flagging.'
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the majority who affirm multiple efficient causes, then responds by establishing the intermediary nature of spirits. The author first quotes the opponents who argue from empirical observation (fire burns, sun gives light) and from spiritual influences (spirits manage bodies). They support their claims with philosophical arguments and prophetic symbols, citing Quranic verses about angels distributing and managing affairs. The author then explains that the world of spirits (ʿālam al-arwāḥ) is intermediary between the divine world and the corporeal world—not spatially, but in rank and nobility. Insofar as spirits are affected by the divine world, they are lower; insofar as they affect the corporeal world, they are higher. Thus, spirits have an intermediate status. The author then describes the first and highest rank: those angels who are completely absorbed in the divine light, so much so that they do not attend to the management of the physical world. Their sustenance is tawḥīd (divine oneness), taqdīs (sanctification), and tanzīh (transcendence). These are the 'near angels' (al-malā'ika al-muqarrabūn). The author cites Quran 21:19-20, which describes how they worship without arrogance or weariness.

The Intermediary Nature of Spirits
The author explains the intermediary role of spirits in rank.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that spirits occupy an intermediate rank between the divine and corporeal worlds, not spatially but in nobility.

Core Idea
Spirits are both affected and affecting, thus intermediate in rank.

The author now elaborates on the nature of spirits, citing Quranic verses about angels who distribute and manage affairs. He clarifies that spirits are not intermediate in space but in honor and rank, being lower than the divine world but higher than the corporeal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses cited
The author quotes two descriptions of angels: 'those who distribute by command' and 'those who manage by command.' Reflect on what these imply about angels' roles.
These verses are from Quran 51:4 and 79:5.
2
Understand the concept of intermediacy
The author explicitly says intermediacy is not in space but in nobility and rank. What does 'nobility' mean here?
It refers to honor and status, not physical location.
3
Identify the two aspects of spirits
Notice the two relations: spirits are affected by the divine world (so lower), and they affect the corporeal world (so higher).
This double relation makes them intermediate.
4
Conclude the intermediate rank
The author concludes that spirits are between the two ranks. Why is this important for the overall argument?

As for the vast majority of the people of the world, they have affirmed many determiners. They said: How can we deny that, while we see fire affecting burning, the sun affecting illumination, bread affecting satiety, and water affecting quenching? Then they said: Spirits are affecting the world of bodies, managing them, and disposing in them. They emphasized their statements sometimes with philosophical arguments and other times with prophetic symbols. The Highest Rank of Angels: Al-Muqarrabun
The author describes the first and highest rank of angels, the muqarrabun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the description of the highest rank of angels—the muqarrabun—who are completely absorbed in divine majesty.

Core Idea
The highest angels are so immersed in Allah's glory that they attend only to tawhid, taqdis, and tanzih.

After establishing the intermediary nature of spirits, the author now details their ranks. The first and highest rank consists of angels who are completely absorbed in the light of divine majesty, to the point that they do not occupy themselves with managing the physical world. Their entire focus is on worship and sanctification.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of the first rank
The author describes the highest rank as those completely immersed in the light of Allah's majesty. Read the Arabic slowly; note the intensity of the language.
2
Identify key characteristics
List the characteristics: total immersion, no concern for managing bodies, their 'food' and 'drink' as tawhid, taqdis, tanzih.
These metaphors indicate spiritual sustenance.
3
Understand the terms tawhid, taqdis, tanzih
Tawhid is declaring Allah's oneness, taqdis is sanctification, tanzih is transcendence. Why are these their sustenance?
These practices fill their entire existence.
4
Read the supporting Quranic verse
The author cites Quran 21:19-20: 'And those who are with Him are not arrogant to worship Him nor do they grow weary; they glorify night and day, never flagging.' How does this support the description?
