Objection and Answer: The Effectiveness of Absorbed Spirits
Al-Razi answers the objection that purely receptive spirits cannot be effective agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi defends the effectiveness of absorbed spirits by showing that reception and emanation can coexist, using analogies from the physical cosmos.

Core Idea
Absorption in God does not prevent spiritual beings from being channels of divine action.

In this passage, Al-Razi addresses a critical objection: if spiritual beings are entirely absorbed in receiving from God, how can they be active agents? He provides a nuanced answer that reconciles passivity and activity. Understanding this argument is key to grasping the Islamic philosophical view of causality in the spiritual realm.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation once to get the flow of the dialogue between the objector and Al-Razi.
Notice that the objector's point is about pure passivity.
2
Identify the two parts of the answer
Al-Razi gives two sub-arguments: first, emanation can occur despite absorption; second, lower intellects receive and then emanate, like the moon.
The second argument uses a detailed analogy.
3
Reflect on the analogies
Consider how the analogies of the sun and moon illustrate that reception and emanation are not mutually exclusive.
This helps connect the physical and metaphysical realms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : فعلى ما تقولونه أنتم ، لم يبق لهذا القسم من الروحانيات ، إلا قبول الوجود عن الحق ، وقبول الأضواء القدسية عن إشراق جلال الحق ، وكل ذلك انفعال وتأثر ، فأين التأثير والفعل ؟ فنقول في الجواب : إنها وإن كانت مستغرقة في تلك التعقلات الإلهية ، والتعقلات القدسية ، إلا أنه لا يبعد أن يفيض عنها : آثار عظيمة ، إما على الأرواح الفلكية ، أو على الأجرام الفلكية (٤) فيضان النور عن الشمس ، والحياة عن (٤) الروح ، وبهذا التقدير تكون فعالة . وأيضاً : فلا يبعد أن يقال : إن تلك العقول ، يكون بعضها أدنى حالاً من البعض ، فيستكمل الناقص منها بالكامل ، كما أن الشمس والقمر ، وإن كانا في العالم الجسماني جوهرين علويين شريفين ، إلا أن القمر ، لما كان أضعف حالا من الشمس لا جرم صار يقبل النور عن الشمس . ثم إنه بعد قبول النور عن الشمس ، يصير فياضاً ، لتلك الأنوار على العالم السفلي ، فلا يبعد أن يكون حال الروحانيات كذلك .
If someone says: According to what you say, nothing remains for this category of spiritual beings except receiving existence from the Truth and receiving the holy lights from the radiance of the majesty of the Truth, and all of that is passivity and impression, so where is influence and action? We say in reply: Even though they are absorbed in those divine intellections and holy intellections, it is not unlikely that great effects emanate from them, either upon the celestial spirits or upon the celestial bodies, (4) like the emanation of light from the sun, and life from the soul, and by this estimation they become effective. And also: it is not unlikely that it be said that some of those intellects are lower in state than others, so the deficient one is perfected by the perfect, just as the sun and the moon, although in the corporeal world they are two noble celestial substances, yet when the moon is weaker in state than the sun, it necessarily receives light from the sun. Then, after receiving light from the sun, it becomes a bestower of those lights upon the lower world. So it is not unlikely that the state of spiritual beings is likewise.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. Al-Razi presents a hypothetical objection: if these spiritual beings are entirely absorbed in receiving from God, they are purely passive—so how can they be active agents? The answer has two parts. First, being absorbed does not prevent emanation of great effects upon celestial spirits or celestial bodies, like light from the sun or life from the soul. Thus, they can be effective. Second, among these intellects, some are lower and receive from higher ones, then in turn emanate to lower levels, analogous to the moon receiving light from the sun and then bestowing it on the lower world. Therefore, spiritual beings can be both receptive and emanative. The footnote (4) includes a variant reading: 'al-falakiyya, fa-fāḍa ʿanhā al-anwār, fayḍān... etc.' from manuscripts, likely an alternative phrasing. The argument uses analogies from the physical cosmos to explain metaphysical causality, a common method in Islamic philosophy and kalām.

Key Terms
انفعال
تأثير
فيضان
أرواح فلكية
أجرام فلكية
نفس كلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spiritual beings absorbed in God are entirely passive.
Correction: Al-Razi demonstrates that they can be channels of divine action, like the sun emanating light or the moon receiving and bestowing light.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Al-Razi use to show that lower intellects can become emanative after receiving?
A) The sun and the moon
B) The heart and the soul
C) The pen and the tablet
D) The king and his subjects

Key Takeaway
Even absorbed spiritual beings can be effective agents through hierarchical emanation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The moon receives light from the sun and then bestows it on the lower world. In your own life, are you merely absorbing spiritual knowledge and experiences, or are you actively passing them on to others who are in darkness? What would it take for you to become a 'bestower'?
?
Al-Razi argues that even absorbed spirits can be effective agents. Reflect on a time when you felt absorbed in your own spiritual state—perhaps in prayer or study. Did you use that state to benefit those around you, or did you keep it to yourself? What held you back?
?
If the lower intellects are perfected by the higher, who in your life is looking to you for spiritual guidance or emanation? Are you fulfilling that role, or are you content to remain a passive recipient? Commit today to one concrete action that allows divine light to flow through you to someone else.

The Tripartite Division of Bodies
Classification of bodies into three types based on causal efficacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi classifies all bodies in the lower world into three hierarchical categories based on their causal efficacy.

Core Idea
Bodies are ranked by whether they affect, are affected, or both.

Razi draws a parallel between the general division of existents into affecting, affected, and intermediate, and the specific bodies of the cosmos. He identifies the highest as the Throne and Footstool, the lowest as the elemental spheres, and the intermediate as the seven heavens with their planets. This lesson establishes the moral and causal hierarchy of the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall classification.
2
Identify the three types
Note the three categories: affecting-only, affected-only, and both.
3
Apply to bodies
See how each category corresponds to specific cosmic bodies (Throne/Footstool, heavens, elements).
4
Reflect on the hierarchy
Understand why the first is noblest and the second is lowest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني : إن من الناس من قال : كما بينا : أن الموجودات على ثلاثة أقسام : المؤثر الذي لا يتأثر ، وهو أشرف الأقسام . ومتأثر لا يؤثر ، وهو أحط الأقسام . والموجود الذي يكون مؤثراً باعتبار ، ومتأثراً باعتبار آخر ، وهو مرتبة متوسطة بين المرتبتين المتقدمتين، فكذلك أجسام العالم [ السفلي) ] منقسمة إلى هذه الأقسام الثلاثة فالقسم الأول : أجسام تؤثر ولا تتأثر عن سائر الأجسام [ وهو طبقتان : العرش والكرسي . والقسم الثاني : الأجسام التي تقبل الأثر عن سائر الأجسام (؟) ] ولا تؤثر في غيرها، وهما الطبقتان السافلتان . فأعلاهما كـرة اللطيف ، وهي كرة النار [ والهواء ] ، وأدونها كرة الكثيف ، وهي كرة الماء والأرض . والقسم الأول أشرف الأقسام . وهذا الثاني أخسها وحصل فيما بين هذين القسمين قسم ثالث ، وهو الذي يقبل الأثر عن الكرتين العاليتين ، ويؤديه إلى الكرتين السافلتين ، وهي السموات السبع التي حصلت الكواكب السبعة فيها . وذلك لأن هذه الكواكب مركوزة في أفلاك التدوير . وهذه التداوير مركوزة في الأفلاك الحاملة ، فإذا وصل فلك التدوير من حضيض الفلك الحامل إلى أوجه ، ثم وصل الكوكب من حضيض فلك التدوير إلى أوجه . فحينئذ يصير الكوكب مماساً للفلك الذي فوقه بنقطة واحدة ، ويستفيد القوى العالية من الفلك الأعلى ، ثم إذا نزل الكوكب من أوج فلك التدوير إلى حضيضه ، ونزل فلك التدوير من أوج الفلك الحامل إلى حضيضه ، فحينئذ يصير الكوكب ملاقياً للفلك الذي تحته بنقطة واحدة ، فتؤدي تلك القوى المأخوذة من الفلك الفوقاني إلى الفلك التحتاني . فأول هذه الحالة ، إنما تظهر في زحل ، حيث يماس الفلك الكوكب بنقطة واحدة . [ وآخرها حيث يماس القمر فلك الأثير بنقطة واحدة (4) ] فثبت : أن هذه الأفلاك السبعة كالأسباب السبعة (٥) بين العرش والكرسي ، وبين عالم العناصر .
The second investigation: Some people said: As we have shown, existents are of three divisions: the affecting which is not affected, and it is the noblest division; the affected which does not affect, and it is the lowest division; and the existent which is affecting in one respect and affected in another respect, and it is an intermediate rank between the two aforementioned ranks. Similarly, the bodies of the [lower] world are divided into these three divisions. So the first division: bodies that affect and are not affected by other bodies [and they are two layers: the Throne and the Footstool]. And the second division: bodies that receive influence from other bodies [?] and do not affect others, and they are the two lower layers: the highest of them is the sphere of the subtle, which is the sphere of fire [and air], and the lowest of them is the sphere of the dense, which is the sphere of water and earth. And the first division is the noblest of the divisions, and this second is the lowest. And between these two divisions there occurs a third division, which receives influence from the two higher spheres and conveys it to the two lower spheres, and they are the seven heavens in which the seven planets are located. This is because these planets are embedded in the epicycles, and these epicycles are embedded in the deferent spheres. When the epicycle reaches from the perigee of the deferent to its apogee, and then the planet reaches from the perigee of the epicycle to its apogee, then the planet becomes tangent to the sphere above it at a single point, and it receives the higher powers from the higher sphere. Then when the planet descends from the apogee of the epicycle to its perigee, and the epicycle descends from the apogee of the deferent to its perigee, then the planet becomes adjacent to the sphere beneath it at a single point, and it conveys those powers taken from the upper sphere to the lower sphere. The beginning of this state appears in Saturn, where the sphere contacts the planet at a single point. [And its end where the moon contacts the sphere of the ether at a single point]. So it is established that these seven spheres are like the seven causes between the Throne and the Footstool and between the world of elements.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the physical world into three types of bodies based on their causal efficacy, applied to both celestial and elemental realms. The author begins by stating that some people (the philosophers) have said that existents are divided into three categories: (1) that which affects and is not affected (the noblest), (2) that which is affected and does not affect (the lowest), and (3) that which affects in one respect and is affected in another (intermediate). This tripartite division is then applied to the bodies of the sublunar world: the highest are the Throne and the Footstool, which affect but are not affected; the lowest are the spheres of fire/air and water/earth, which are affected but do not affect; and between them are the seven heavens with their planets, which act as intermediaries—receiving influence from above and passing it downward. The author then provides a detailed astronomical mechanism: the planets in their epicycles and deferents, through their motions of apogee and perigee, come into contact with the spheres above and below at single points, thereby transferring powers from the upper to the lower spheres. This process begins with Saturn and ends with the Moon. The conclusion is that these seven spheres serve as the seven causes or intermediaries connecting the highest realm (Throne and Footstool) to the elemental world.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think all celestial bodies are self-sufficient and not causally linked.
Correction: The passage shows a clear causal hierarchy where the Throne and Footstool affect the heavens, and the heavens affect the elements.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which bodies constitute the highest division that affect but are not affected?
A) The seven heavens and planets
B) The Throne and Footstool
C) The spheres of fire and air
D) The spheres of water and earth

Key Takeaway
The physical world mirrors the metaphysical division of existents into three causal categories, with the Throne and Footstool at the top, the elements at the bottom, and the seven heavens as intermediaries.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you more often seek to affect others (like the Throne) or passively receive from them (like the elements)? How does this cosmic hierarchy challenge you to reconsider your role as a mere creature before Allah, the only true Mu'aththir?
?
The Throne and Footstool affect but are not affected by other bodies—yet they remain created and utterly dependent on Allah. Is there any area of your life where you pride yourself on being 'unaffected' by others, while forgetting your total dependence on your Creator?
?
The seven heavens serve as intermediaries, receiving from above and passing down. Who in your life is waiting for you to pass on the mercy, knowledge, or help you have received? What is one concrete action you will take this week to become a more faithful conduit?

The Mechanism of Celestial Intermediaries
Detailed astronomical explanation of how the seven heavens transmit influence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the seven planets in their epicycles and deferents serve as intermediaries, transmitting power from the upper spheres to the lower.

Core Idea
Planetary motions at apogee and perigee allow transfer of influence through point tangency.

This lesson delves into the astronomical mechanism by which the seven heavens act as intermediaries. Razi explains how planets in epicycles and deferents, through their motions of apogee and perigee, come into contact with the spheres above and below at single points, thereby transmitting the higher powers downward. This mechanism is essential for understanding how the causal hierarchy operates in the cosmos.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
The planets are embedded in epicycles, which are in deferents.
2
Focus on the motion
The epicycle moves from perigee to apogee of the deferent, and the planet moves within its epicycle.
3
Identify the tangency
At the highest point, the planet touches the sphere above; at the lowest, it touches the sphere below.
4
Trace the transmission
The planet receives powers from above and conveys them below.
5
Note the endpoints
This process begins with Saturn and ends with the Moon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني : إن من الناس من قال : كما بينا : أن الموجودات على ثلاثة أقسام : المؤثر الذي لا يتأثر ، وهو أشرف الأقسام . ومتأثر لا يؤثر ، وهو أحط الأقسام . والموجود الذي يكون مؤثراً باعتبار ، ومتأثراً باعتبار آخر ، وهو مرتبة متوسطة بين المرتبتين المتقدمتين، فكذلك أجسام العالم [ السفلي) ] منقسمة إلى هذه الأقسام الثلاثة فالقسم الأول : أجسام تؤثر ولا تتأثر عن سائر الأجسام [ وهو طبقتان : العرش والكرسي . والقسم الثاني : الأجسام التي تقبل الأثر عن سائر الأجسام (؟) ] ولا تؤثر في غيرها، وهما الطبقتان السافلتان . فأعلاهما كـرة اللطيف ، وهي كرة النار [ والهواء ] ، وأدونها كرة الكثيف ، وهي كرة الماء والأرض . والقسم الأول أشرف الأقسام . وهذا الثاني أخسها وحصل فيما بين هذين القسمين قسم ثالث ، وهو الذي يقبل الأثر عن الكرتين العاليتين ، ويؤديه إلى الكرتين السافلتين ، وهي السموات السبع التي حصلت الكواكب السبعة فيها . وذلك لأن هذه الكواكب مركوزة في أفلاك التدوير . وهذه التداوير مركوزة في الأفلاك الحاملة ، فإذا وصل فلك التدوير من حضيض الفلك الحامل إلى أوجه ، ثم وصل الكوكب من حضيض فلك التدوير إلى أوجه . فحينئذ يصير الكوكب مماساً للفلك الذي فوقه بنقطة واحدة ، ويستفيد القوى العالية من الفلك الأعلى ، ثم إذا نزل الكوكب من أوج فلك التدوير إلى حضيضه ، ونزل فلك التدوير من أوج الفلك الحامل إلى حضيضه ، فحينئذ يصير الكوكب ملاقياً للفلك الذي تحته بنقطة واحدة ، فتؤدي تلك القوى المأخوذة من الفلك الفوقاني إلى الفلك التحتاني . فأول هذه الحالة ، إنما تظهر في زحل ، حيث يماس الفلك الكوكب بنقطة واحدة . Conclusion: The Elemental World's Subjection to the Celestial
Razi concludes that the elemental world is subject to the celestial world based on prior explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Razi concludes the elemental world is entirely subject to the celestial world, based on the preceding investigations.

Core Idea
The elemental world is not independent; it is entirely subordinate to the celestial realm.

In this concluding section, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi summarizes the outcome of the previous arguments. He asserts that the careful reader will now recognize the fundamental relationship between the two worlds: the lower, elemental world is dependent on and governed by the higher, celestial world. While the detailed proofs are found in the earlier investigations, this passage seals the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding fragment
Read the Arabic fragment and its translation. Notice that it is a concise statement invoking prior knowledge.
The text is incomplete but the intention is clear.
2
Identify the key claim
The claim is that the elemental world (عالم العناصر) is تابع (subject/follower) to the celestial world (العالم السماوي). Understand 'tabi'' as indicating causal dependence.
3
Relate to prior investigations
Recall that earlier investigations established the causality and influence of celestial bodies on the sublunar realm. This conclusion is the natural outcome.
Even though the proof is not repeated here, it is assumed from the context.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this conclusion matters for Islamic cosmology: it affirms a hierarchical universe where the heavens govern the earth, aligning with both philosophical and scriptural views.

Classification of Bodies in the Lower World
Classification of bodies into three categories based on causal interaction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of bodies in the lower world and how the seven heavens act as intermediaries between the highest and lowest realms.

Core Idea
The cosmos is a hierarchy of active, passive, and mediating bodies.

In this passage, the author presents a cosmological classification that explains how influence flows from the highest realms to the material world. By dividing bodies into those that affect only, those that are affected only, and those that both affect and are affected, he establishes a chain of causality. The seven heavens play a crucial role as transmitters of power from the Throne and Footstool to the elements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Read the passage and locate the three categories: bodies that affect without being affected, bodies that are affected without affecting, and bodies that both affect and are affected. Notice what examples are given for each.
The first category includes the Throne and Footstool; the second includes the spheres of fire/air and water/earth.
2
Understand the mediating role of the heavens
Focus on the description of the third category. The author explains how planets in epicycles transfer power from higher to lower spheres. Trace the motion described: from perigee to apogee and back.
The seven planets and their spheres act as conduits for power.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification matters for understanding causality in the universe. The seven spheres are described as 'seven causes' linking the highest to the lowest.
This underscores the interconnectedness of all cosmic levels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مرتبة متوسطة بين المرتبتين المتقدمتين، فكذلك أجسام العالم السفلي منقسمة إلى هذه الأقسام الثلاثة
فالقسم الأول : أجسام تؤثر ولا تتأثر عن سائر الأجسام وهو طبقتان : العرش والكرسي . والقسم الثاني : الأجسام التي تقبل الأثر عن سائر الأجسام (؟) ولا تؤثر في غيرها، وهما الطبقتان السافلتان . فأعلاهما كرة اللطيف ، وهي كرة النار والهواء ، وأدونها كرة الكثيف ، وهي كرة الماء والأرض . والقسم الأول أشرف الأقسام . وهذا الثاني أخسها وحصل فيما بين هذين القسمين قسم ثالث ، وهو الذي يقبل الأثر عن الكرتين العاليتين ، ويؤديه إلى الكرتين السافلتين ، وهي السموات السبع التي حصلت الكواكب السبعة فيها . وذلك لأن هذه الكواكب مركوزة في أفلاك التدوير . وهذه التداوير مركوزة في الأفلاك الحاملة ، فإذا وصل فلك التدوير من حضيض الفلك الحامل إلى أوجه ، ثم وصل الكوكب من حضيض فلك التدوير إلى أوجه . فحينئذ يصير الكوكب مماساً للفلك الذي فوقه بنقطة واحدة ، ويستفيد القوى العالية من الفلك الأعلى ، ثم إذا نزل الكوكب من أوج فلك التدوير إلى حضيضه ، ونزل فلك التدوير من أوج الفلك الحامل إلى حضيضه ، فحينئذ يصير الكوكب ملاقياً للفلك الذي تحته بنقطة واحدة ، فتؤدي تلك القوى المأخوذة من الفلك الفوقاني إلى الفلك التحتاني . فأول هذه الحالة ، إنما تظهر في زحل ، حيث يماس الفلك الكوكب بنقطة واحدة . وآخرها حيث يماس القمر فلك الأثير بنقطة واحدة (4) . فثبت : أن هذه الأفلاك السبعة كالأسباب السبعة (5) بين العرش والكرسي ، وبين عالم العناصر .
A middle rank between the two preceding ranks. Likewise, the bodies of the lower world are divided into these three sections: The first section: bodies that affect and are not affected by other bodies, and they are two levels: the Throne and the Footstool. The second section: bodies that receive effect from other bodies (?) and do not affect others, and they are the two lower levels. The highest of them is the sphere of the subtle, which is the sphere of fire and air, and the lowest of them is the sphere of the dense, which is the sphere of water and earth. The first section is the noblest of the sections, and this second is the basest of them. And between these two sections occurs a third section, which is that which receives effect from the two higher spheres and conveys it to the two lower spheres, and they are the seven heavens in which the seven planets are placed. That is because these planets are fixed in epicycles, and these epicycles are fixed in the carrying spheres. When the epicycle reaches from the perigee of the carrying sphere to its apogee, and then the planet reaches from the perigee of its epicycle to its apogee, then the planet becomes tangent to the sphere above it at a single point and acquires the higher powers from the higher sphere. Then when the planet descends from the apogee of its epicycle to its perigee, and the epicycle descends from the apogee of the carrying sphere to its perigee, then the planet comes into contact with the sphere below it at a single point, and it conveys those powers taken from the upper sphere to the lower sphere. The first of these states appears in Saturn, where the sphere touches the planet at a single point. And the last of them is where the moon touches the sphere of the ether at a single point (4). So it is established that these seven spheres are like the seven causes (5) between the Throne and the Footstool, and between the world of the elements.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the bodies of the lower world into three categories based on their ability to affect and be affected. The author first states that bodies are divided into three sections. The first section consists of bodies that affect but are not affected by other bodies, specifically the Throne and the Footstool. The second section consists of bodies that are affected but do not affect others, namely the two lower spheres: the sphere of subtle matter (fire and air) and the sphere of dense matter (water and earth). The author notes that the first section is the noblest and the second is the basest. Between these two extremes lies a third section: the seven heavens containing the seven planets. These planets are intermediaries: they receive power from the higher spheres (the Throne and Footstool) and transmit it to the lower spheres (the elemental world). The author then provides a detailed astronomical explanation of how this transmission occurs through the motion of epicycles and planets. As a planet moves to its apogee, it contacts the higher sphere and acquires power; as it moves to its perigee, it contacts the lower sphere and delivers that power. This process is exemplified by Saturn (the first) and the Moon (the last). The passage concludes that the seven spheres act as seven intermediary causes linking the highest realms (Throne and Footstool) to the elemental world.

العالم السفلي
العرش
الكرسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Planets are independent agents that cause effects on their own.
Correction: The planets are merely intermediaries that receive power from higher spheres and transmit it to lower spheres; they are not independent causes.

Checkpoint
To which category do the Throne and the Footstool belong according to this passage?
A) Bodies that affect and are affected
B) Bodies that affect and are not affected
C) Bodies that are affected and do not affect
D) Bodies that neither affect nor are affected

Key Takeaway
The lower world is organized in a hierarchy where the highest bodies (Throne, Footstool) act on lower ones through the mediation of the seven heavens.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this cosmic hierarchy, the Throne and Footstool are purely active, while the elemental spheres are purely passive. Where do you find yourself in this spectrum—are you primarily acting on others or being acted upon? What would it take to move toward being a more active transmitter of good rather than a passive receiver of circumstances?
?
The seven heavens receive power from above and then convey it downward, touching higher and lower spheres at single points of connection. Think of your own life: at what single points of connection are you receiving from those above you (parents, teachers, Allah's commands) and passing it on to those below you (children, students, society)? Are those points of contact sharp and intentional, or diffuse and weak?
?
The passage describes a precise mechanism for causation mediated by the planets. Many people today believe events happen by chance or random forces. How does this teaching challenge your own assumptions about cause and effect—do you live as if every blessing and trial comes through a chain of ordained intermediaries leading back to the Throne? What would change in your daily gratitude and patience if you truly believed that?

Hierarchy of Spirits from Celestial to Earthly
The author describes the descending hierarchy of spirits from the celestial to the earthly ranks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the descending hierarchy of spirits as described by the author, from the noblest celestial spirits to the lowest plant souls.

Core Idea
Spirits descend in a chain from the noblest celestial to the basest earthly.

In this passage, the author builds on earlier explanations to present the full hierarchy of spirits. He uses striking analogies to show the vast difference between celestial and earthly spirits, and then notes the variation even within the human level. Finally, he places animal and plant souls at the bottom. This lesson unpacks that ordered descent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the overall structure: it begins with the world of spirits descending from noblest to basest, then gives two analogies, then discusses variation within human spirits, and finally lists the last two ranks.
The passage is dense and uses comparative language.
2
Understand the two analogies
The author compares earthly spirits to celestial spirits in two ways: (1) as human bodies are to the bodies of the spheres, and (2) as hearts in human bodies are to the planets that act as hearts of the spheres. This illustrates the immense qualitative gap between lower and higher spirits.
The phrase 'like non-existence relative to existence' emphasizes the near-nothingness of lower spirits.
3
Identify the variation among human spirits
After the analogies, the author notes that among human spirits there is severe variation in nobility and baseness. This counters the idea that all human spirits are equal.
This variation is important for later discussions on human spiritual ranks.
4
List the final ranks in order
The passage concludes by stating that after human spirits come animal souls, and after them plant souls, which is the end of the hierarchy. Confirm the order: celestial spirits → earthly spirits (including human) → animal souls → plant souls.
The hierarchy is strictly descending; nothing comes after plant souls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث : وهو أنك عرفت بالبيان الذي ذكرناه : أن عالم الأرواح ، ابتدأ بالأشرف فالأشرف، منحطاً إلى الأدون فالأدون، حتى بلغت في آخر المراتب إلى الأرواح الأرضية. وهي بالنسبة إلى الأرواح الفلكية، كنسبة أجساد الناس إلى أجساد الأفلاك، وكنسبة القلوب المودعة في هذه الأجساد إلى الكواكب، التي هي جارية مجرى القلوب لتلك الأفلاك، ولما كانت نسبة الأجساد إلى الأجساد، والقلوب إلى القلوب، كالعدم بالنسبة إلى الوجود، فكذلك نسبة الأرواح السفلية إلى الأرواح العلوية. ثم إن الأرواح البشرية فيما بينها تفاوت شديد في الشرف والدناءة . وبعد الأرواح البشرية : النفوس الحيوانية ، وبعدها : النفوس النباتية . وههنا آخر مراتب النفوس والأرواح .
The third investigation: And it is that you have understood from the explanation we mentioned that the world of spirits began with the noblest, then the nobler, descending to the basest, then the baser, until it reached at the end of the ranks to the earthly spirits. They are in relation to the celestial spirits as the relation of the bodies of people to the bodies of the spheres, and as the relation of the hearts deposited in these bodies to the planets, which run in the manner of hearts for those spheres. And since the relation of bodies to bodies, and hearts to hearts, is like non-existence relative to existence, likewise is the relation of the lower spirits to the higher spirits. Then, among human spirits there is severe variation in nobility and baseness. And after human spirits: the animal souls, and after them: the plant souls. And here is the end of the ranks of souls and spirits.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is the third investigation. It states that from the earlier explanation we know that the world of spirits began with the most noble and progressively descended to the least noble, ending with earthly spirits. The author then draws two analogies: (1) earthly spirits are to celestial spirits as human bodies are to the bodies of the spheres, and (2) as the hearts in human bodies are to the planets, which function as hearts of the spheres. The point is that just as bodies and hearts of the lower realm are negligible compared to the celestial realm, so also are lower spirits compared to higher spirits. Then the author notes variation among human spirits in nobility, and after human spirits come animal souls, then plant souls, which represent the lowest end of the hierarchy.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All spirits within the same category (e.g., human spirits) are equal in rank.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that among human spirits there is severe variation in nobility and baseness, so they are not all equal.

?

?
The author says human spirits have severe variation. Considering your own spiritual state, are you closer to the celestial or earthly? What specific step will you take this week to move upward?
?
If the lowest spirits are animal and plant souls—focused only on survival and basic desires—what does that say about a life obsessed with materialism? Have you ever considered that your soul’s rank is determined by your choices? What will you sacrifice today to prove your rank is noble?

Establishing Duration as an Existent and Substance
Al-Razi summarizes his earlier proofs that duration is an existent and a self-subsistent substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi establishes that duration (al-muddah) is an existent and a self-subsistent substance independent of motion.

Core Idea
Duration is a real, self-subsistent substance, not merely the measure of motion.

Al-Razi begins his proof by recalling earlier work where he established that duration is a genuine existent, not reducible to the measure of celestial motion. He asserts that duration is a substance subsisting by itself, independent of motion and its accidents. This sets the foundation for the subsequent syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully along with its literal translation. Notice how al-Razi refers to his previous book 'Time and Space' as the basis for his claims.
This is a summary of previously established proofs.
2
Identify the key claims
Identify three claims: (1) duration is an existent, (2) it is not the measure of celestial motion, (3) it is a substance subsisting by itself.
These are the premises for the later syllogism.
3
Understand the significance
Reflect on why these claims are important: they establish duration as a real entity with independent existence, not dependent on motion or bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع
ذكر الدلائل الدالة على إثبات هذا القسم من الموجودات على سبيل الإجمال
اعلم : أنا قد استقصينا في إثبات هذا المطلوب في أول كتاب « تنزيه الله تعالى » ، ولا بأس بإعادة بعض الدلائل هنا :
الحجة الأولى : إنا أقمنا الدلائل القاهرة في كتاب « الزمان والمكان » ، على أن المدة : موجود من الموجودات . ثم بينا : أنه لا يجوز أن تكون المدة عبارة عن مقدار الحركة الفلكية . وبينا : أن المدة جوهر قائم بذاته غني عن وجود الحركة ولواحقها . ثم إنا نعلم : أن ذلك الجوهر يمتنع أن يكون جسماً ، لأن كل ما كان جسماً فإنه يكون قريباً من جسم، وبعيداً من جسم آخر . وبديهة العقل شاهدة بأن نسبة المدة إلى جميع الأشياء على السوية . ويمتنع أن يقال : إن هذه النسبة قريبة من فلان ، وبعيدة من فلان آخر . وعند هذا ينعقد قياس من الشكل الثاني ، وهو أن كل ما كان مدة ، فإن نسبته إلى جميع الأجسام بالقرب والبعد على السوية ، ولا شيء مما يكون جسماً كذلك . ينتج : فلا شيء من المدة بجسم . فثبت : أن المدة : جوهر مجرد، مغاير للجسم ، وغير حال فيه . وهو مطلوب .
وهذا المطلوب إنما يتم بالبناء على أصلين :
أحدهما : إثبات المدة . والثاني : إثبات أنه ليس بجسم ، ولا حال في الجسم . وقد أحكمنا هذين الأصلين في كتاب « الزمان والمكان ».
Chapter Four: Mention of the proofs indicating the establishment of this category of existents in summary. Know: We have exhausted [the discussion] in establishing this sought [proposition] in the beginning of the book 'Tanzih Allah Ta'ala', and there is no harm in repeating some proofs here: The first proof: We have presented conclusive proofs in the book 'Time and Space' that duration (al-muddah) is an existent among existents. Then we showed that it is not permissible for duration to be an expression of the measure of celestial motion. And we showed that duration is a substance (jawhar) subsisting by itself, independent of the existence of motion and its accidents. Then we know that this substance is impossible to be a body, because everything that is a body is near some body and far from another body. And the intuition of reason testifies that the relation of duration to all things is equal. And it is impossible to say that this relation is near to someone and far from someone else. At this point a syllogism of the second figure is formed: Everything that is duration has its relation to all bodies in nearness and distance equally, and nothing that is a body is like that. The conclusion: No duration is a body. So it is established that duration is an abstract substance (jawhar mujarrad), different from the body, and not subsisting in it. And that is the sought [proposition]. This sought [proposition] is only completed by building upon two principles: One of them: The establishment of duration. The second: The establishment that it is not a body, nor subsisting in the body. And we have perfected these two principles in the book 'Time and Space'.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the existence of an abstract substance, specifically duration (al-muddah). The author introduces the chapter by stating that they will summarize proofs for this category of existents. The proof begins by referring to their earlier work 'Time and Space', where they established that duration is an existent, not merely the measure of celestial motion, but a self-subsistent substance independent of motion. The key argument is that duration cannot be a body because its relation to all bodies is perfectly equal—it is equally near to all and equally far from all—whereas any body has a relative position (near some, far from others). This yields a syllogism of the second figure: All bodies have unequal spatial relations; duration has equal relations; therefore duration is not a body. Hence duration is an abstract substance (jawhar mujarrad), distinct from body and not inhering in it. The author then notes that this conclusion rests on two principles: proving the existence of duration and proving it is neither a body nor subsisting in a body, both of which they claim to have already established in their earlier work. The passage thus demonstrates the method of building upon previously established premises to reach new conclusions.

Key Terms
المدة
جوهر
الزمان والمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi claim about duration in this passage?
A) It is only the measure of celestial motion.
B) It is a self-subsistent substance independent of motion.
C) It is an accident of bodies.
D) It is non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Duration is established as a real, self-subsistent substance, not reducible to motion or its measure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If duration is a real substance independent of motion, then your seconds of stillness are just as real as your seconds of action. What does that mean for how you value quiet moments? Will you now consider them as substance in which you are accountable to Allah?
?
Al-Razi argues that duration exists even when there is no motion. Have you ever experienced a moment of pure stillness and felt that something eternal was present? How could that awareness transform your next five minutes of silence?
?
If time is not just a measure of events but a substance created by Allah, then every moment is a direct creation from Him. Does your daily rush reflect a belief that time 'passes' on its own? What would change if you consciously saw each second as a new gift from Allah, independent of your activity?

The Syllogism that Duration is Not a Body
Al-Razi presents a syllogism of the second figure to prove duration is not a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the syllogism of the second figure that proves duration is not a body, and therefore is an abstract substance.

Core Idea
Duration's equal relation to all bodies proves it cannot be a body, so it must be an abstract substance.

Now that duration is established as a substance, al-Razi uses a syllogism to show it cannot be a body. The key observation is that duration relates equally to all bodies, whereas any body is near some and far from others. This yields a logical conclusion that duration is an abstract substance (jawhar mujarrad).

Guided Path
1
Read the syllogism passage
Read the Arabic from 'ثم إنا نعلم' to the end of the proof. Follow the reasoning carefully.
This is a categorical syllogism of the second figure.
2
Identify the premises
Identify the two premises: (1) Everything that is duration has equal nearness/farness relation to all bodies. (2) No body has such equal relation.
3
Understand the conclusion
From these premises, the conclusion follows: No duration is a body. Therefore, duration is an abstract substance (jawhar mujarrad) distinct from bodies.
The author notes that this rests on two principles: existence of duration and its non-corporeality.

?

?
Al-Razi argues that reason itself testifies to duration's equal relation. But your heart often feels that some times (like a sin you committed) are 'far' and others (like a moment of prayer) are 'near'. Which testimony do you trust more—your reason or your feelings? What would it take to align them?
?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
5 min
Void as an Abstract Substance
Author uses void to prove abstract substance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of void (al-khala') is used to prove the existence of an abstract substance, and why void cannot be considered a body.

Core Idea
Void is not a body because place does not move.

This lesson examines the second proof from the author, which uses the idea of void—an abstract dimension—to argue for the existence of a non-material substance. The proof rests on the definition of body as something that moves between places, while void itself is a place and therefore immobile. Understanding this argument clarifies a key distinction in Islamic metaphysics between material bodies and abstract entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the concise structure—the author presents a proof, defines a term, and draws a conclusion. Let the original wording shape your understanding.
Note that 'void' here is not empty space in the modern sense, but a metaphysical concept.
2
Understand the definition of body
The author defines a body as 'a substance that accepts movement from place to place.' This is crucial—body is defined by its capacity for locomotion, not by extension or solidity.
3
Apply the definition to void
Since void (al-khala') is itself a place (a dimension), it cannot move from one place to another—it is the very container of movement. Therefore, void does not fit the definition of body.
Think: if place moved, it would need another place to move into, leading to infinite regress.
4
Reflect on the infinite regress point
The author argues that if place could move, it would require another place ad infinitum—an impossibility. This strengthens the conclusion that void is not a body.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument in Islamic philosophy.
5
Conclude the argument
Since void is not a body and yet exists (as evidence shows), it must be an abstract substance (jawhar mujarrad). This is the second proof for the existence of non-material entities.
This conclusion supports the broader theological framework of the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن الدليل دل على وجود الخلاء ، وهو البعد المجرد القائم بالنفس . فنقول : هذا البعد ليس بجسم . لأن المراد من الجسم : الجوهر الذي يقبل الحركة من حيز إلى حيز . ونفس الحيز لا يقبل الحركة من حيز إلى حيز ، وإلا لحصل للحيز : حيز آخر إلى غير النهاية . وهو محال ، فثبت : أنه جوهر مجرد.
The second proof: The evidence indicates the existence of void (al-khala'), which is the abstract dimension (al-bu'd al-mujarrad) subsisting by itself. We say: This dimension is not a body. Because the meaning of 'body' is the substance that accepts movement from place to place. And the place itself does not accept movement from place to place, for then the place would have another place ad infinitum. That is impossible. So it is established that it is an abstract substance.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for an abstract substance, this time using the notion of void (al-khala'). The author states that evidence proves the existence of void, defined as an abstract dimension subsisting by itself. The key argument is that this void-dimension cannot be a body because a body is something that moves from one place to another. Place itself does not move; if it did, it would require another place to move into, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore void, being a kind of place, is not a body and must be an abstract substance (jawhar mujarrad). The proof is concise and relies on the definition of body as subject to change of place, whereas place is fixed.

Key Terms
الخلاء
البعد المجرد
جوهر مجرد
حيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Void is exactly like the empty space of modern physics—a three-dimensional vacuum.
Correction: In this metaphysical context, void (al-khala') is an abstract, non-physical dimension that subsists by itself, not a physical empty region. It is not extended in matter and does not change location.

Checkpoint
Why is void (al-khala') not a body according to the author?
A) It has no dimensions.
B) It does not accept movement from place to place.
C) It is composed of atoms.
D) It is created by God.

Key Takeaway
The concept of void proves an abstract substance because void is a non-moving, non-material dimension that cannot fit the definition of a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof argues that place itself cannot move, and that any change in place requires another place, leading to an infinite chain. In your own life, have you ever assumed that everything is changeable, including the very foundations you stand on? How does recognizing that some realities are fixed (like place) affect your trust in unchangeable truths, such as the attributes of Allah? Will you commit today to identifying one unchangeable truth you have been neglecting, and act upon it?
?
The author uses the concept of void—an abstract, non-material reality—to argue for abstract substances. If void is real but not material, what does that imply about your own spiritual existence that is not reducible to the physical? How often do you prioritize your non-material reality (e.g., your soul, intentions) over material concerns? What specific change will you make this week to treat your spiritual substance as more real than the physical world?

Proof of Immaterial Entities by Refuting Circularity
Proof of immaterial entities via refutation of circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument for the existence of immaterial entities (Platonic Forms) by refuting the circular dependence of essence on its attributes.

Core Idea
Essences must exist independently of their attributes, proving immaterial realities.

This passage presents a concise proof for the existence of immaterial Forms, famously upheld by Plato. The argument hinges on a simple yet powerful logical tool: the impossibility of a vicious circle. By showing that an essence cannot depend on its attributes without circularity, the author demonstrates that there must be entities free from physical attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dichotomy presented in the first sentence.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents two possibilities: either an essence's existence depends on its attributes, or it does not. Find these two alternatives in the text.
3
Understand the refutation of the first alternative
The author argues that if essence depended on attributes, a vicious circle would result because attributes themselves depend on the essence. Grasp why this is impossible.
The key logical move: دور (circularity) is impossible.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since circularity is impossible, the essence must exist independently. Therefore, there exist entities that are not dependent on physical attributes – immaterial entities.
The proof establishes وجود موجودات مجردة.
5
Connect to historical context
The author identifies these immaterial entities with the Platonic Forms (المثل المجردة). Consider how this argument fits into the broader tradition of philosophical theology.
Plato is referred to as الإمام أفلاطون.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على قيام هذه الصفات به، أعني: الطول والقصر والشكل والأين، وإما أن لا يكون موقوفاً. والأول باطل. لأن قيام تلك الصفات متوقف على تحقق تلك الماهية. فلو كان تحققها متوقفاً على قيام تلك الصفات بها، لزم الدور. وهو محال. فثبت بهذا البرهان: وجود موجودات مجردة في الأعيان. وهي التي كان يقول بها الإمام أفلاطون، وكان يسميها بالمثل المجردة.
On the existence of these attributes in it, namely: length, shortness, form, and place; or that it is not dependent. The first is false, because the existence of those attributes depends on the realization of that essence. So if its realization depended on the existence of those attributes in it, a vicious circle would result, which is impossible. So by this proof it is established: the existence of immaterial entities in reality. And these are what the imam Plato used to assert, and he used to call them the immaterial Forms.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a proof (برهان) for the existence of immaterial entities, specifically the Platonic Forms. The argument proceeds by presenting a dichotomy: either the existence of an essence (ماهية) is dependent on its attributes (صفات) – here exemplified by length, shortness, form, and place – or it is not dependent. The first alternative is refuted because the attributes themselves depend on the essence: they require the essence to be realized first. If the essence's realization in turn depended on those attributes, a vicious circle (دور) would result, which is impossible. Therefore, the essence must exist independently of its attributes. This independence implies that there are entities that are not bound by physical attributes, i.e., immaterial entities (موجودات مجردة). The author identifies these with the Forms (المثل المجردة) of Plato.

Key Terms
ماهية
صفات
دور
برهان
موجودات مجردة
مثل مجردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies all attributes of essences.
Correction: The argument only denies the dependence of essence on attributes; it does not deny that essences have attributes.

Checkpoint
What logical problem does the author use to refute the claim that an essence's existence depends on its attributes?
A) Infinite regress
B) Circularity (دور)
C) Contradiction
D) Equivocation

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of circular dependence between essence and attributes proves the existence of immaterial essences (Platonic Forms).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body has length, form, and place — all attributes that change over time. If your essence (your true self) depended on these, you would be a different person every moment. Yet you know you are the same you. What does that tell you about the reality of your soul? Will you live today as if the physical is all that matters, or will you acknowledge the immaterial essence that does not fade?
?
The proof shows that without independent immaterial essences, circularity would destroy all existence. Your own actions have an immaterial dimension — intentions, love, faith. Are you investing in these as real, or only in the visible? What would change if you truly treated your soul as more real than your body?
?
If the Platonic Forms exist immaterially, then truth, goodness, and beauty are not just human ideas but realities. Have you been living as if moral truths are relative, or do you acknowledge an absolute standard? How would your decisions today be different if you believed that your soul will encounter these Forms in the next life?

Proof that the Soul Moves Bodies Without Contact
Razi presents a proof that the soul moves bodies without physical contact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical proof that the soul can move any body without physical contact, grounded in the soul's abstract nature and the uniformity of bodies.

Core Idea
If contact is unnecessary for one body, it is unnecessary for all.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a philosophical argument to show that the soul does not need to be in physical contact with a body to move it. He builds on the observation that the soul already moves 'this' body without contact, and then uses a principle of uniformity to extend this to all bodies. This proof is important for understanding the soul's nature as an abstract substance and its causal powers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text carefully, then the translation, to get the flow of the argument. The key phrase is 'والجواب' (the answer), indicating Razi is responding to an objection.
Notice the logical connectors: 'لما كان... لا يتوقف' (since it does not depend), 'وجب أن' (it must be that).
2
Identify the core premise
Focus on why all bodies are equal in accepting motion and the soul's relation is uniform. This is the heart of the proof.
The soul is abstract (مجردة) from corporeality, so its relation to all bodies is the same.
3
Understand the conditional reasoning
Razi uses a conditional: if contact is unnecessary for moving one body, it must be unnecessary for all. Grasp why this follows from uniformity.
He considers both possibilities: if contact is needed for one, it would be needed for all, but we know it's not needed for this body.
4
Examine the response to the love objection
Razi addresses the possible objection that the soul loves this body more. See how he distinguishes between increased action and changed essence.
The soul's essence does not change; only the degree of attachment varies.
5
Appreciate the certainty claimed
Razi ends by stating this proof yields certainty (اليقين). Reflect on the structure of a burhān (proof) in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : إنه لما كان قبول البدن لتصرفات النفس ، لا يتوقف على حصول المماسة بين النفس وبين البدن ، وجب أن يكون الحال كذلك في تحريك سائر الأجسام . لأن جميع الأجسام متساوية في قبول الحركة (١) ونسبة النفس : إلى جميعها على السوية. لأن النفس إذا كانت مجردة عن الحجمية وعلائق الحجمية ، كانت نسبة ذاتها إلى الكل على السوية [ ومتى كانت ذات الفاعل بالنسبة إلى الكل على السوية (۲) ] وذات جميع القوابل بالنسبة إلى ذات الفاعل بالسوية ، وجب أن يكون التأثير بالنسبة إلى الكلى على السوية ، فإذا استغنى الفاعل في الفعل عن مماسة محل الفعل في حق البعض ، وجب أن يكون الحال كذلك [ في حق الكل وإن أفتقر إلى المماسة في حق البعض ، وجب أن يفتقر إليها في حق الكل (٣) ] . وأقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : النفس كالعاشقة لهذا البدن ، إلا أن هذا العشق الشديد يقتضي أن يكون تعلقها بهذا البدن أكثر ، وأن يكون تصرفها في هذا البدن أكثر . فأما أن يتغير مقتضى ذاتها بالنسبة إلى هذه الأجسام . فذاك محال . وهذا برهان يفيد اليقين في هذا المطلوب
The answer: Since the body's acceptance of the soul's actions does not depend on the occurrence of contact between the soul and the body, it must be the same in moving other bodies. Because all bodies are equal in accepting motion (1) and the soul's relation to all of them is uniform. Because if the soul is abstracted from corporeality and the attachments of corporeality, its essence's relation to the whole is uniform [and when the agent's essence is uniform in relation to the whole (2)] and the essences of all recipients are uniform in relation to the agent's essence, then the effect must be uniform in relation to the whole. So if the agent dispenses with contact with the locus of the act in the case of some, it must be the same [in the case of the whole; and if it requires contact in the case of some, it must require it in the case of the whole (3)]. The utmost that can be said is: the soul is like a lover of this body, except that this intense love requires that its attachment to this body be greater, and its actions in this body be greater. But as for its essence's requirement changing in relation to these bodies, that is impossible. And this proof yields certainty in this demand.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (burhān) that the soul can move bodies without physical contact. It begins by noting that the body accepts the soul's actions without contact, so the same must hold for other bodies. The reasoning is that all bodies are equal in accepting motion, and the soul's relation to them is uniform because the soul is abstract (mujarrada) from corporeality and its attachments. Since both the agent (the soul) and the recipients (bodies) are uniform in relation, the effect must be uniform. Therefore, if contact is unnecessary for moving one body, it is unnecessary for all; if necessary for one, it would be necessary for all, but we see the soul moves 'this' body without contact, so it must be able to move all bodies without contact. The objection that the soul loves this body more is addressed: intense attachment only increases action, not the essence's requirement. The proof is declared certain (yaqīn).

Key Terms
النفس
البدن
المماسة
الحركة
الجوهر المجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul must be physically connected to a body to affect it.
Correction: The proof shows that since the soul is abstract and all bodies are equal in potential, contact is not necessary.

Checkpoint
Why must the soul's relation to all bodies be uniform?
A) Because the soul is abstract from corporeality.
B) Because all bodies are equal in accepting motion.
C) Because the soul loves all bodies equally.
D) Because the soul is a lover of this body.

Key Takeaway
The soul's abstract nature and the uniformity of bodies entail that physical contact is not required for the soul to move any body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul moves your body without any physical contact—so what is holding you back from using your limbs in obedience to Allah? Is it the body's desires or a lack of will?
?
Razi says the soul loves this body more, but that love does not change the soul's essential requirement. Are you more attached to your body than to your soul's purpose? What would it take for you to loosen that attachment and let your soul lead?
?
If the soul can act without physical connection, then your excuses about 'not feeling connected' to worship are invalid. What would it mean for you to truly believe that your soul can command your body without needing a physical trigger?

The Point Exists Externally: A Chain of Finite Limits
A concise proof that the point exists externally through a chain of finite limits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that proves the external existence of the point as the limit of a finite line, based on the finitude of bodies and surfaces.

Core Idea
The point is not just a mental construct; it exists as the real limit of a finite line.

This lesson presents a concise proof that the point has external reality. The argument builds a chain from the finitude of the body to the existence of its limits — surface, line, and finally point. By following this reasoning, you will see how kalam uses finitude to affirm the reality of even the smallest spatial elements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of the argument
Notice that this passage refutes an opponent's claim that the point is nonexistent. The proof uses a chain of logical implications based on finitude.
The chain moves from the body to its limits.
2
Follow the chain of limits
The argument proceeds: body is finite -> its limit (surface) exists externally; surface is finite -> its limit (line) exists; line is finite -> its limit (point) exists. Each step is justified by the finitude of the previous.
Finitude necessarily implies a limit, and that limit must exist.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that the point exists externally, not merely in the mind. This refutes the view that the point is a mental construct.
The point is as real as the body itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكلام باطل . لأن الجسم متناهي في الخارج، ونهايته هو السطح ، فوجب أن يكون السطح موجوداً في الخارج. والسطح متناهي الخط ، ونهايته الخط ، والخط موجود في الخارج فنهايته موجودة في الخارج [ فالنقطة موجودة في الخارج ]
So their statement is false. Because the body is finite in externality, and its limit is the surface, so the surface must exist externally. And the surface is finite in line, and its limit is the line, and the line exists externally, so its limit exists externally; thus [the point exists externally].
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. It proves that the point has external existence through a chain of reasoning: the body is finite, so its limit (the surface) exists; the surface is finite, so its limit (the line) exists; the line is finite, so its limit (the point) exists. Each step follows from the finitude of the preceding entity. The conclusion is that the point is not a mere mental construct but has external reality.

Correction: The argument shows the point is the real limit of a finite line, so it exists externally.

Third Proof: Relations Are Indivisible Accidents
Shows that relations like above-ness are accidents that cannot be divided.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why relations like above-ness cannot be divided, proving that division of a body does not divide its accidents.

Core Idea
Relations cannot be split into halves — proving accidents aren't divided by body division.

This passage presents a third rational proof that accidents are not divisible by the division of their locus. The author uses the example of relations such as above-ness and below-ness, which are accidents that cannot be conceptually divided. This demonstrates that division of the body does not entail division of the accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the original Arabic passage and its English translation to understand the argument in its own words.
Notice the clear logical structure: premise, example, conditional, absurdity.
2
Identify the specific relations mentioned
The passage lists above-ness, below-ness, master-ness, and slave-ness as examples of relations. Reflect on why these are accidents.
3
Understand the conditional inference
The proof states: If an accident were divided when its locus is divided, then relations (being accidents) would be divisible. Grasp this implication.
4
Recognize the absurd consequence
The passage asserts that it is absurd to speak of half of above-ness or a third of below-ness. Understand why the intellect rejects this.
Think about whether you can conceive of a half of above-ness.
5
Complete the checkpoint
Test your understanding by answering the multiple-choice question based solely on this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن الإضافات عارضة للأجسام . مثل الفوقية والتحتية والمالكية والمملوكية . فلو انقسم الحال لإنقسام محله ، لزم كون الإضافات منقسمة ، وذلك محال . لأنه يلزم أن يحصل للفوقية والتحتية نصف وثلث وربع . وذلك مما لا يقبله العقل .
The third proof: Relations are accidents of bodies, such as above-ness and below-ness, and being a master and being a slave. So if the accident were to be divided by the division of its locus, relations would necessarily be divisible, and that is impossible. Because it would necessitate that above-ness and below-ness have half, third, and quarter. And that is something the intellect does not accept.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a concise proof using the example of relations (al-idafat). It states that relations like above-ness and below-ness are accidents that inhere in bodies. If the accident were divided when its locus is divided, then these relations would become divisible into parts. But it is obviously absurd to speak of half of above-ness or a third of below-ness. Therefore, the intellect rejects such division, proving that division of the locus does not necessitate division of the accident.

Key Terms

فوقية (above-ness)
تحتية (below-ness)
انقسام (division)
عارض (accident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a body is divided, all its accidents must also be divided into parts.
Correction: Relations like above-ness are accidents that cannot be divided, showing that division of the body does not necessitate division of every accident.

Checkpoint

B) Weight

Key Takeaway

Fourth Proof: The Estimative and Cogitative Powers Are Indivisible
Sanusi presents a reductio argument against the divisibility of the estimative and cogitative powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the estimative and cogitative powers must be indivisible, and how this rebuts the claim that bodily accidents are divisible.

Core Idea
A judgment cannot be split; therefore the power that makes it is also indivisible.

This lesson presents the fourth proof against the divisibility of certain bodily powers. The author argues that the estimative and cogitative powers, though considered bodily by Ibn Sina, cannot be divided because their product—a unitary judgment—resists partition. This argument reinforces the broader kalām position on the indivisibility of certain accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the three alternatives the author presents.
This is a reductio argument: assume division leads to absurdity.
2
Understand the initial assumption
The opponent (Ibn Sina) holds these powers are bodily, implying they must have parts. Grasp this starting point.
The argument works within the opponent's framework.
3
Trace the dilemma
Follow the three disjuncts: each part is either a full estimation, a partial estimation, or not an estimation at all. See why each is impossible.
The key insight is that estimation is a single judgment, like 'this is a friend', which cannot be divided.
4
Connect to the earlier proof
The author notes this same reasoning was used against the rational power. See the pattern.
This shows consistency in the argument.
5
Conclude the lesson
Summarize why the proof succeeds: because the judgment is indivisible, the power that produces it must also be indivisible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن القوة الوهمية والقوة المفكرة : قوى جسمانية ، عند الشيخ ، فيجب أن تحصل للقوة الوهمية والفكرية أجزاء وأبعاض، وذلك محال لأنها لو انقسمت لكان كل واحد من أبعاضها، إما أن يكون وهماً ، وحينئذ يكون الجزء مساوياً للكل ، وهو محال . أو يكون بعض الوهم . لكن الوهم لا معنى له إلا الحكم. يكون هذا صديقاً ، وكون ذاك عدواً . وهذا الحكم لا يقبل القسمة البتة . أو لا يكون وهماً لكل ذلك الشيء ولا لبعضه، وحينئذ يبطل هذا القسم بعين ما أبطلتم مثله في القوة العقلية
The fourth proof: The estimative power and the cogitative power are bodily powers, according to the Shaykh, so they must have parts and fragments, but that is impossible because if they were divided, each one of their parts would either be an estimation—then the part would equal the whole, which is absurd—or it would be part of an estimation. But estimation has no meaning except judgment: 'This is a friend' and 'That is an enemy.' And this judgment absolutely does not admit division. Or it would not be an estimation of the whole thing nor of a part of it, and then this part would be invalidated by the very same argument you used against the rational power.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This proof focuses on two internal faculties: the estimative power (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya) and the cogitative power (al-quwwa al-mufakkira). The opponent (Ibn Sina) holds these are bodily powers, implying they should be divisible. The author argues that if they were divisible, each part would either be a full estimation (making the part equal to the whole, which is impossible) or a partial estimation. But estimation is a unitary judgment (e.g., 'this is a friend') that cannot be split. Alternatively, the part might not be an estimation at all, which would contradict its nature. Thus these powers are indivisible, showing that not all bodily accidents are divisible.

Key Terms
قوة وهمية (estimative power)
قوة مفكرة (cogitative power)
جسمانية (bodily)
حكم (judgment)
قسمة (division)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a power resides in a divisible body, then the power itself must be divisible.
Correction: This proof shows that even if the power is bodily, its product (judgment) is indivisible, so the power itself cannot be divided.

Checkpoint
Why can the estimative power not be divided?
A) Because it is not bodily.
B) Because its judgment is unitary and indivisible.
C) Because it is the same as intellect.
D) Because it has no parts.

Key Takeaway
The estimative power's judgment is a single, indivisible act, proving that the power itself cannot be divided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there any judgments in your life that you treat as divisible—like partially accepting Allah's oneness or partially submitting to His commands? This lesson shows judgment is indivisible. Will you examine your heart for divided loyalties today?
?
If the judgment 'This is a friend' cannot be split, what does that imply about your declaration of faith? Is your shahada a whole commitment, or do you hold back a part? What part are you refusing to give?
?
The argument uses reductio to expose contradiction. Where in your own beliefs might you be holding a contradiction for convenience—perhaps between what you say you believe and how you live? Will you reject that contradiction now, even if it costs you comfort?

Refuting the Premises of Finite Bodily Power
Refutes four premises of the argument that bodily power cannot perform infinite acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the argument that bodily power cannot perform infinite acts by challenging four key premises.

Core Idea
Disparity between enablers does not necessarily imply finitude.

The author now turns to a detailed refutation of the opponent's argument about bodily power. He breaks down their reasoning into four premises and questions each one. The third premise—that disparity between enablers requires finitude—is particularly contested. The author offers an alternative explanation: disparity could be due to slowness without cessation. This lesson walks you through each premise and the author's critical response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the opponent's argument structure and the author's numbered response.
The passage is dense; focus on the four premises.
2
Identify the four premises
The author lists four premises of the opponent. Find each one and note how the author introduces them.
Premises begin with 'First', 'Second', 'Third', 'Fourth'.
3
Focus on the third premise objection
The author concedes disparity but objects that it need not imply finitude. Consider the alternative: slowness without cessation.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Reflect on the fourth premise
The author notes that the premise 'everything less than another is finite' is debated elsewhere. Think about why this premise is not self-evident.
The author references the finitude of dimensions discussion.

Source Text

السؤال الرابع : هب أن النفس تقوى على أفعال غير متناهية ، فلم قلتم : إن القوة الجسمانية لا تقوى على أفعال غير متناهية ؟ قوله : لأن كل متحيز فهو منقسم، والحال في المنقسم منقسم وإذا كان كذلك ، كان مقوى بعض تلك القوة أقل من مقوى كلها ، والأقل من الغير : [؟] من متناه . فمقوي بعض القوة : متناه . فيجب أن يكون مقوي كلها : متناهياً فنقول : هذه الحجة تشتمل على مقدمات : أولها : قوله : ( كل متحيز منقسم ، وهو بناء على نفي الجوهر الفرد . وقد سبق الكلام فيه وثانيها : ( إن الحال في المنقسم : منقسم ، وقد ذكرنا النقوض الثمانية في الحجة الأولى . وثالثها : ( إن مقوي بعض القوة ، يجب أن يكون أقل من مقوي كلها ، فنقول : نسلم أنه لا بد من حصول التفاوت بين مقوي البعض وبين مقوي الكل . لكن لم قلتم : إنه لا يحصل التفاوت إلا بأن ينقطع مقوي البعض قبل انقطاع مقوي الكل ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يظهر هذا التفاوت بسبب أن مقوي البعض ، أبطأ من مقوي الكل وإن بقي كل واحد منهما أبد الآباد ، وعلى هذه الحالة ؟ . وبالجملة : فنحن نسلم أنه لا بد من التفاوت . لكن لا نسلم أنه لا يحصل التفاوت ، إلا بانقطاع أثر البعض . فهم مطالبون بإثبات هذه المقدمة . ورابعها : إن قوله : « كل ما كان أنقص من غيره ، فهو متناه ، فهذه مقدمة كثيرة المباحث والشبهات . وقد استقصينا الكلام فيها في مسألة و تناهي الأبعاد ، في دليل : كون الأبعاد قابلة للزيادة والنقصان .
The Fourth Question: Suppose that the soul can perform infinite acts, why do you say that bodily power cannot perform infinite acts? His statement: Because every extended thing is divisible, and the state of the divisible is divisible, and if that is so, then the enabler of some of that power is less than the enabler of all of it, and the lesser than the other is [?] from finite. So the enabler of some power is finite. So the enabler of all of it must be finite. So we say: This argument contains premises: First: His statement: 'Every extended thing is divisible,' and this is based on the denial of the indivisible atom, and we have already discussed it. Second: 'The state of the divisible is divisible,' and we have mentioned the eight refutations in the first proof. Third: 'The enabler of some power must be less than the enabler of all of it.' We say: We concede that there must be a disparity between the enabler of some and the enabler of all. But why do you say that disparity occurs only by the cessation of the enabler of some before the cessation of the enabler of all? And why is it not permissible that this disparity appears because the enabler of some is slower than the enabler of all, even if each of them lasts forever and ever in this state? In summary: We concede that disparity is necessary, but we do not concede that disparity occurs only by the cessation of the effect of some. They are required to prove this premise. Fourth: His statement: 'Everything that is less than another is finite' — this premise has many discussions and ambiguities, and we have thoroughly discussed it in the issue of the finitude of dimensions, in the proof that dimensions are susceptible to increase and decrease.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponent's argument that bodily power cannot perform infinite acts. The author presents the opponent's argument and then breaks it down into four premises, each of which he critiques. First, the opponent says every extended thing is divisible, which assumes the indivisible atom does not exist—a claim already discussed. Second, the state of the divisible is divisible, which has been refuted in eight ways earlier. Third, the enabler of some power must be less than the enabler of all. The author concedes disparity but objects that disparity need not imply finitude; it could be due to slowness without cessation. Fourth, that which is less than another is finite—this premise is contentious and linked to the finitude of dimensions. The author demands proof for each premise.

Misconception: Disparity between enablers always implies finitude of the lesser.
Correction: The author argues that disparity could be due to slowness, not cessation, so both enablers could persist indefinitely.
