The Celestial Soul and Infinite Motions: An Objection and Response
Presents and critiques the objection that the celestial soul, though bodily, produces infinite motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that the celestial soul (a bodily power) produces infinite motions is answered, and how the same reasoning leads to a further challenge concerning the rational soul.

Core Idea
Bodily power cannot produce infinite acts unless it derives strength from a separate intellect.

The author raises a self-objection: if the celestial soul is a bodily power, how can it produce infinite motions? The answer lies in its connection to the separate intellect. But this answer opens a door for a parallel argument about the rational soul, forcing us to reconsider the boundaries of bodily power.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the author presents the objection: the celestial soul is a bodily power, yet it produces infinite celestial motions. This seems to contradict the claim that bodily powers are finite.
Notice that this objection is raised by the author against his own position.
2
Identify the Sheikh's answer
The Sheikh answers that the celestial soul, though bodily, derives perfection and power from the separate intellect. This explains how a bodily power can produce infinite acts.
3
Analyze the counter-argument
The author then turns the answer against the Sheikh: if the celestial soul can do this, why can't the rational soul also derive power from the Active Intellect and perform infinite acts? This challenges the consistency of the argument.
This counter-argument is not answered in this passage; it concludes the discussion.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this objection and response relate to the broader debate about bodily powers and their ability to produce infinite effects. The passage shows the importance of external intellectual support.
Think about how this might apply to other discussions in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الخامس : وهو الذي أورده الشيخ على نفسه . فقال : و أليس أن النفس الفلكية المباشرة لتحريكات الفلك : قوة جسمانية ، مع أن الحركات الفلكية غير متناهية . فههنا قد صدر عن القوى الجسمانية : أفعال غير متناهية ، وأجاب عنه : بأن النفس الفلكية ، وإن كانت قوة جسمانية ، إلا أنها تستمد الكمال والقوة من العقل المفارق، فلهذا السبب قدرت القوة الجسمانية على أفعال غير متناهية هذا هو الجواب الذي ذكره الشيخ في أكثر كتبه . فيقال له : فإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال أيضاً : النفس الناطقة قوة جسمانية ، ألا أنها تستمد القوة والكمال من العقل الفعال ، فلا جرم قويت النفس الناطقة مع كونها جسمانية ، على أفعال غير متناهية . وههنا آخر الكلام على هذه الطريقة .
The Fifth Question: This is what the Sheikh raised against himself. He said: Is it not the case that the celestial soul that directly moves the sphere is a bodily power, while the celestial motions are infinite? So here infinite acts have issued from bodily powers. And he answered: That the celestial soul, even though it is a bodily power, yet it derives perfection and power from the separate intellect, and for this reason the bodily power is able to perform infinite acts. This is the answer that the Sheikh mentioned in most of his books. So it is said to him: If the matter is so, then why is it not permissible to say also: The rational soul is a bodily power, but it derives power and perfection from the Active Intellect, so necessarily the rational soul, despite being bodily, is able to perform infinite acts? And here ends the discussion on this method.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the Fifth Question, which is a self-objection by the author (the Sheikh). The objection is: The celestial soul, a bodily power, produces infinite motions, contradicting the claim that bodily powers cannot produce infinite acts. The Sheikh's answer is that the celestial soul derives power from the separate intellect. The author then turns this answer against him: If that is the case, why not say the same for the rational soul—it also derives power from the Active Intellect and thus can perform infinite acts despite being bodily. This concludes the discussion on this method.

Independence in Effects Does Not Imply Independence in Essence
Refutes a false inference about independence from substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an effect's independence from its substrate does not entail that the effect's essence is independent of that substrate.

Core Idea
An effect can be independent in its operation while still depending on its substrate for its very existence.

The opponent argues that if effects don't depend on the substrate, then the essences themselves must also be independent. But is that necessarily true? In this passage, the author clarifies that contingency and independence are not the same thing, using the simple example of accidental forms that inhere in their substrates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the rhythm of the argument come through.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find the opponent's statement: 'If the occurrence of these effects does not depend on the state of the substrate, then their essences must not depend.' Notice the assumption being made.
3
Understand the refutation
The author counters with the example of forms and accidents. They need their substrates in essence, yet their effects (judgments) may be independent. Grasp how this breaks the opponent's logic.
Think of heat in fire: heat's effect may not depend on fire's state, but heat itself still inheres in fire.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that independence in necessitating a judgment does not imply independence in essence or existence.
5
Reflect on real examples
Apply the lesson: Can you think of other cases where a dependent thing has independent effects? This solidifies the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ثانياً : « إذا لم يتوقف صدور هذه الآثار عنها ، على اعتبار حال المحل ، وجب أن لا تتوقف ذواتها على اعتبار حال المحل ) .
فنقول : أليس أن الصور والأعراض محتاجة إلى محالها ، وليس احتياجها إلى تلك المحال ، إلا لمجرد ذواتها ، ثم لا يلزم من استقلالها بهذا الحكم استغناؤها في ذواتها عن تلك المحال ؟
فعلمنا : أنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء مستقلاً باقتضاء حكم من الأحكام أن يكون مستغنياً في ذاته عن المحل ، بل نقول : إن جميع الآثار الصادرة عن الأجسام لأجل قوى ، وأعراض (۱) حالة في تلك الأجسام . وليس لتلك الأجسام [ مدخل (۲) ] في اقتضاء تلك الآثار. فعلمنا : أنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء مستقلاً باقتضاء حكم ، كونه غنياً في وجوده عن المحل .
As for his second statement: 'If the occurrence of these effects does not depend on the state of the substrate, then their essences must not depend on the state of the substrate.' We say: Are not the forms and accidents in need of their substrates, and their need for those substrates is only for their own essences, yet it does not follow from their independence in this judgment that they are independent in their essences from those substrates? So we know: it does not follow from a thing being independent in necessitating a judgment that it is independent in its essence from the substrate. Rather we say: All effects emanating from bodies are due to powers and accidents (1) subsisting in those bodies, and those bodies have no [input (2)] in necessitating those effects. So we know: it does not follow from a thing being independent in necessitating a judgment that it is independent in its existence from the substrate.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer format. The opponent's second argument claims that if an effect does not depend on the state of its substrate, then the essence of the effect itself must also be independent of the substrate. The author refutes this by pointing out that forms and accidents inherently need substrates, even if their effects are independent. For example, the effect (e.g., heat) may not depend on the state of the fire, but the heat itself still inheres in the fire. The author concludes that independence in producing a judgment does not imply independence in essence or existence from the substrate.

Key Terms

أعراض

ذوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an effect is independent of its substrate, then the thing causing the effect must also be independent of that substrate.
Correction: An effect can be independent in its operation while its cause (the thing producing it) still depends on the substrate for its own existence, as with accidents inhering in bodies.

Checkpoint

A) Independence in effect always implies independence in essence.
B) Independence in effect does not imply independence in essence.
C) Independence in essence always implies independence in effect.
D) Essence and effect are the same thing.

Key Takeaway
Independence in producing an effect does not imply independence in essence or existence from the substrate.

Can the Soul Be Material Parts or Preserved by God?
Author presents objection, two counter-positions, then refutation using two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers refute the idea that the soul could be composed of special material parts preserved by God through the principles of the uniformity of bodies and the universality of divine effusion.

Core Idea
Divine power cannot arbitrarily preserve one similar body over another without cause.

This passage presents a key theological objection about the nature of the soul: could it be made of special material parts that God preserves from decay? The author responds by first presenting two ways the opponent might argue, then delivers the philosophers' refutation based on two philosophical premises. We'll work through the objection, the two counter-positions, and the decisive answer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial objection
Read the opening objection: why can't man be special parts pervading the body like fire in coal? Note that the objector admits these parts are material and perishable.
The objection is designed to force a conclusion that man is different from these parts.
2
Understand the first counter-position
The first position (المقام الأول) suggests that bodies differ in essence; some bodies (the soul) are living and active by themselves, and when they penetrate the body, the body lives. This is said to be a possibility that must be refuted.
This position posits a special non-elemental body for the soul.
3
Understand the second counter-position
The second position concedes the parts are like other bodies, but God the free agent preserves them until death and then rewards or punishes them.
This position relies on divine power to preserve similar parts.
4
Analyze the philosophers' refutation
The answer refutes both positions using two premises: (1) all bodies are alike in complete essence, and (2) God's effusion is universal, so He cannot specify one similar body over another without a cause.
These premises together block the idea of arbitrary preservation.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the philosophers' argument is effective: if all bodies are alike, special preservation would require an unjustified preference, which is impossible for God.
This argument connects to broader principles of causality and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الإنسان عبارة عن أجزاء مخصوصة سارية في أجزاء هذا البدن ، سريان النار في الفحم وسريان ماء الورد في الورد وسريان دهن السمسم في السمسم ولا يقال : إن تلك الأجزاء لا بد وأن تكون من جنس الأجسام العنصرية إما المفردة وإما المركبة . وعلى كلا التقديرين ، فهي قابلة للانحلال والتفرق والتمزق . وحينئذ يعود الكلام المذكور من أنها غير باقية ، والإنسان باق. فوجب أن يكون الإنسان مغايراً لها ؟ لأنا نقول : لنا ههنا مقامان : الأول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الأجسام مختلفة بالماهية فبعضها هذه الأجسام العنصرية ، بسائطها ومركباتها . وبعضها أجسام مخالفة لها . أما الأجسام التي هي النفس ، فهي حية لذواتها ، فعالة لذواتها . فإذا اتفق أن نفذت تلك الأجسام الشريفة الروحانية النورانية في تضاعيف البدن ، بقى البدن حياً ، فعالاً دراكاً ، وإذا فسد مزاج البدن واستولت الأخلاط الغليظة عليه ، وصار بحيث لا يقبل الآثار عن الجوهر المسمى بالروح ، وانفصل الروح عن البدن ؟ فهذا احتمال لا بد من إبطاله والمقام الثاني : وهو أنا نسلم أن تلك الأجزاء التي هي الإنسان ممائلة لسائر الأجسام في تمام الماهية ، إلا أن الفاعل المختار يبقيها في داخل البدن مصونة عن التغير والانحلال ، من أول وقت الحياة إلى آخر وقت الممات ، ثم إنها عند الموت تنفصل . والفاعل المختار يفعل ما بها ما يشاء . إما بأن يوصل إليها الروح والراحة والريحان ، وإما بأن يعذبها بأنواع العذاب والخسران والجواب : اعلم : أن الفلاسفة يدفعون هذا الاحتمال بناء على مقدمتين : فالمقدمة الأولى : إنهم أقاموا الدلالة على أن جميع الأجسام متماثلة في تمام الماهية . وقد سبق ذكر هذا الدليل والمقدمة الثانية : إنه سبحانه وتعالى عام الفيض ، ويمتنع أن يخص أحد المثلين بخاصية وأثر لا يحصل في الثاني وهذه المقدمة فلسفية محضة إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إن تلك الأجزاء الأصلية البدنية مساوية للأجزاء الفرعية القابلة للانحلال في تمام الماهية . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : نسبة الأحوال الموجبة للانحلال والذوبان إلى جميع الأجزاء الموجودة في هذا البدن على التساوي . ويمتنع أن يقال : إن إله العالم خصص بعض تلك الأجزاء بالإبقاء والصون عن الانحلال والفناء من غير سبب مخصص . لأنا ذكرنا : أن الفيض عام ، وأن الترجيح من غير مرجح محال في العقول وإذا كان الأمر
For if it is said: why may it not be said that man consists of specific parts pervading the parts of this body, like the pervasion of fire in coal and the pervasion of rosewater in roses and the pervasion of sesame oil in sesame seeds, and it is not said that these parts necessarily must be of the genus of elemental bodies, either simple or composite. On both assumptions, they are susceptible to dissolution, dispersion, and tearing apart. Then the aforementioned argument returns that they are not lasting, while man is lasting. So must it be that man is different from them? [We reply:] because we say: we have two positions: The first: why may it not be said that bodies differ in essence, and some of them are these elemental bodies, their simples and composites, and some of them are bodies different from them. As for the bodies which are the soul, they are living by their essence, active by their essence. If it happens that those noble, spiritual, luminous bodies penetrate the folds of the body, the body remains alive, active, perceptive; and if the temperament of the body becomes corrupt and coarse humors prevail over it, and it becomes such that it no longer receives effects from the substance called the spirit, and the spirit separates from the body? This is a possibility that must be refuted. The second position: that we concede that those parts which constitute man are similar to all other bodies in complete essence, except that the free agent keeps them inside the body protected from change and dissolution from the first time of life until the last time of death; then at death they separate. The free agent does with them what He wills: either He brings them spirit, rest, and fragrance, or He torments them with kinds of punishment and loss. The answer: Know that the philosophers refute this possibility based on two premises: The first premise: they have established proof that all bodies are similar in complete essence, and this proof has been mentioned previously. The second premise: that He, glorified and exalted, is universal in effusion, and it is impossible that He should specify one of two similars with a property and effect that does not obtain in the second. And this premise is purely philosophical. If this is established, we say: those original bodily parts are equal to the secondary parts susceptible to dissolution in complete essence. And if this is established, we say: the relation of conditions necessitating dissolution and melting to all parts existing in this body is equal. And it is impossible to say that the God of the world has specified some of those parts with preservation and protection from dissolution and annihilation without a specifying cause. Because we have mentioned: that the effusion is universal, and that preponderance without a preponderator is impossible in intellects. And if the matter is...
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the author's response (with two positions and then a refutation). The objector suggests that man might be special material parts pervading the body, like fire in coal. The objector then argues that even these parts are material and perishable, so man would still be perishable, leading to the conclusion that man must be different from them. The author responds by offering two positions (مقامان) from which the opponent might argue: First, bodies differ in essence; some bodies (the soul) are living and active by themselves, and when they penetrate the body, the body lives. This possibility is then said to be refutable. Second, even if the parts are like other bodies, God the free agent could preserve them until death and then reward or punish. Then the author gives the philosophers' answer: they refute both based on two premises: (1) all bodies are alike in essence, and (2) God's effusion is universal, so He cannot arbitrarily preserve one similar body over another without a cause. Therefore, the parts cannot be specially preserved. The passage ends mid-sentence, continuing the argument.

Key Terms
أجزاء مخصوصة (ajzāʾ makhṣūṣa)
أجسام عنصرية (ajsām ʿunṣuriyya)
فاعل مختار (fāʿil mukhtār)
فيض (fayḍ)
ترجيح بلا مرجح (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think God can preserve any part of the body arbitrarily, just by His power, because He is all-powerful.
Correction: The philosophers argue that divine power operates through wisdom; God cannot arbitrarily favor one similar body over another without a cause, as that would be preponderance without a preponderator, which is rationally impossible.

Checkpoint
Which premise do the philosophers use to argue that God cannot preserve certain bodily parts from dissolution?
A) The soul is non-material and cannot be preserved.
B) All bodies are alike, and divine effusion is universal, preventing arbitrary specification.
C) God only preserves the entire body, not parts.
D) The body is made of a single substance that cannot be divided.

Key Takeaway
The soul cannot be material parts divinely preserved because all bodies are uniform and God's universal effusion prevents arbitrary specification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought that God could do anything without reason, even contradict justice? How does the argument against arbitrary specification (ترجيح بلا مرجح) challenge that view, and what does it require of you to trust God's wisdom in your life?
?
When you reflect on your own soul, do you consider it as a material thing preserved by God's power? This passage argues that such a view leads to irrationality about God's actions. How does this make you re-evaluate the nature of your soul and your accountability to a God who acts with wisdom, not caprice?
?
If God cannot arbitrarily preserve one body over another, what does that imply about His fairness and the necessity of cause in your own life? Are you willing to accept that your trials and blessings come from a process of wisdom, even when you cannot see the reason?

The Eighth Proof: One Cause Cannot Produce Both Perfection and Deficiency
The author presents the eighth proof: the same cause cannot bring about both perfection and deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle that a single cause cannot produce both perfection and deficiency in the same thing proves that the soul is distinct from the body.

Core Idea
One cause cannot make something both perfect and deficient; so soul and body must be different.

The author presents a logical proof for the soul's distinctness from the body. He begins with a self-evident principle: one thing cannot be the cause of both perfection and deficiency for the same thing. Then he shows that intellectual activity perfects the soul but harms the body, proving they are not the same substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the principle
Read the Arabic passage carefully. The first sentence states the key principle: the same thing cannot be a cause of both perfection and deficiency for the same thing.
This principle is presented as self-evident (بديهة العقل).
2
Observe the application to soul and body
The text explains that intense intellectual activity perfects the soul (moves it from potentiality to actuality) while harming the body (causing dryness and weakness). This shows the same cause produces opposite effects on soul and body.
Notice the two outcomes: perfection of soul and deficiency of body.
3
Follow the logical conclusion
If the soul were identical to the body, the same cause would produce perfection and deficiency in the same thing, violating the principle. Therefore, the soul must be distinct from the body.
This is a proof by contradiction (burhān al-khalf).
4
Reflect on the proof's strength
Consider why this argument is compelling: it relies on an intuitive principle and observable effects. Ask yourself if you can think of any counterexample.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة في إثبات هذا المطلوب: إن بديهة العقل قاضية بأن الشيء الواحد بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد يمتنع أن يكون سبباً لكماله، وسبباً لنقصانه. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن المواظبة على الأفكار الكثيرة في العلوم الدقيقة توجب كمال حال النفس. فإن النفس بسبب هذه الأفكار تنتقل من القوة إلى الفعل في الكمالات العقلية، وتصير نورانية مشرقة مضيئة مبتهجة. والمواظبة على الأفكار الكثيرة المذكورة توجب نقصان حال البدن وهلاكه، وذلك لأن عند حصول الاشتغال بتلك الأفكار والاستغراق فيها، يستولي الجفاف على الدماغ، ويضعف حال الهضم فيسوء حال البدن. فثبت: أن المواظبة على الأفكار المذكورة، توجب كمال حال النفس، ونقصان حال البدن. فلو كانت النفس عين البدن أو جزءاً من أجزائه، لزم كون الشيء الواحد بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد، سبباً للكمال والنقصان معاً، وذلك محال. فوجب الجزم بأن النفس هي شيء غير البدن، وغير كل واحد من أجزائه. وهو المطلوب.
The eighth proof for establishing this desired (conclusion): The intuition of the intellect (بديهة العقل) judges that one thing in relation to one thing is impossible to be a cause of its perfection and a cause of its deficiency. And if this is established, then we say: We know by necessity that perseverance in many thoughts in the subtle sciences necessitates perfection of the state of the soul. For the soul, by means of these thoughts, moves from potentiality to actuality in intellectual perfections, and becomes luminous, radiant, bright, and joyful. And perseverance in the aforementioned many thoughts necessitates deficiency of the state of the body and its destruction, because when there is occupation with those thoughts and immersion in them, dryness overcomes the brain, and the state of digestion weakens, so the state of the body worsens. So it is established that perseverance in the mentioned thoughts necessitates perfection of the state of the soul and deficiency of the state of the body. So if the soul were exactly the body or a part of its parts, it would necessarily follow that one thing in relation to one thing would be a cause of perfection and deficiency together, and that is impossible. So it is necessary to conclude that the soul is something other than the body and other than each one of its parts. And that is the desired (conclusion).
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction. It first states the principle that one thing cannot be both the cause of perfection and deficiency for the same thing. Then it applies this to the soul and body: intense intellectual activity perfects the soul but weakens the body. Therefore, the soul cannot be identical with the body or any part of it.

Key Terms
بديهة العقل
كمال
نقصان
قوة
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the same activity affects both soul and body because they are connected, so they might be the same substance.
Correction: The proof shows that because the same activity produces opposite effects (perfection vs. deficiency) on soul and body, they cannot be the same substance; their distinctness is required to avoid contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth proof, what does perseverance in intellectual thoughts do?
A) Perfects both soul and body.
B) Perfects the soul but harms the body.
C) Harms both soul and body.
D) Has no effect.

Key Takeaway
The same activity cannot produce both perfection and deficiency in the same thing; since intellectual activity perfects the soul and harms the body, the soul must be distinct from the body.

The Soul's Essence Determines Inclinations
Argument that differing inclinations stem from soul essence, not body or external factors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that differences in human inclinations are rooted in the essence of the soul, not in bodily temperaments or external influences.

Core Idea
Your soul's essence, not your body or environment, determines your deepest inclinations.

People differ in what they naturally desire or avoid. Some attribute these differences to physical makeup or upbringing. But the author presents a compelling argument that such variations cannot be fully explained by these factors, and must instead be traced back to the soul's own essence. This lesson unpacks that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the carpenter analogy at the beginning.
2
Identify the core argument
The passage makes a positive claim (difference in soul essence) and refutes an objection. Identify the claim and the objection separately.
The objection involves teachers, peers, and environment.
3
Reflect on the counterexamples
The author gives examples of Noah and his son, Abraham and his father. Think about how these examples disprove the objection.
These show that environment does not always determine character.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للثقب . فإن كون هذه الآلات صالحة لهذه الأعمال ، لا يوجب حصول ملكات هذه الأعمال في ذات النجار. فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن اختلاف نفوس الناس في الرغبة والنفرة في بعض الأشياء دون البعض، لا يمكن أن يكون لأجل اختلاف الأمزجة البدنية والأعضاء الآلية فوجب أن يكون ذلك الاختلاف في جواهرها وماهياتها . ولا يمكن أن يقال : إن تلك الرغبة والنفرة ، إنما حصلت لأجل تعليم المعلمين ، وإرشاد الأساتذة ، ومجالسة الأقران ، ومخالطة الأصحاب . وذلك لأنا نشاهد كثيراً : أن الأمر يقع في هذه الأحوال بالضد ، فقد يكون الإنسان بحيث يكون أبواه من أهل الشر والفساد والدعارة، ويتربى فيما بينهم ويخالط تلك الطوائف ، ولا يلقى أحداً من أهل الخير، ثم إنه في أول النشوء يميل إلى الخير والطاعة ، ويظهر منه أعمال عجيبة من أعمال الخير والفهم . وقد يكون بالضد منه . واعتبر ذلك بنوح عليه السلام وولده ، وإبراهيم عليه السلام ووالده . وإذا كان هذا المعنى مشاهداً ، علمنا : أن حصول هذه الأحوال لا تمكن إحالته إلى هذا السبب .
For a hole. Indeed, the fact that these tools are suitable for these works does not necessitate the acquisition of faculties for these works in the essence of the carpenter. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that the difference of people's souls in desire and aversion for some things over others cannot be due to differences in bodily temperaments and instrumental organs, so it must be that the difference is in their essences and quiddities. And it cannot be said that that desire and aversion only occurred due to the teaching of teachers, guidance of masters, sitting with peers, and mixing with companions. This is because we often observe the opposite occurring in these situations. For a person may have parents who are people of evil, corruption, and immorality, and he is raised among them and mixes with those groups, and meets no one of the people of good, yet in the beginning of his development he inclines to good and obedience, and shows amazing works of goodness and understanding. And the opposite may occur. Consider Noah (peace be upon him) and his son, and Abraham (peace be upon him) and his father. And when this meaning is observable, we know that the occurrence of these states cannot be attributed to this cause.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that differences in human inclinations are due to the essence of the soul, not bodily temperament or external factors. It begins with an analogy from carpentry: having suitable tools does not grant the carpenter the skill—similarly, the body's organs being suited for actions does not produce those actions without a soul. The author then states the conclusion that differences in desire and aversion must be in the soul's essence. Next, he refutes the objection that these inclinations come from teachers, peers, and environment by pointing to observable counterexamples: children who grow up among evil people yet incline to good, and vice versa. He cites Noah and his son, and Abraham and his father. Thus, external causes cannot be the determining factor.

الرغبة والنفرة (desire and aversion)
الجواهر (essences)
الماهيات (quiddities)
الأمزجة (temperaments)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That environment and upbringing are the sole determinants of character.
Correction: The passage shows that people can defy their upbringing, proving that the soul's essence, not environment, is the root cause of inclinations.

Children's Moral Differences Prove Soul Essences
Fourth proof: elimination of causes to demonstrate soul essences as source of moral differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observation of innate moral differences in young children proves that souls have distinct essences, and why external and biological explanations are insufficient.

Core Idea
Children's innate moral differences reveal that souls are distinct essences, not products of environment or biology.

This passage presents the fourth proof for the existence and distinctness of soul essences. The author observes that very young children display strikingly different moral characters from their earliest actions. By systematically eliminating alternative explanations—bodily temperament and external influences—he concludes that these differences must originate in the souls themselves. This argument is a classic example of reasoning by elimination (sabr wa taqsim) in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the full argument. Notice the three possible causes listed.
The passage is a continuous argument; read it as a whole.
2
Identify the three proposed causes
The author lists three possible causes for moral differences: soul essences, bodily temperaments, and external factors (teachers/peers). Locate each in the text.
He rejects the second and third, leaving the first as the conclusion.
3
Understand why temperaments are rejected
Focus on the agent-instrument argument. The soul is the agent, the body is an instrument. An instrument does not change the agent. Even if an instrument limits action, that requires long practice, but children show differences from the start.
This refutes the idea that biology determines character.
4
Understand why external factors are rejected
Children's differences are present before significant learning or companionship. Therefore, external causes cannot be the origin.
5
Grasp the conclusion
With two causes eliminated, the remaining cause—soul essences—must be the true source. This is a valid logical deduction.
The conclusion is stated: 'it is due to differences in the essences of souls.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إنا نرى الصبيان الصغار يختلفون في الأخلاق والأفعال فقد يكون الواحد منهم رحيماً حبيباً سخياً مطيعاً ، سلس القياد . ومنهم من يكون شريراً بخيلاً ، نماماً ، شرهاً ، وقحاً ، شكس الأخلاق . والسبب لهذه الأحوال المختلفة : إما جواهر النفوس، وإما اختلاف أمزجة الأبدان . وإما الأسباب الخارجية ، وهي التعلم من المعلمين [ والاستفادة ] من القرناء والشركاء . والقسمان الأخيران باطلان ، فبقي الأول . وهو المطلوب . وإنما قلنا إنه لا يمكن أن يكون ذلك بسبب الأمزجة المختلفة ، وذلك لأن الفاعل هو النفس ، والأعضاء المختلفة البدنية آلات للنفس في الفعل ، والآلة منفعلة قابلة . والمنفعل القابل من حيث هو كذلك ، لا يوجب تغير حال الفاعل . نعم ربما ظن أن الآلة المعينة إذا كانت لا تصلح إلا للعمل الواحد ، فالفاعل الذي لا يجد إلا تلك الآلة ، لا بد وأن يبقى مواظباً على ذلك الفعل وتكرر ذلك الفعل يوجب حصول الملكة الراسخة لذلك الفاعل ، إلا أن هذا الخيال إنما يحصل بعد المواظبة على ذلك الفعل زماناً طويلاً . أما الصبيان فإنهم أول ما يشرعون في الفعل يشرعون على هذه الوجوه المختلفة ، فامتنع أن تكون تلك الأخلاق ، بسبب اختلاف الأمزجة والأبدان . وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يمكن أن يكون ذلك الاختلاف بسبب القرناء والمعلمين ، لأن الصبيان لم توجد في حقهم هذه الأحوال . ولما ظهر فساد هذين القسمين ، بقي القسم الأول وهو أن يكون ذلك الاختلاف ، لأجل اختلاف جواهر النفوس . وهو المطلوب .
The fourth proof: We see young children differing in morals and actions. One may be compassionate, lovable, generous, obedient, easy to lead. Another may be evil, stingy, slanderous, greedy, shameless, bad-tempered. The cause for these different states is either the essences of souls, or differences in bodily temperaments, or external causes—namely learning from teachers and benefiting from peers and associates. The last two are false, so the first remains, and that is what is sought. And we say that it cannot be due to different temperaments because the agent is the soul, and the different bodily organs are instruments for the soul in action, and the instrument is passive and receptive. The passive and receptive as such does not necessitate a change in the state of the agent. Yes, one might imagine that a specific instrument, if it is only suitable for one action, then the agent who finds only that instrument must remain persistent in that action, and repetition of that action necessitates the acquisition of a firmly rooted habit for that agent, but this imagination only occurs after persistence in that action for a long time. As for children, the first time they begin an action, they begin in these different ways, so it is impossible that those morals are due to differences in temperaments and bodies. And we say that the difference cannot be due to peers and teachers because these states are not present in children with regard to them. Thus, when the falsehood of these two categories became apparent, the first category remains—that the difference is due to differences in the essences of souls—and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This is the fourth proof (al-hujja al-rabi'a). It observes that young children naturally differ in morals and actions, enumerating three possible causes: soul essences, bodily temperaments, and external factors (teachers and companions). The author rejects the second and third causes. Temperaments are rejected because the soul is the agent and the body is a passive instrument; an instrument does not change the agent. Even if one argues that limited instruments force repetition and habit, that requires long practice, but children show differences from their earliest actions. External factors are rejected because children's differences are innate, not learned. Thus, differences must stem from soul essences.

Key Terms
الصبيان (children)
الأخلاق (morals)
جواهر النفوس (essences of souls)
الأمزجة (temperaments)
الفاعل (agent)
الآلة (instrument)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Children's moral character is entirely shaped by their environment (teachers, peers) or by their biological temperament.
Correction: The passage argues that children's innate moral differences appear before significant environmental influence and cannot be explained by the passive body; therefore, they must originate from the distinct essences of their souls.

Checkpoint

C) Because children's temperaments change rapidly.
D) Because temperaments only affect physical traits.

Key Takeaway
Children's innate moral differences, observable before significant external influence, demonstrate that souls have distinct essences that cause those differences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Animal Diversity as Evidence for the Soul
Enumeration of diverse animal natures as evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the diversity of non-rational animal natures supports the existence of a soul essence distinct from bodily temperament.

Core Idea
Animal diversity without reason points to soul-based essences.

In this proof, the author observes the remarkable variety of behaviors and dispositions among non-rational animals. Since these animals lack reason, their differences cannot be attributed to intellect or learning. This suggests that their varied natures arise from the essence of their souls, not from mere bodily composition.

Guided Path

Commentary
This is the sixth proof, which is incomplete in this window. It argues from the diversity of non-rational animals. Since animals lack reason, their different natures (tame, predatory, deceitful, social, solitary, etc.) cannot be due to learning or reason, and must be attributed to the essence of their souls. The proof is headed in the same direction: that differences in character and action are rooted in the soul's essence, not in bodily temperament.

D) Only predators and prey.

Key Takeaway
The diversity of non-rational animals, from tame to predatory, cannot be explained by reason or temperament, so it must stem from the essence of their souls.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the variety of animals—from the loyal dog to the cunning fox—do you see them as mere biological machines, or as beings with a soul that gives them a distinct nature? How does your answer affect how you treat them?
?
If animal behavior is evidence of their souls, what does your own behavior—your choices, your habits, your character—reveal about the state of your soul? Are you more like the tame and beneficial cattle, or the harmful lion?
?
The proof in this lesson shows that diversity in non-rational animals points to a soul. In your life, what are the 'non-rational' patterns—the habits or instincts you have not consciously chosen—that nevertheless show something deep about your soul? What would you change if you examined them honestly?

The Soul's Essence as the Source of Moral Differences
Concludes that moral differences stem from soul-essences after refuting environmental causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a proof by elimination to argue that moral differences stem from the essences of souls, not from bodily temperaments or environment.

Core Idea
Moral differences are rooted in the soul's essence, not in body or surroundings.

This passage concludes a larger argument about the origin of moral character. After refuting two common explanations—bodily temperament and social environment—the author affirms that the only remaining cause is a difference in the very essences of souls. The argument relies on the observation that children exhibit varied behaviors from the start, before environmental or bodily factors can take effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the author presents a positive statement about habit formation, then refutes two alternative causes, then states the conclusion.
Focus on the logical flow: the author uses the case of children to disprove temperament and environment.
2
Identify the refuted causes
The author explicitly refutes two explanations: (1) differences in bodily temperaments (amzijah), and (2) influence of companions/teachers (qurana'). Mark where each refutation occurs and the reasoning given.
For temperaments, the key is that children start actions in various ways before habits form. For environment, children lack those influences.
3
Understand the conclusion
After eliminating the two alternatives, only one possibility remains: the difference is due to the essences of souls (jawahir al-nufus). This is the desired conclusion (al-matlab).
This is a classic proof by elimination (burhan al-tamanu'/al-sabr wa al-taqsim).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالفاعل الذي لا يجد إلا تلك الآلة ، لا بد وأن يبقى مواظباً على ذلك الفعل وتكرر ذلك الفعل يوجب حصول الملكة الراسخة لذلك الفاعل ، إلا أن هذا الخيال إنما يحصل بعد المواظبة على ذلك الفعل زماناً طويلاً . أما الصبيان فإنهم أول ما يشرعون في الفعل يشرعون على هذه الوجوه المختلفة ، فامتنع أن تكون تلك الأخلاق ، بسبب اختلاف الأمزجة والأبدان . وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يمكن أن يكون ذلك الاختلاف بسبب القرناء والمعلمين ، لأن الصبيان لم توجد في حقهم هذه الأحوال . ولما ظهر فساد هذين القسمين ، بقي القسم الأول وهو أن يكون ذلك الاختلاف ، لأجل اختلاف جواهر النفوس . وهو المطلوب
The doer who does not find except that instrument must remain constant upon that action, and repetition of that action necessitates the acquisition of a firmly rooted faculty for that doer. However, this imagination only occurs after constancy upon that action for a long time. As for children, when they first begin an action, they begin in these various ways. So it is impossible that those morals are due to the difference of temperaments and bodies. And we only said: it is not possible that that difference is due to companions and teachers because children do not have these states in their case. And when the corruption of these two divisions became apparent, the first division remained, which is that the difference is due to the difference of the essences of souls. And that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage concludes an argument (likely part of a preceding proof) for the view that souls differ in their essences. The author first presents a hypothetical scenario: a doer who only has one instrument must constantly repeat the same action, and repetition produces a firm habit (malakah). But this only happens after long practice. Children, however, when they first start an action, do so in many different ways. This shows that their moral dispositions cannot be caused by differences in bodily temperaments (amzijah) because children's bodies are not yet fully developed with distinct temperaments. Next, the author eliminates another possibility: that differences come from companions or teachers. This is rejected because young children are not yet influenced by such external factors. Having refuted two possible causes—bodily temperament and environment—the only remaining explanation is the first division: that differences in moral character arise from differences in the very essences (jawahir) of the souls. This is the desired conclusion (al-matlab). The argument is a proof by elimination, typical in kalam reasoning.

Correction: The passage argues that children's innate variety proves that the primary cause is the soul's essence, not external or bodily factors.

Fifth Proof: Differences in Cognitive Ability
Presents proof from variation in mental skills across sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how variations in intellectual aptitude across different sciences demonstrate that souls differ in their essences, not merely due to bodily temperaments.

Core Idea
Our varying mental strengths point to a cause deeper than the body.

Why can one person master logic but struggle with grammar, while another finds theology easy but philosophy hard? This familiar observation is the basis of our fifth proof. Sanusi invites us to look beyond bodily explanations and recognize the soul as the true source of these differences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping to analyze. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to the observation, the example, and the conclusion.
2
Identify the key observation
Notice the initial claim: souls differ in many states, and this cannot be explained by temperament alone. Find the specific example given (logic vs. theology, or grammar vs. higher sciences).
3
Follow the reasoning to the soul's primacy
The author argues that since the body is merely an instrument, the variations should be attributed to the soul (the principal cause). See how he uses the analogy of tools and the original principle.
4
Reflect on the conclusion and check your understanding
The final line invokes necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri) to dismiss the idea that temperament alone causes the differences. Confirm that you see why this points to the soul's distinct essence.
Use the checkpoint to test yourself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إنا نرى أن النفوس تختلف في أحوال كثيرة ، ولا يمكن تعليل ذلك الاختلاف ، باختلاف الأمزجة مثل : إن بعض النفوس قد تقوى على تحصيل علم دقيق غامض، وتعجز عن تحصيل علم سهل ، كالنحو والتصريف، بل قد يختلف حال الفهم في مسائل العلم الواحد ، فقد يسهل تعلم الباب الواحد من علم ، ويصعب تعلم الباب الثاني . ومن تأمل في أحوال الناس ، عرف أن الأمر كذلك . والنفس هي الأصل ، والبدن محل تصرفها ، ومنزل عملها . وإحالة هذه الأحوال المختلفة على المبدأ الأصلي ، أولى من إحالتها على اختلاف الآلات والأدوات ، لا سيما والعلم الضروري حاصل بأنه يبعد أن يقال : السبب في كون الإنسان بحيث يفهم المنطق ، أسهل ، والإلهي أصعب : هو وقوع التفاوت في الأمزجة
The fifth proof: We see that souls differ in many states, and it is not possible to explain that difference by the difference of temperaments. For example: some souls are strong in acquiring a subtle, obscure science but incapable of acquiring an easy science like grammar and morphology. Rather, the state of understanding may differ within the very questions of one science, so learning one chapter of a science may be easy, while learning another chapter is difficult. And whoever ponders the states of people knows that the matter is so. And the soul is the origin, the body is the locus of its disposal and the abode of its action. Attributing these various states to the original principle is more appropriate than attributing them to the difference of tools and instruments, especially since necessary knowledge is obtained that it is far-fetched to say: the reason why a person finds logic easier and theology more difficult is the occurrence of variation in temperaments.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof for the claim that souls differ in their essences. The author observes that souls vary in many conditions, and this variation cannot be explained by differences in bodily temperaments. He gives an example: some souls can master a subtle and difficult science (like theology, ilahi) but struggle with an easy one (like grammar and morphology, nahw wa tasrif). Even within a single science, one chapter may be easy to learn while another is hard. This variation in cognitive ability points to a cause beyond the body. The author then asserts that the soul (nafs) is the origin (asl), and the body is merely its instrument and place of operation. Therefore, it is more logical to attribute these variations to the soul itself (the original principle) than to differences in bodily 'tools' (the humors or organs). He adds that it is intuitively known (al-ilm al-daruri) that saying the ease or difficulty of understanding different sciences is due to temperamental differences is far-fetched. The proof relies on the principle that effects of a thing are better attributed to its primary cause than to secondary instruments.

النفوس
الأمزجة
النحو والتصريف
العلم الضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Cognitive differences are purely due to neurological or environmental factors (e.g., brain chemistry or upbringing).
Correction: While these factors play a role, the author argues that the primary cause is the soul's essence, as the body is merely an instrument.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof shows that our varying intellectual strengths are best explained by the soul's distinct essence, not by bodily temperaments.

The Sixth Proof: Behavioral Diversity as Evidence for Essential Differences of Souls
Inductive proof from behavioral diversity in animals and humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the vast behavioral variations in animals and humans serve as an inductive argument for the essential difference of souls.

Core Idea
Behavior reveals the soul's essence: diversity is not accidental but foundational.

This sixth and final proof draws on empirical observation—the striking variety of behaviors across animals and humans, persisting throughout life. The author argues that such deep and lasting differences cannot arise from external factors but must reflect the very essences of souls. By examining this inductive argument, we see how empirical data supports a metaphysical conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation slowly. Notice the extensive list of animal types and behaviors.
The author uses a descriptive, enumerative style.
2
Distinguish the two halves
The argument first covers non-rational animals, then humans. Identify the pattern: each behavioral type is listed.
Note that the human behaviors mirror the animal ones.
3
Grasp the inductive inference
The passage concludes that this variation is required by soul-essences. Understand why induction leads to this.
The brother example is key to showing the lack of external cause.
4
Reflect on the example of brothers
The specific case of lifelong sibling hatred without cause is a crucial piece. It shows the inherent nature, not environment.
This counters the objection that upbringing determines behavior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إنا نرى الاختلاف العظيم في الحيوان الذي ليس بناطق ، فبعضها آنسة نافعة كالأنعام والخيل والبغال والحمير ، وبعضها مفسدة مؤذية كالأسد والنمر والذئب ، وبعضها ذو مكر وخداع كالقرد والثعلب , وبعضها يحب الانفراد كالذئب والأسد , وبعضها يحب الاجتماع كالخيل والبقر والغنم ، وبعضها يجمع الغذاء ويدخره لنفسه كالنمل والنحل ، وبعضها يحصل الغذاء يوماً فيوماً كالحمام , وبعضها يسرق ما لا ينتفع به ويخبئه كالعقعق فإنه يسرق الفصوص والخواتيم والدنانير ويخبئها . وكذلك أحوال الناس مختلفة في هذه الصفات. ففي الناس من يكون كريماً كثير النفع ، وبعضهم يكون مؤذياً والمؤذي قد يقبل الرياضة سريعاً ، وقد لا يقبل إلا قليلا ، وقد يكون بحيث لا يقبل الرياضة لا بالكثير ولا بالقليل ، وقد يكون كثير المكر والخداع ، وقد يكون محباً للخلوة والانفراد ، وقد يكون محباً للمخالطة والاجتماع، ونرى الاختلاف في هذه الأحوال باقياً من أول العمر إلى آخره وقد رأيت كثيراً : الأخوين كانا يتباغضان من غير سبب أصلاً ، ويبقيا على تلك الحالة إلى آخر العمر . واستقراء هذه الأحوال يدل على أن اختلاف الصفات النفسانية قد يكون من مقتضيات جواهر النفوس . وذلك يدل على أنها قد تكون مختلفة بحسب الماهية. فهذا جملة ما نذكره في تقرير هذا المطلوب. والله أعلم
The sixth proof: We see great variation in non-rational animals: some are tame and beneficial like cattle, horses, mules, and donkeys; some are corruptive and harmful like the lion, tiger, and wolf; some are full of cunning and deceit like the monkey and fox; some love solitude like the wolf and lion; some love society like horses, cows, and sheep; some gather food and store it for themselves like ants and bees; some obtain food day by day like pigeons; and some steal what they do not benefit from and hide it like the magpie, for it steals rings, signets, and dinars and hides them. Likewise, the states of people differ in these qualities. Among people there is the generous, very beneficial; some are harmful, and the harmful one may accept discipline quickly, or may accept only a little, or may not accept discipline at all whether much or little; some are full of cunning and deceit; some love seclusion and solitude; some love mixing and society. And we see the difference in these states remaining from the beginning of life to its end. And I have seen many: two brothers would hate each other for absolutely no reason, and they would remain in that state until the end of life. Induction of these states indicates that the difference of psychological qualities may be required by the essences of souls. And that indicates that they may be different according to quiddity. So this is the sum of what we mention in establishing this desired conclusion. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth and final proof for the difference of souls by essence. The author draws an inductive argument from the vast behavioral diversity in non-rational animals and in humans. First, he lists animal types: tame and beneficial (cattle, horses, etc.), harmful predators (lion, tiger, wolf), cunning (monkey, fox), solitary (wolf, lion), social (horses, cows, sheep), hoarders (ants, bees), daily foragers (pigeon), and thieves that steal useless items (magpie). Then he notes that humans similarly exhibit these traits—generosity vs. harmfulness, varying receptivity to discipline, deceitfulness, love of solitude vs. society. These differences persist from the beginning to the end of life. He gives a striking observation: two brothers may hate each other without any cause and remain that way for life. The induction (istiqra') of these cases shows that differences in psychological attributes (sifat nafsaniyya) are required by (muqtadayat) the very essences (jawahir) of souls, not by bodily temperaments. This indicates that souls are essentially different in their quiddity (mahiyya). The passage concludes the entire argument section with a summary statement: 'This is the sum of what we mention in establishing this desired conclusion. And God knows best.'

Induction: Soul Essences Explain Psychological Differences
Concludes that psychological quality differences arise from soul essences using induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how induction (al-istiqra) from observed differences in psychological qualities leads to the conclusion that souls differ in their essences.

Core Idea
Psychological differences are not just external; they stem from the very essence of the soul.

This passage presents a powerful inductive argument. Through examples from animal behavior and human dispositions, the author shows that persistent differences in psychological qualities, such as generosity or enmity, point to a deeper cause: the essence (mahiyya) of the soul itself. By examining these examples, we'll see how induction is used to support a foundational metaphysical claim about the diversity of souls.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the examples and the author's reasoning come through.
Notice the author starts with animal examples, then moves to human examples, then gives a striking case of brothers.
2
Identify the observations
List the examples: the pigeon (gets food day by day), the magpie (steals and hides unnecessary things), human types (generous, harmful, deceitful, solitary, social), and the case of two brothers hating each other without cause.
Observe that the differences persist from the beginning of life to the end.
3
See the inductive leap
The author says 'استقراء هذه الأحوال يدل' — the induction of these states indicates that the differences may be due to the essences of souls. This is the conclusion.
Induction here means generalizing from repeated observations.
4
Reflect on the significance
This implies that souls (jawahir al-nufus) are not all identical in essence; they differ in their very natures (mahiyyat).
This is a key metaphysical claim in the wider discussion of the soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحصل الغذاء يوماً فيوماً كالحمام وبعضها يسرق ما لا ينتفع به ويخبئه كالعقعق فإنه يسرق الفصوص والخواتيم والدنانير ويخبئها. وكذلك أحوال الناس مختلفة في هذه الصفات. ففي الناس من يكون كريماً كثير النفع، وبعضهم يكون مؤذياً والمؤذي قد يقبل الرياضة سريعاً، وقد لا يقبل إلا قليلاً، وقد يكون بحيث لا يقبل الرياضة لا بالكثير ولا بالقليل، وقد يكون كثير المكر والخداع، وقد يكون محباً للخلوة والانفراد، وقد يكون محباً للمخالطة والاجتماع، ونرى الاختلاف في هذه الأحوال باقياً من أول العمر إلى آخره وقد رأيت كثيراً: الأخوين كانا يتباغضان من غير سبب أصلاً، ويبقيا على تلك الحالة إلى آخر العمر. واستقراء هذه الأحوال يدل على أن اختلاف الصفات النفسانية قد يكون من مقتضيات جواهر النفوس. وذلك يدل على أنها قد تكون مختلفة بحسب الماهية. فهذا جملة ما نذكره في تقرير هذا المطلوب. والله أعلم.
Food is obtained day by day, like the pigeon. And some of them steal what is not beneficial and hide it, like the magpie, for it steals gems, rings, and dinars and hides them. Similarly, the states of people differ in these qualities. Among people, there is one who is generous, of much benefit, and some are harmful. The harmful one may accept discipline quickly, or may accept only a little, or may be such that he does not accept discipline at all, much or little. And there may be one who is full of deceit and guile, and one who loves solitude and seclusion, and one who loves mixing and society. We see the difference in these states remaining from the beginning of life to its end. And I have seen many: two brothers who hated each other without any cause at all, and they remained in that state until the end of life. And the induction of these states indicates that the difference in psychological qualities may be due to the requirements of the essences of souls. And that indicates that they may be different according to essence. This is the sum of what we mention in establishing this objective. And God knows best.

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion and an inductive argument. The author presents examples from animals (pigeons and magpies) and from human behavior to show that differences in psychological qualities persist throughout life and are often not caused by external factors. He gives the example of two brothers who hate each other without any cause, remaining so until death. Through induction (استقراء) of these states, he concludes that such differences may be due to the very essences of souls (جواهر النفوس), implying that souls differ in their essences (ماهيات). This is the summary of his argument.

Consider the two brothers who hated each other for no apparent reason and remained so until death. Is there someone in your life toward whom you feel an unexplained resentment or coldness? What would it require of you to act against that feeling, knowing it may come from something deep within you?
?
If your psychological traits—generosity, harshness, love of solitude—are partly shaped by your soul's essence, then who is responsible for that essence? How does this truth change the way you pray, repent, or seek to change your heart?

Classifies causes into essential cause and four external causes.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Soul differences come from both essence and external factors—celestial, terrestrial, and bodily.

In this passage, the author classifies the causes of soul differences into two broad categories: the essential cause (already discussed) and external causes. He then details four types of external causes, ranging from astronomical influences to bodily temperaments. This classification helps us understand that human diversity in qualities and actions has multiple origins.

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the causes that lead to differences in souls. This passage presumes causal connections between celestial and terrestrial conditions and human qualities.

Second Proof: Anger and Desire Require Perception
Author proves that anger and desire require perception, thus their subject is one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof demonstrates that anger and desire presuppose perception, leading to the conclusion that they belong to a single subject.

Core Idea
Anger and desire require perception, proving they belong to one subject.

This lesson presents the second proof from the text. The author defines anger and desire as psychological states oriented toward harmful and agreeable objects. He then argues that intentional action requires awareness, so the faculties of anger, desire, and perception cannot be separate substances.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definitions
Read the definitions of anger and desire as psychological states for repelling harm and seeking benefit. Notice that they are defined in relation to objects.
These definitions are the starting point of the proof.
2
Grasp the necessary condition
The author asserts that repelling and seeking require awareness (shuʿūr) of the object being harmful or agreeable. This is presented as a necessary truth.
3
Apply the condition to irascible faculty
If the irascible faculty had no awareness, it could not intentionally repel harm. This shows that effective anger and desire depend on perception.
4
Draw the conclusion
The proof concludes that the one who gets angry and desires must be the same as the one who perceives. Therefore, perception, anger, and desire are attributes of one essence.
This refutes the idea of separate substances for each faculty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الغضب حالة نفسانية تحدث عند محاولة دفع المنافي ، والشهوة حالة نفسانية تحدث عند طلب الملائم ، ثم من المعلوم بالضرورة [ أن دفع المنافي ، وطلب الملائم مشروط بحصول الشعور بكون ذلك الشيء (١) ] منافياً أو ملائماً . فالقوة الغضبية التي هي قوة دافعة للمنافي إن لم يكن لها شعور، يكون ذلك الشيء منافياً ، امتنع كونها دافعة لذلك المنافي على سبيل الاختيار والقصد . لأن القصد إلى الجلب تارة وإلى الدفع أخرى ، مشروط بالشعور بذلك الشيء . فثبت : أن الذي يغضب لا بد وأن يكون هو يعينه مدركاً . والذي يشتهي لا بد وأن يكون هو يعينه مدركاً . فثبت بهذا البرهان القاطع : أن الإدراك والغضب والشهوة : صفات ثلاثة لذات واحدة ، ويمتنع كونها صفات ثلاثة لذوات ثلاثة [ متباينة (٢) ] .
Indeed, anger is a psychological state that occurs when attempting to repel what is harmful, and desire is a psychological state that occurs when seeking what is agreeable. Then it is known necessarily that repelling the harmful and seeking the agreeable are conditional upon having awareness of that thing being harmful or agreeable. So the irascible faculty, which is a faculty for repelling the harmful, if it does not have awareness that something is harmful, it is impossible for it to repel that harm in a voluntary and intentional manner. Because the intention to attract at one time and to repel at another is conditional upon awareness of that thing. So it is established that the one who gets angry must necessarily be the very same one who perceives. And the one who desires must necessarily be the very same one who perceives. So it is established by this decisive proof that perception, anger, and desire are three attributes of one essence, and it is impossible for them to be three attributes of three distinct essences.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-ḥujjah al-thāniyah). The author begins by defining anger and desire as psychological states that arise in response to harmful and agreeable things respectively. He then asserts a necessary truth: repelling harm and seeking benefit require awareness (shuʿūr) of something being harmful or agreeable. Without such awareness, the irascible faculty cannot act intentionally. Since intentional action requires knowing the object, the faculties of anger, desire, and perception must belong to a single subject. Thus, the proof concludes that perception, anger, and desire are three attributes of one essence, not three separate substances.

Mutual Exclusivity Proves One Substance
Author argues that if faculties were separate substances, engagement in one would not hinder another, but empirical observation shows hindrance, proving they belong to one substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutual exclusivity of conflicting acts proves that thought, anger, and desire are faculties of a single substance, not separate substances.

Core Idea
Conflicting emotions interfere because they belong to one soul.

This lesson presents a powerful argument from everyday experience: the fact that intense desire prevents anger shows that these emotions cannot be independent substances. The author uses a logical proof to demonstrate that thought, anger, and desire are attributes of a single substance, not separate entities. Understanding this proof clarifies the unity of the human soul.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key premise
Recognize the assumption that if two substances are independent, one's action cannot prevent the other's action. This is the logical foundation of the proof.
The author starts with this conditional statement.
2
Apply the premise to human faculties
Consider what would happen if thought, anger, and desire were each separate substances. According to the premise, they would not interfere.
The author then states the conditional conclusion.
3
Observe the contradiction with experience
Note that we do experience interference: intense desire prevents anger. This contradicts the conditional, so the initial assumption must be false.
This leads to the conclusion that they are attributes of one substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إنا إذا فرضنا جوهرين مستقلين ، بكون كل واحد منهما مستقلاً بفعله الخاص به ، امتنع أن يكون اشتغال أحدهما بفعله الخاص به مانعاً للآخر من الاشتغال بفعله الخاص به. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو كان محل الفكر جوهراً ، ومحل الغضب جوهراً ثانياً ، ومحل الشهوة جوهراً ثالثاً أن لا ، وجب يكون اشتغال القوة الغضبية بفعلها مانعاً للقوة الشهوانية [ من الاشتغال ] بفعلها، ولا بالعكس. لكن التالي باطل . فإن اشتغال الإنسان بالشهوة ، وانصبابه إليها يمنعه من الإشتغال بالغضب والانصباب إليه ، فعلمنا : أن هذه الأمور الثلاثة ليست مبادىء مستقلة ، بل هي صفات مختلفة لجوهر واحد ، فلا جرم كان اشتغال ذلك الجوهر بأحد هذه الأفعال مانعاً له عن الاشتغال بالفعل الآخر .
The third proof: If we suppose two independent substances, each being independent in its own action, it is impossible that one's engagement in its own act prevents the other from engaging in its own act. Having established this, we say: If the locus of thought were one substance, the locus of anger a second substance, and the locus of desire a third substance, then it would be necessary that the engagement of the irascible faculty in its act does not prevent the concupiscent faculty from engaging in its act, and vice versa. But the consequent is false. For a person's engagement in desire and his inclination towards it prevents him from engaging in anger and inclining towards it. Thus we know that these three things are not independent principles, but rather different attributes of a single substance. Hence, that substance's engagement in one of these acts prevents it from engaging in another act.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This is the third proof. The author posits that if two substances are independent, the action of one cannot hinder the action of the other. Applying this to the faculties of thought, anger, and desire, if they were separate substances, then being absorbed in desire should not prevent being absorbed in anger. However, experience shows that they do hinder each other. For example, intense desire prevents anger. Therefore, these faculties cannot be independent principles; they must be attributes of a single substance, so that when that substance occupies itself with one act, it is unavailable for another.

Correction: The proof shows that if they were independent, they would not interfere; but interference occurs, so they must belong to one subject.

Key Takeaway
The mutual exclusivity of conflicting acts proves they originate from a single substance.

How does this change your accountability before Allah? Will you now own every outburst as your own, and seek to discipline your single soul rather than blame a part?

Fourth proof: perception triggers both desire and anger
Author argues that perception causes desire and anger, so the subject must be the same.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that perception, desire, and anger must belong to the same substance because perception causally leads to both emotions.

Core Idea
A single perception can cause either desire or anger, so the subject must be the same.

This proof builds on the causal relationship between perception and emotions. If perception causes both desire and anger, then the one who perceives must also be the one who desires and gets angry. The author uses a conditional argument to show that separating these faculties would break the causal chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author presents a hypothetical scenario (if the perceiver were different) and then rejects it based on observed effects.
2
Identify the causal link
The proof claims that perception can be the cause (سبب) of both desire and anger. Observe that the same perception leads to different emotions depending on the object.
This is the key observation that drives the argument.
3
Understand the conditional argument
The author assumes for argument's sake that the perceiver is a different substance from the one that desires or gets angry. Then he deduces that perception would have no effect on those emotions, contradicting experience.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the opposite leads to a contradiction.
4
Conclude the unity of the subject
Since the effect (desire or anger) does follow perception, the original assumption must be false. Therefore, the perceiving substance is identical to the one that desires and gets angry.
This conclusion supports the unity of the soul or self.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إنا إذا أدركنا شيئاً، فقد يكون الإدراك سبباً لحصول [ الشهوة ، وقد يصير سبباً لحصول الغضب . فلو كان الجوهر مدركاً شيئاً مغايراً للجوهر الذي يغضب ، وللجوهر الذي يشتهي ، فحين أدرك صاحب الإدراك ، لم يكن [ شيئاً (۱) ] من هذا الإدراك له أثر ولا خبر عند صاحب الشهوة ، ولا عند صاحب الغضب ، [ فوجب أن لا يترتب على ذلك الإدراك ، لا حصول الشهوة ولا حصول الغضب (۲) ] وحيث حصل هذا التأثير واللزوم، علمنا : أن صاحب الإدراك هو بعينه صاحب الشهوة وصاحب الغضب .
The fourth proof: When we perceive something, perception may be the cause of desire, and it may become the cause of anger. If the substance that perceives were something different from the substance that gets angry and the substance that desires, then when the perceiver perceives, none of this perception would have any effect or news on the owner of desire or the owner of anger. Therefore, it would be necessary that neither desire nor anger result from that perception. But since this effect and connection occur, we know that the owner of perception is the very same as the owner of desire and the owner of anger.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
The fourth proof observes a causal relationship: a single perception can lead to either desire or anger, depending on the object. For example, perceiving a harmful thing may cause anger, while perceiving a beneficial thing may cause desire. If the perceiver were a different substance, then its perception would be isolated and could not affect the other faculties. But since it does affect them, the perceiver, the one who desires, and the one who gets angry must be the same substance.

Correction: The proof shows that perception is causally linked to desire and anger, as we observe that perceiving something can directly trigger these emotions.

Key Takeaway

Galen's First Proof: The Brain as Origin of Nerves
Galen's first argument for brain as seat of perception with two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Galen's first argument that the brain is the seat of perception, including its two premises and the empirical evidence for each.

Core Idea
The brain's direct connection to nerves makes it the seat of perception.

Galen, the renowned physician, argued from anatomy and experiment that the brain—not the heart—is the center of sensation and movement. His first proof establishes the brain as the origin of nerves, which are the instruments of perception. This passage dissects his reasoning into clear premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Notice the structure: Galen's claim, then two premises with evidence.
Pay attention to the logical flow: if A is origin of B, and B is instrument of C, then A is source of C.
2
Identify the two premises
Find where the text states 'As for the first premise' and 'As for the second premise'. Note what each premise claims and the evidence given.
The first premise is anatomical (brain origin of nerves), the second is experimental (nerve tightening shows loss of function).
3
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why Galen uses empirical evidence. How does this differ from purely philosophical arguments? Does the evidence convincingly support the premises?
This approach anticipates modern neuroscience's reliance on observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس في
حكايات شبهات «جالينوس»
على مذهبه. والجواب عنها
اعلم : أنه احتج بوجوه :
الحجة الأولى : على أن معدن الإدراك هو الدماغ
(۱)
قال : إن الدماغ منبت العصب، والعصب آلة للإدراك، وما كان منبتاً
للآلة التي بها الإدراك ، وجب أن يكون معدناً لقوة الإدراك .
وهذه الحجة مبنية على مقدمات ثلاثة :
أما المقدمة الأولى : وهي أن الدماغ منبت للعصب . فالدليل عليه : أن
الأعصاب الكثيرة لا توجد إلا في الدماغ، وأما القلب فلا يحصل فيه إلا عصبة
صغيرة ، وإذا كان كذلك ، وجب أن يكون الدماغ هو المنبت للأعصاب
•
وأما المقدمة الثانية : وهي قولنا : إن الأعصاب هي الآلات للحس والحركة . فالدليل عنه : أنك إذا كشفت عن عصبة وشددتها ، وجدت ما كان أسفل من موضع الشد ، فإنه يبطل عنه الحس والحركة ، وما كان أعلى منه مما يلي جانب الدماغ ، فإنه لا تبطل عنه قوة الحس والحركة الإرادية ، وهذا يدل على أن آلة الحس والحركة الإرادية : هي العصب .
The fifth chapter: Narrations of the doubts of Galen concerning his doctrine, and the response to them. Know that he argued with several proofs. The first proof: that the seat of perception is the brain. (1) He said: Indeed, the brain is the origin of the nerve, and the nerve is the instrument of perception. Whatever is the origin of the instrument by which perception occurs must be the seat of the power of perception. This proof is based on three premises. As for the first premise: that the brain is the origin of the nerve. The evidence for it: that the numerous nerves are not found except in the brain; as for the heart, nothing occurs in it except a small nerve. If that is so, then the brain must be the origin of the nerves. As for the second premise: our statement that the nerves are the instruments for sensation and movement. The evidence for it: that if you expose a nerve and tighten it, you find that what is below the point of tightening loses sensation and movement, and what is above it, towards the side of the brain, does not lose the power of sensation and voluntary movement. This indicates that the instrument of sensation and voluntary movement is the nerve.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents Galen's first proof that the brain is the seat of perception, along with the author's analysis of its premises. It opens by stating that Galen argued with several proofs, and this is the first: the brain is the origin of nerves, nerves are the instrument of perception, so the brain must be the seat (معدن) of perception. The author then notes that this proof rests on three premises, but only discusses the first two in this passage. The first premise (المقدمة الأولى) is that the brain is the origin (منبت) of nerves. The evidence is anatomical: numerous nerves exist only in the brain, while the heart has only a small nerve. Therefore, the brain must be their origin. The second premise (المقدمة الثانية) is that nerves are the instruments (آلات) for sensation (الحس) and movement (الحركة). The evidence is empirical: if you expose a nerve and tighten it, the part below the tightening loses sensation and movement, while the part above (toward the brain) retains them. This proves that nerves are the instruments of these functions. The third premise is not covered in this passage, presumably being discussed later.

الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Galen's argument is purely philosophical without empirical basis.
Correction: The passage shows Galen uses anatomical observation (nerves in brain vs. heart) and experimental evidence (nerve tightening) to support his premises.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Galen's first proof uses anatomical and experimental evidence to show the brain is the origin of nerves, which are the instruments of perception, implying the brain is the seat of perception.

?
The passage presents a proof that is incomplete (missing a premise). How often do you accept conclusions without checking if all the steps are sound? What is one belief you hold that needs re-examination of its foundations?

The Brain as the Source of Sensation and Movement
Presents the third premise and its proof from Galen regarding the brain as the source of sensory and motor nerves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third premise in Galen's argument that the brain is the source of sensory and motor faculties, and the causal proof based on the nerves.

Core Idea
The brain is the source of sensation and movement because all nerves originate from it.

In this passage, the author presents the third premise of Galen's argument for the brain as the center of sensation and voluntary movement. The reasoning is causal: since nerves carrying sensory and motor powers all originate in the brain, the brain must be the repository of those powers. This concise proof grounds Galen's medical-anatomical doctrine.
