Third Objection: The Brain's Net-Like Tissue as Origin
Razi presents a conditional objection against Galen's theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third objection against Galen's theory of nerve origin and the reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
Galen's own logic leads to the brain's net-like tissue, not the heart, as the origin of nerves.

In this objection, Razi challenges Galen's conclusion by turning his own logic against him. He argues that if Galen were correct about certain premises, the conclusion would actually be that the brain's net-like tissue is the origin, not the heart. This objection highlights how Galen's reasoning could be used to support alternative views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure.
The passage begins with 'و ثالثها : لو صح ما ذكره جالينوس' – a conditional statement.
2
Identify the conditional and its consequence
Find the 'if' part and the 'then' part. What is the premise and what is the conclusion?
The conclusion is that the origin is the net-like tissue in the brain.
3
Understand the anatomical reasoning
Why does Razi think the net-like tissue should be the origin? Look at the reasons given: countless veins and resemblance to tree branches.
This reasoning is based on the structure and abundance of vessels.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider how this objection uses Galen's own premises to reach a contradictory conclusion. Why might this be a powerful refutation?
This is a classic 'reductio ad absurdum' argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها : لو صح ما ذكره جالينوس لوجب أن يقال : إن أصل العروق الضوارب هي النسيجة الشبيهة بالشبكة التي في الدماغ ، لا القلب لان في تلك النسيجة من العروق والضوارب ، عدد لا يحصى . وهي في غاية المشابهة لعروق الشجرة . واعلم أن هذه النقوض التي ذكروها على جالينوس : وجوه متكلفة . وله جالينوس أن يجيب عنها . ولولا ظهورها لأوردناها .
And the third (objection): If what Galen mentioned were correct, it would be necessary to say that the origin of the pulsating veins is the net-like tissue in the brain, not the heart, because in that tissue there are countless veins and pulsating vessels, and it is extremely similar to the branches of a tree. And know that these refutations that they mentioned against Galen are contrived aspects. And Galen can answer them. And if it were not for their obviousness, we would have mentioned them.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against Galen's theory regarding the origin of nerves. The author argues that if Galen's premise is accepted, it would instead lead to the conclusion that the origin of the pulsating veins is the net-like tissue in the brain, not the heart, because that tissue has many veins and resembles tree branches. The author then remarks that these objections against Galen are forced (mutakallifa) and that Galen could potentially answer them, but because they are not fully developed, the author omits them.

Proof that the Heart is the Source of Nerves
Razi argues that nerves originate from arteries, making the heart their source.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the heart is the source of nerves, based on the shared genus of arteries and nerves and the transformation of arteries into nerves in the brain.

Core Idea
Arteries and nerves share a genus; nerves are transformed arteries.

This passage presents a detailed philosophical argument for the heart as the source of nerves. The argument relies on two premises: first, that arteries and nerves belong to the same genus, and second, that because of this, nerves can be generated from arteries. The author then explains how the transformation occurs, concluding that the heart is the ultimate source.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two premises
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the two premises the author uses to build the argument.
The first premise is about the genus of arteries and nerves; the second is about the possibility of generation.
2
Examine the evidence for the first premise
Look at the proof given for the first premise: the physical similarities and the test of tying a nerve to show it is insensitive in itself.
This establishes that arteries and nerves share essential properties.
3
Understand the second premise and the transformation process
Read how arteries, when they branch and enter the brain, change form and become nerves. Note the explanation about their changing function.
The transformation is described as a physical change due to branching and connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني : سلمنا أن الكثرة والقوة لا يحصلان إلا عند المبدأ . إلا أنا نقول : لا نزاع أن القلب منبت الشرايين. ثم إن اجرام الشرايين من جنس أجرام الأعصاب . وإذا كان كذلك ، أمكن أن تكون الأعصاب متولدة من تلك الشرايين. وبهذا التقدير يكون القلب منبتا ومنشأ للعصب . فيفتقر في تقرير هذا الكلام إلى إثبات مقدمات : المقدمة الأولى : إن العروق من جنس الأعصاب . فالدليل عليه : أن أجرام العروق تتفشى وتنقسم إلى الشظايا الليفية التي لا حس لها ، وهي بيض لدنة ، عديمة الدم ، صلبة غير حساسة في أنفسها ، والأعصاب كذلك في جميع الصفات . والدليل على أن الأعصاب غير حسّاسة في أنفسها : إنك إذا شددت العصبة برباط قوي ، شدا قوياً ، صار ما هو أسفل من موضع الشد : عديم الحس . وذلك يدل على أن العصب غير حساس في نفسه ، وإنما يجري إليه الحس من موضع آخر. فثبت : أن الشرايين والأعصاب من جنس واحد . وأما المقدمة الثانية : وهي في بيان أن الأعصاب والشرايانات ، لما كانت من جنس واحد ، أمكن تولد الأعصاب من الشرايانات : فهو أنا نقول : إن هذا الشرايانات لما إنقسمت وتشعبت ، ودقت وصغرت ، ونفدت في الدماغ ، التف بعضها على بعض ، وانضمت أجزاؤها واتصلت . فصارت على صورة الأعصاب. وتمام الكلام فيه : أن هذه الشرايين حين انفصلت عن القلب ، كان يحتاج إليها في أن تكون حاملة للدم والروح الحيواني إلى جوهر الدماغ ، فلما صغرت ونفذت في جوهر الدماغ ، وحصل هذا المقصود ، حصل الاستغناء عنها في هذا العمل ، فلا جرم صرفت إلى بعض وصارت على صورة الأعصاب ؛ وبهذا الطريق صارت الشريانات أعصاباً . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : القلب هو المنبت للشريانات ، وهذه الشريانات هي التي تولدت الأعصاب منها ، فحينئذ يكون المنبت للأعصاب ، هو القلب . وهذا قول ذكره و خروسیس ، وكان من أفاضل الحكماء وكان شريكاً لـ : أرسطاطاليس ، في العلم . ونصرناه نحن بهذه البيانات. وبهذا الطريق يسقط كلام جالينوس بالكلية
The second aspect: We concede that abundance and strength are only attained at the origin. But we say: There is no dispute that the heart is the source of the arteries. Then, the bodies of the arteries are of the same genus as the bodies of the nerves. And if that is so, it is possible that the nerves are generated from those arteries. By this estimation, the heart is the source and origin of the nerve. So the establishment of this statement requires proving premises. The first premise: That the veins are of the same genus as the nerves. The proof for that is that the bodies of the veins spread out and divide into fibrous splinters that have no sensation, and they are white, soft, bloodless, hard, and insensitive in themselves, and the nerves are likewise in all these attributes. And the proof that the nerves are insensitive in themselves is that if you tie a nerve with a strong ligature, tightly, what is below the binding becomes insensible. That indicates that the nerve is not sensitive in itself, but rather sensation flows to it from another place. So it is established that the arteries and nerves are of one genus. As for the second premise: It is to explain that since the nerves and arteries are of one genus, it is possible for nerves to be generated from arteries. It is that we say: When these arteries divided and branched, and became fine and small, and penetrated into the brain, some twisted around others, their parts joined and connected, so they became in the form of nerves. The completion of the matter is that these arteries, when they separated from the heart, were needed to carry blood and animal spirit to the substance of the brain. When they became small and penetrated into the substance of the brain, and this purpose was achieved, they became superfluous in this function, so they were turned to something else and became in the form of nerves. In this way the arteries became nerves. And if this is established, then we say: The heart is the source of the arteries, and these arteries are the ones from which the nerves were generated. Therefore, the source of the nerves is the heart. This is a statement mentioned by Kharosis, who was among the excellent philosophers and was a partner of Aristotle in knowledge. We have supported it with these explanations. By this way Galen's statement is completely refuted.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument in favor of the heart as the source of nerves. The argument rests on two premises: First, that veins (including arteries) are of the same genus as nerves, supported by evidence of similar physical properties and the insensitivity of nerves. Second, that because they are of the same genus, nerves can be generated from arteries. The author explains that as arteries branch and enter the brain, they change form and become nerves. Thus the heart, as the origin of arteries, is also the origin of nerves. The author attributes this view to a philosopher named Kharosis, a partner of Aristotle, and claims that this argument completely refutes Galen.

Key Terms
القلب
الشرايين
الأعصاب
المنبت
جنس
الروح الحيواني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nerves themselves are sensitive and generate sensation.
Correction: The passage proves nerves are insensitive in themselves; sensation flows to them from elsewhere, likely the brain.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Galen's Five Arguments for the Distinction of Nerves and Arteries
Razi enumerates Galen's five arguments distinguishing nerves from arteries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Galen's five empirical arguments that nerves and arteries are fundamentally different in structure and function.

Core Idea
Galen used five key observations to prove nerves and arteries are distinct.

Razi presents Galen's counterarguments against the claim that nerves and arteries share the same genus. These five points are based on observable differences in pulsation, hollowness, blood content, layering, and function. Understanding these arguments helps us appreciate the early medical and theological reasoning about the body's design.

Guided Path
1
Read Galen's five points
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the list of five arguments.
Notice that each point is a distinct empirical observation.
2
Identify each point
For each of the five points, note the specific characteristic that differentiates nerves from arteries.
The key terms in the passage label these characteristics.
3
Consider the implications
Think about how these differences show that nerves and arteries have distinct functions in the body.
For example, pulsation suggests a different role in circulation.
4
Connect to theological context
Understand why this argument matters in kalam regarding causality and design.
This passage is part of a larger debate about the nature of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوه : واعلم : أن جالينوس أجاب عن هذا الكلام من وجهين : الأول : قال : الدليل على أن الأعصاب ليست من جنس الشريانات : الأول : إن الشريانات نابضة ، والأعصاب ليست نابضة . الثاني : إن الشريانات مجوفة ، والأعصاب ليست كذلك . الثالث : إن الشريانات محتوية على الدم. بدليل أنها إذا ثقبت ، جلب ذلك الثقب على صاحبه من انفجار الدم أمراً صعباً ، والعصب كله لا دم فيه . الرابع : إن الشريانات مؤلفة من طبقتين : إحداهما : تتحلل إلى أجزاء ذاهبة في العرض على الاستدارة ، والأخرى تتحلل إلى أجزاء تذهب على الاستقامة في الطول . وأما الأعصاب فهي تتحلل إلى ليف أبيض عديم الدم ، ذاهب على الاستقامة في الطول . الخامس : إنك إذا شددت العصبة ، حصل عدم الحس والحركة
Aspects: Know that Galen answered this statement in two ways: The first: He said: The proof that the nerves are not of the same genus as the arteries: First: The arteries pulsate, and the nerves do not pulsate. Second: The arteries are hollow, and the nerves are not. Third: The arteries contain blood, as evidenced by the fact that if they are punctured, the puncture brings about severe bleeding for the person, while the nerve has no blood in it. Fourth: The arteries are composed of two layers: one dissolves into parts going in the circular direction, and the other dissolves into parts going straight lengthwise. As for the nerves, they dissolve into white, bloodless fibers going straight lengthwise. Fifth: If you tie a nerve, loss of sensation and movement occurs.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage lists Galen's counterarguments against the claim that nerves and arteries are of the same genus. Galen presents five points of differentiation: (1) arteries pulsate, nerves do not; (2) arteries are hollow, nerves are solid; (3) arteries contain blood, nerves contain no blood; (4) arteries have two layers with different fiber orientations, nerves have a single type of fiber; (5) tying a nerve causes loss of sensation and movement, implying nerves transmit sensation whereas arteries do not. These points collectively aim to show that nerves and arteries are fundamentally distinct in structure and function.

Key Terms
نابضة
مجوفة
دم
طبقتين
ليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think nerves and arteries are identical or of the same genus because both are tubular structures in the body.
Correction: Galen's five points show they differ in pulsation, hollowness, blood content, layering, and function, so they are fundamentally distinct.

Checkpoint
According to Galen's points, what is a key difference between arteries and nerves?
A) Both pulsate.
B) Nerves are hollow and arteries are solid.
C) Arteries contain blood while nerves do not.
D) Nerves have two layers.

Key Takeaway
Galen provided five empirical distinctions proving nerves and arteries are not of the same genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Galen’s five arguments show that God designed nerves and arteries with distinct purposes. When you notice something in your own body—like your pulse or sensation—do you treat it as a sign of intentional creation, or do you take it for granted? Will you today thank the Creator for at least one such detail?
?
The passage warns against the misconception that nerves and arteries are the same. Are there areas in your life where you have lumped together two distinct truths—like your relationship with God and your habits—and treated them as one? What would change if you carefully distinguished them?
?
If you were to lose sensation or movement (as the fifth argument describes), you would realize the gift of these distinct systems. In what way are you currently 'tying' something in your own soul—neglecting a gift until it is threatened? How can you awaken gratitude today?

Nature's Economy and the Transformation of Arteries
The author refutes Galen's objection by applying the principle of nature's economy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophical principle of nature's economy supports the transformation of arteries into nerves.

Core Idea
Nature minimizes instruments, so arteries repurpose into nerves.

Galen argued that the structure of arteries disproves their transformation into nerves. The author responds by invoking a fundamental principle of nature: she uses the fewest instruments necessary. Since arteries already convey blood and spirit to the brain, nature does not create new instruments but transforms the arterial remnants into nerves. This lesson examines that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand Galen's objection
Recall that Galen argued arteries have certain qualities that prevent their transformation into nerves. The author begins by proposing a counter-possibility.
The possibility is that arteries change form after reaching the brain.
2
Grasp the principle of nature's economy
The author cites a philosophical consensus: nature avoids unnecessary instruments when fewer suffice. This is the key premise used to support the transformation.
This principle is known as 'nature does nothing in vain'.
3
See how it applies to arteries
Arteries were created to convey blood and spirit to the brain. Once that purpose is achieved, nature reuses the same material to form nerves, which serve a different purpose (sensation and movement).
The transformation is efficient and consistent with nature's economy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا جاز ذلك، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن الحال في الشرايين كذلك؟ فإن تلك الصفات التي ذكرها (جالينوس) كانت حاصلة للشرايين من وقت انشعابها من القلب إلى وقت نفوذها في جرم الدماغ. أما بعد ذلك فقد حدثت لها صورة أخرى، وخلقة أخرى، بخلاف الصورة الأولى. وهذا الاحتمال لا يبطل بما ذكره (جالينوس)، والدليل عليه: أن الفلاسفة اتفقوا على أن الطبيعة، متى أمكنها الاكتفاء بالآلات القليلة، لم تعدل عنه إلى إعداد الآلات الكثيرة. فههنا هذه الأجسام المسماة بالشريانات، صورت بصور موافقة لتحصيل مطلوب معين، وهو إيصال الدم والروح إلى الدماغ، فلما حصل هذا المقصود، فحينئذ تركب الطبيعة تلك الأجسام على صورة أخرى، صالحة لمقصود آخر، وهي صبورة الأعصاب، فثبت: أن الكلام الذي ذكره جالينوس لا يمكن جعله طعنا في مذهب (خروسيس). وأما جوابه الثاني: فضعيف أيضاً. وذلك لأن الروح القلبي، كما صعد إلى الرأس في الشرايين الصغيرة، فقد صعد أيضاً إلى أسفل البدن في الشرايين الصغيرة. ثم إن القسم الصاعد إلى الرأس، تغير عن حالته بسبب وصوله إلى جرم الدماغ، وصار روحاً دماغياً حاملاً لقوة الحس والحركة. وأما القسم النازل من الأرواح القلبية، لما وصلت إلى الرجلين، فلم يتغير عن حالته البتة. فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الحال في أجرام الشريانات كذلك؟ وتمام التقرير فيه: أنا ذكرنا أن الطبيعة تسعى في تقليل الآلة (1) ففي الدماغ احتاجت الطبيعة إلى إعداد آلة حاملة لقوة الحس والحركة، فلا يبعد تكوين تلك الآلة من تلك الشظايا الشريانية. ولم توجد هذه الحاجة (2) في الرجلين. فظهر الفرق، وثبت بهذا البيان الذي ذكرناه: أن الكلام الذي ذكره جالينوس في إبطال الاحتمال الذي ذكره خروسيس، في غاية الضعف والسقوط. وههنا آخر الكلام في الجواب عن دليل (1) جالينوس، على أن منبت العصب هو الدماغ.
If that is permissible, then why may it not be said that the case with the arteries is similar? For those qualities which Galen mentioned were present in the arteries from the time of their divergence from the heart until the time of their penetration into the substance of the brain. But after that, another form and another constitution occurred for them, unlike the first form. And this possibility is not invalidated by what Galen mentioned. The evidence for it is that the philosophers agreed that nature, when it can suffice with few instruments, does not turn away from that to prepare many instruments. Here these bodies called arteries are formed with forms suitable for achieving a specific purpose, which is conveying blood and spirit (rūḥ) to the brain. When this purpose was achieved, then nature constructed those bodies in another form suitable for another purpose, which is the origin of the nerves. So it is established that the statement which Galen made cannot be taken as an objection to the doctrine of Khrusīs. As for his second answer, it is also weak. That is because the cardiac spirit, just as it ascended to the head in the small arteries, also descended to the lower body in the small arteries. Then the part ascending to the head changed from its state due to its arrival at the substance of the brain and became a cerebral spirit bearing the power of sensation and movement. As for the descending part of the cardiac spirits, when it reached the legs, it did not change from its state at all. So why may it not be that the case with the bodies of the arteries is similar? And the complete exposition of that is: we mentioned that nature strives to minimize the instrument. In the brain, nature needed to prepare an instrument bearing the power of sensation and movement, so it is not unlikely that it formed that instrument from those arterial fragments. And this need was not present in the legs. So the difference became clear, and it was established by this exposition which we mentioned that the statement which Galen made in invalidating the possibility mentioned by Khrusīs is extremely weak and fallacious. And here ends the discussion in response to Galen's proof concerning the origin of the nerve being the brain.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Galen's argument that the arteries disprove the heart's role in nerve origin. The author begins by proposing a counterargument: if Galen's objection is allowed, then why can we not say the same about the arteries? The qualities Galen described for arteries (such as structure and function) are present only from the heart to the brain; after reaching the brain, the arteries transform into a different form suitable for nerve origin. The author supports this by appealing to a philosophical principle: nature minimizes instruments. Since the arteries already served the purpose of conveying blood and spirit to the brain, nature repurposes their remnants to form nerves, avoiding the need for a separate instrument. The author further strengthens the argument by comparing the upward (to brain) and downward (to legs) pathways of cardiac spirit: the spirit changes in the brain but not in the legs, explaining why nerves originate only from the brain area. The conclusion is that Galen's attempted refutation fails. The passage ends with a note that this completes the response to Galen's proof that the nerve originates from the brain.

Nature's principle of minimizing instruments supports the transformation of arteries into nerves.

?

Cardiac Spirit: Ascent and Descent
The author uses the behavior of cardiac spirit to further refute Galen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the behavior of cardiac spirit explains why nerves originate only from the brain.

Core Idea
Cardiac spirit changes in the brain but not in the legs, explaining nerve origin.

The author presents a second response to Galen: the cardiac spirit ascending to the brain transforms into a cerebral spirit, while the descending part remains unchanged. This difference explains why the transformation of arteries into nerves occurs only in the brain region. In this lesson, we examine that argument and its conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two pathways of cardiac spirit
The author notes that cardiac spirit travels upward to the brain and downward to the legs via small arteries. Notice that both pathways exist.
The spirit is the same initially.
2
Notice the transformation in brain vs. legs
Upon reaching the brain, the spirit changes to a cerebral spirit carrying sensation and movement. In the legs, it remains unchanged. This key difference is crucial for the argument.
The brain causes a transformation.
3
Connect to nature's economy and conclusion
Nature's economy requires that in the brain, where a new instrument (nerves) is needed, the arterial fragments are transformed. In the legs, no such need exists, so no transformation occurs. Thus, Galen's objection fails.
The argument reinforces the earlier principle.

Aristotle's Proof: The Heart as Origin of Nerves
Aristotle's logical proof for the heart as nerve origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Aristotle's deductive argument that the heart is the origin of nerves based on the requirement of solidity for voluntary movement.

Core Idea
The heart's solidity makes it the logical source of nerves for voluntary motion.

Aristotle presents a concise logical proof to establish the heart as the origin of nerves, challenging the common view that it is the brain. The argument hinges on a simple premise: voluntary movement requires a solid, strong instrument. By examining the properties of the heart and brain, Aristotle deduces which organ is more suitable. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read Aristotle's argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Aristotle states his premise and then applies it to the heart and brain.
The argument is deductive (qiyāsī).
2
Identify the premise
Find the general rule that Aristotle uses: voluntary movement requires a strong, solid instrument. This is the foundation of the proof.
3
Examine the evidence for the heart
Aristotle gives three specific reasons why the heart is solid. List them in your own words.
These are three distinct kinds of solidity.
4
Understand the conclusion
See how Aristotle brings the premise and evidence together to conclude that the heart is more appropriate than the brain as the origin of nerves.
5
Reflect on the method
Consider how this argument illustrates a rational approach to determining natural causes. What does it assume about the relationship between form and function?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن أرسطاطاليس احتج على أن منبت العصب هو القلب بأن قال: الحركة الإرادية لا بد وأن تكون بآلة صلبة قوية، والدماغ ليس بجرمه شيء من الصلابة والقوة، فامتنع كونه منبتاً للعصب. وأما القلب ففيه أنواع من الصلابة. منها أن لحمه قوي [ شديد صلب (۲) ] من سائر اللحوم. ومنها: أنه فيه من الرباطات العصبية مقداراً كثيراً. ومنها: أنه بسبب كثرة حركته، لا بد وأن يكون أقوى جرماً، وإذا كان كذلك فقد ظهر أنه جعل القلب منبتاً للأعصاب، التي هي آلات للحركات القوية، أولى من جعل الدماغ منبتاً لها.
Know that Aristotle argued that the origin of the nerve (ʿaṣab) is the heart, saying: 'Voluntary movement must necessarily be by means of a strong, solid instrument, and the brain in its substance has nothing of solidity and strength, so it is impossible for it to be the origin of the nerve. As for the heart, it contains kinds of solidity. Among them is that its flesh is stronger [intensely solid?] than the rest of the flesh. Among them is that it contains a great amount of nerve ligaments. Among them is that, due to the frequency of its movement, it must necessarily be stronger in substance. And if that is so, then it has become apparent that making the heart the origin of the nerves, which are instruments for strong movements, is more appropriate than making the brain the origin of them.'
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents Aristotle's proof that the origin of nerves is the heart, not the brain. The argument is based on a premise: voluntary movement requires a solid, strong instrument. Since the brain lacks solidity and strength, it cannot be the origin. The heart, on the contrary, has several types of solidity: its flesh is stronger than other flesh; it contains many nerve ligaments; and its constant movement makes it stronger in substance. Therefore, the heart is more fitting to be the source of nerves, which are instruments for strong movements. The proof is deductive (qiyāsī), relying on a general premise about voluntary movement.

Key Terms
عصب
قلب
دماغ
حركة إرادية
آلة صلبة قوية
رباطات عصبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Aristotle denies the brain any role in sensation or movement.
Correction: Aristotle only argues about the origin of nerves, not the eventual function of the brain. The brain may still have a role in processing signals.

Key Takeaway
Aristotle's proof that the heart is the origin of nerves rests on the premise that voluntary movement requires solidity, which the heart possesses and the brain lacks.

Second Answer to Galen's Nerve Origin Argument
The author presents the second answer to Galen's argument about nerve thickness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second response to Galen's argument about nerve origin and why it is considered weak.

Core Idea
Thick nerves at the brain do not prove origin; softness contradicts it.

In this passage, the author critiques a second argument from Galen about nerve origin. The Aristotelians point out a contradiction in Galen's reasoning, showing that thickness alone is not decisive. Let's examine this response and its weakness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through.
2
Identify Galen's claim
Notice that Galen argues from the thickness and abundance of nerves at the brain to conclude the brain is the origin.
This is the premise under attack.
3
Understand the Aristotelian counter
The Aristotelians respond by noting that if thick nerves prove origin, then the brain's softness disproves it, because a soft organ cannot produce tough nerves.
This creates a contradiction.
4
Observe the author's conclusion
The author judges this answer as weak and concludes that Galen's argument falls due to the contradiction. The debate between heart and brain is settled.
The author implies the heart is the true origin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوتها عند الدماغ. وقد بينا أن هذا القدر لا يدل على كون الدماغ منبتاً للأعصاب. وأما الجواب الثاني: فضعيف أيضاً. لأن جالينوس استدل بغلظ العصب وكثرته عند الدماغ، على أن المنبت هو الدماغ. وأصحاب أرسطاطاليس قالوا: هذا الوجه الثاني الذي ذكره إن دل على قوله فكون الدماغ في غاية اللين وكون العصب في غاية القوة، يمنع من كون الدماغ منبتاً للعصب، ولما حصل التعارض فيه سقط كلام جالينوس. فهذا تمام الكلام في أن منبت العصب هو القلب أو الدماغ.
And its strength is at the brain. We have shown that this degree does not indicate that the brain is the origin of nerves. As for the second answer: it is also weak. Because Galen argued by the thickness and abundance of nerves at the brain that the origin is the brain. The followers of Aristotle said: this second aspect he mentioned, if it indicates his statement, then the extreme softness of the brain and the extreme strength of the nerve prevent the brain from being the origin of the nerve. Since there is contradiction in it, Galen's speech falls. So this completes the discussion on whether the origin of the nerve is the heart or the brain.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents the second answer to a Galenic argument. The author begins by stating that a previous point (about strength at the brain) does not prove the brain is the origin of nerves. Then he introduces the second answer, calling it weak. Galen argued from the thickness and abundance of nerves at the brain that the brain must be their origin. The Aristotelians respond: if this argument proves anything, the brain's softness and the nerve's hardness contradict the idea that the brain produces nerves. Since the reasoning is contradictory, Galen's argument fails. The passage concludes that the entire issue of whether the nerve origin is the heart or the brain has been settled, implying the heart is correct.

Key Terms

غلظ العصب
الدماغ
القلب
أرسطاطاليس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thick nerves at the brain mean the brain is the origin.
Correction: Softness of the brain contradicts the need for a strong origin.

Checkpoint
What is the main weakness in Galen's argument as presented in this passage?
A) It relies on an incorrect measurement of nerve thickness.
B) The brain's softness contradicts the idea that it produces strong nerves.
C) Aristotle himself denied that nerves originate from the brain.
D) The blood supply to the brain is insufficient.

Key Takeaway
Galen's argument from nerve thickness is self-defeating because the brain's softness contradicts the production of strong nerves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Just as the brain cannot produce strong nerves due to its softness, what weakness in your own character prevents you from producing the 'strong' actions you claim to value? Are you willing to confront that contradiction today, or will you continue to ignore it?
?
If you were in Galen's position, would you have the humility to accept when your own argument is self-defeating? Where in your life are you stubbornly holding onto a flawed argument because admitting defeat would cost you your pride? What would it take for you to let go?

Brain as Conduit, Heart as Source
The author presents the second stance: conceding brain as nerve origin but denying it as source of sensation and motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author concedes the brain's role as nerve origin but argues that the source of sensation and motion is the heart, thereby refuting Galen's argument.

Core Idea
The brain is the conduit, not the source, of sensation and motion.

In this passage, the author tackles Galen's argument by making a crucial distinction. He grants that the brain produces nerves but questions whether that makes it the source of the powers themselves. Instead, he proposes that the heart is the true origin, with the brain serving as a transmitter. This logical move shows how theological reasoning can reinterpret anatomical evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Notice the concession, question, and explanation.
The author begins by agreeing with Galen but then challenges the conclusion.
2
Identify the concession and the question
Find where the author grants that the brain is the origin of nerves. Then find the rhetorical question that challenges the inference.
The question is: why does that make the brain the source of sensation and motion?
3
Understand the alternative explanation
The author proposes that the power of sensation and motion is generated in the heart, and the brain sends a fixed instrument to the heart. Trace this possibility.
This is a hypothetical scenario to show Galen's argument is not definitive.
4
Reflect on the force of the argument
Since this possibility is logically valid, Galen's argument fails. Consider why the author uses 'God knows best' at the end.
It signals the argument is sufficient to refute, but ultimate truth is with God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقام الثاني في الجواب عن شبهة جالينوس: فهو أن نقول: سلمنا أن الدماغ هو المنبت للعصب، الذي هو آلة للقوة الحساسة، والحركة الإرادية. فلم قلتم: إنه يلزم من هذا المعنى كون الدماغ معدناً لقوة الحس والحركة؟ بيانه: أنه لا يبعد أن تكون قوة الحس والحركة متولدة في القلب. إلا أن الدماغ يرسل إليه آلة ثابتة منه إلى القلب، فيستفيد بتلك الآلة قوة الحس والحركة من القلب. وإذا كان هذا الاحتمال قائماً، فقد سقطت الحجة التي ذكرها جالينوس. والله أعلم.
As for the second stance in answering Galen's doubt: it is that we say: we grant that the brain is the origin of the nerve, which is the instrument for the sensitive power and voluntary motion. So why do you say that from this meaning it follows that the brain is the source of the power of sensation and motion? Its explanation: it is not far-fetched that the power of sensation and motion is generated in the heart. But the brain sends to it a fixed instrument from it to the heart, so it benefits by that instrument the power of sensation and motion from the heart. If this possibility stands, then the argument which Galen mentioned falls. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents the second stance in answering Galen's doubt. The author concedes that the brain is the physical origin of nerves (the instruments of sensation and motion). However, he denies that this makes the brain the source of the powers themselves. He asks: why must it follow that the brain is the 'mine' (source) of sensation and motion? He explains that it is possible that the powers are generated in the heart, and the brain merely transmits them via a fixed instrument (the nerve) sent from the brain to the heart. Since this possibility is logically valid, Galen's argument fails. The author concludes with 'God knows best', emphasizing the hypothetical nature.

Correction: The passage shows a distinction between being an instrument (nerve) and being the source (ma‘din) of the power. The brain may be the origin of the instrument, but the power itself could originate elsewhere, such as the heart.

Checkpoint

The brain is the instrument, not the source; the heart is the true origin of sensation and motion according to this argument.

Galen's Ligature Argument and the Author's Response
Galen's second argument using ligature and the author's response regarding heart spirit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Galen's second argument against the heart as the source of perception and the author's response based on the nature of the heart spirit.

Core Idea
The ligature test can be explained by the heart needing cooling from the brain.

In this passage, the author presents another argument by Galen that seems to challenge the heart's role as the seat of perceptive power. Galen uses a physical test: tying a nerve and observing where sensation remains. The author then offers a clever response invoking the heat of the heart spirit and its need for brain cooling. Understanding this exchange is key to grasping the Islamic theological position on the heart's primacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow of the argument and response.
Notice the structure: first the argument, then the answer.
2
Identify Galen's argument structure
Break down Galen's reasoning: premise (if heart is source, sensation should remain on heart side after tying), experiment (tying a nerve), observed result (sensation remains on brain side), conclusion (flow is from brain to heart).
Galen calls this argument 'better than the first' because it requires fewer premises.
3
Examine the author's response
The author challenges Galen's implicit assumption that the heart's readiness is independent of the brain. He introduces the heart spirit's heat and its need for cooling from the brain. Blocking the passage prevents cooling, so the heart loses its readiness.
The response ends with a rhetorical question 'why may it not be said?'
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this response preserves the heart as the ultimate source of perceptive power while explaining the ligature phenomenon. Ask yourself: does the response fully refute Galen or only offer an alternative explanation?
This touches on the nature of causality and the role of intermediate causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية لجالينوس على فساد القول بأن معدن القوة المدركة هو القلب: إنه لو كانت قوة الحس والحركة تنفذ من القلب إلى الدماغ، لكنا إذا شددنا العصب بخيط شداً قوياً، وجب أن تبقى قوة الحس والحركة في الجانب الذي يلي القلب، وأن تبطل من الجانب الذي يلي الدماغ، لكن الأمر بالضد منه. فعلمنا: أن قوة الحس والحركة تجري من الدماغ إلى القلب، ولا تجري من القلب إلى الدماغ. وهذه الحجة أحسن من الحجة الأولى، ولا نحتاج فيها إلى المقدمات الكثيرة المذكورة في الحجة الأولى.
والجواب: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: الروح القلبي يكون في غاية الحرارة. فإذا كان بينه وبين الدماغ منفذ مفتوح، وصل تبريد الدماغ إلى القلب، فاعتدل واستعد لقبول قوة الحس والحركة. فأما إذا انسد ذلك المسلك عن القلب أثر الدماغ، فلا جرم لم يبق مستعداً لقبول قوة الحس والحركة، فبطلت هذه القوة من الجانب الذي يلي القلب؟
Galen's second argument for the invalidity of the statement that the source of the perceptive power is the heart: If the power of sensation and motion passed from the heart to the brain, then when we tightly tied the nerve with a thread, it would be necessary that the power of sensation and motion remain in the side next to the heart and cease in the side next to the brain, but the opposite occurs. Thus we know that the power of sensation and motion flows from the brain to the heart and not from the heart to the brain. This argument is better than the first, and we do not need the many premises mentioned in the first argument.
The answer: Why may it not be said: The heart spirit is extremely hot. If there is an open passage between it and the brain, the cooling of the brain reaches the heart, so it becomes balanced and prepared to receive the power of sensation and motion. But if that passage from the heart to the brain is blocked, then inevitably it is no longer prepared to receive the power of sensation and motion, so this power ceases in the side next to the heart?
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents Galen's second argument and the author's answer. Galen argues: if sensation/motion originates in the heart and flows to the brain, then tying a nerve should leave sensation on the heart side and abolish it on the brain side. The opposite happens, proving flow from brain to heart. The author responds by invoking the nature of the 'heart spirit' (a subtle substance) which is extremely hot. Normally, brain cooling reaches the heart via an open channel, balancing the heart and preparing it to receive sensation/motion. If the channel is blocked (as by tying), cooling cannot reach the heart, so it becomes too hot and loses its readiness, explaining why sensation ceases on the heart side. This answer preserves the heart as the ultimate source while explaining the ligature effect.

Key Terms
شدة العصب
الروح القلبي
تبريد الدماغ
قوة الحس والحركة
معدن القوة المدركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ligature test definitively proves the brain is the source of perceptive power, disproving the heart's role.
Correction: The author shows the test can be explained by the heart spirit needing cooling from the brain; the heart remains the source, but its readiness depends on brain cooling.

Checkpoint
What is Galen's second argument based on?
A) The thickness of nerves at the brain.
B) Tying a nerve and observing where sensation remains.
C) The emptiness of cavities in the brain.
D) The heartbeat continuing after brain removal.

Key Takeaway
Galen's ligature test does not disprove the heart as source; its outcome can be explained by the heart spirit's need for brain cooling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author questioned Galen's widely accepted argument with a simple 'why may it not be said?' What is one assumption in your life that you have never questioned? Commit today to examine that assumption as if hearing 'why may it not be said?' and journal your honest response.

Galen's Third Argument: Dissection Test on the Heart
Galen's third argument: brain vs heart origin of spirit, with dissection test.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Galen's third argument against the heart as the source of spirit, including his dissection experiment and the needle test on the left ventricle.

Core Idea
Galen used a needle test to prove the heart's left cavity contains blood, not spirit.

Galen, a renowned physician and philosopher, challenged the heart's role as the origin of the spirit by appealing to anatomy and direct observation. In this passage, he describes a dissection experiment where the heart is exposed without killing the animal, and then a needle is pierced into the left ventricle to observe whether blood or spirit emerges. His findings undermine the common belief that the left cavity is filled with spirit.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the key claim: the brain is a more suitable origin for the spirit than the heart because it has empty cavities.
The passage is incomplete, but the core argument and experiment are fully presented.
2
Identify the components of the argument
The passage has two main parts: first, a logical preference for the brain based on empty spaces; second, an experimental refutation of the heart's left ventricle containing spirit.
Focus on how Galen uses anatomical observation (empty places in brain vs. full right cavity) and then a live dissection test.
3
Analyze the dissection test
Galen describes two experiments: exposing the heart without killing the animal (pulse remains normal), and piercing the left ventricle's membrane with a needle, which yields blood, not spirit.
The condition of keeping the heart warm is interesting—avoiding cold air to maintain pulse.
4
Consider the implications
Reflect on why this argument is important for the larger debate about the source of sensation and motion. If the left ventricle contains blood, it cannot be the source of the subtle spirit.
The passage cuts off mid-sentence, but the intended conclusion is that the heart is not the origin of spirit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة لجالينوس: إن الحكماء والأطباء اتفقوا على أن الحامل لقوة الحس والحركة: جسم لطيف نافذ في الأعضاء وهو الروح. وإذا كان كذلك، فالدماغ أن يكون مبدأ لهذه الروح أولى من القلب. وذلك لأنا نجد في الدماغ مواضع خالية، وتلك المواضع الخالية تصلح لأن تتولد فيها تلك الأرواح، وأما القلب فليس كذلك، لأن التجويف الأيمن منه مملوء من الدم، وإنما الشبهة في التجويف الأيسر، فإنه يعتقد أنه مملوء من الروح.
قال جالينوس: وليس الأمر كذلك فإن القلب إذا كشف عنه وأبرز من غير أن تثقب وتخرق أغشيته، لم يمت الحيوان بهذا السبب، بل قد يلبث مدة طويلة، تلمسه بيدك، وتنظر إليه بعينك، وهو مكشوف، فتعرف كيف أن نبضه في هذه الحالة يساوي نبضه قبل أن تكشف عنه، ولكن بشرط أن يقع هذا التشريح في موضع لا يكون هواؤه بارداً، لئلا يتبرد القلب. فإنه لو برد لصار النبض ضعيفاً بطيئاً متفاوتاً.
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إنا إذا غرزنا إبرة في غشاء هذا التجويف الأيسر، سال في الحال منه دم، ولو كان هذا التجويف مملوءاً من الروح، لوجب أن لا يسيل منه الدم البتة، ولو كان هذا التجويف في بعضه روح وفي بعضه دم، لوجب أن تخرج الروح أولاً، ثم يسيل الدم بعده. وعلى
Galen's third argument: The philosophers and physicians agree that the carrier of the power of sensation and motion is a subtle body penetrating the organs, which is the spirit. If so, the brain is more worthy to be the origin of this spirit than the heart. That is because we find in the brain empty places, and those empty places are suitable for those spirits to be generated in them. As for the heart, it is not like that, because its right cavity is filled with blood. The doubt is only about the left cavity, for it is believed to be filled with spirit.
Galen said: It is not so. For if the heart is uncovered and exposed without piercing and tearing its membranes, the animal does not die for this reason; rather it may last a long time. You touch it with your hand and look at it with your eye while it is uncovered, and you know how its pulse in this state equals its pulse before you uncovered it, but on condition that this dissection takes place in a place where its air is not cold, lest the heart become cold. For if it becomes cold, the pulse becomes weak, slow, and irregular.
If you know this, we say: If we pierce a needle into the membrane of this left cavity, blood immediately flows from it. If this cavity were filled with spirit, it would be necessary that no blood at all flows from it. And if this cavity had some spirit and some blood, it would be necessary that the spirit come out first, then the blood flow after it. And on
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents Galen's third argument against the heart as source of sensation. First, Galen establishes that the carrier of sensation/motion is a subtle spirit. He argues the brain is more suited to originate this spirit because it has empty cavities (ventricles) where spirit can be generated, while the heart's right cavity is full of blood and the left cavity is debated. Galen then describes a dissection experiment: the heart can be exposed without killing the animal if cold air is avoided, and its pulse remains normal. He then describes a needle test: piercing the left cavity's membrane yields blood, not spirit. If it were full of spirit, no blood would flow; if mixed, spirit would escape first. The text cuts off mid-sentence ('and on'), presumably leading to a further conclusion that the left cavity contains blood, not spirit, thus the heart cannot be the source of spirit. The author does not give his own answer in this fragment.

Two possibilities regarding psychological power transfer
Analyzes two possibilities for the presence of psychological powers in the spirit before transfer to the brain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possibilities for the existence of psychological powers in the spirit before their transfer to the brain, and the implications for how they produce actions.

Core Idea
The brain is needed either to activate dormant powers or to silence competing ones.

In this passage, the author examines a key problem in understanding how psychological powers move from the heart to the brain. He proposes two scenarios: either the powers already existed in the spirit before transfer, or they did not. Both lead to different explanations for why the brain is necessary for psychological actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possibilities
Read the passage and note the two main divisions: whether the psychological power existed in the spirit before transfer or not.
The author clearly states 'إما أن يقال ... أو ما كانت موجودة'.
2
Understand the author's preferred view
Notice that the author mentions that he chose the second possibility (that the power did not exist before transfer) in his work 'al-Adwiya al-Qalbiyya'.
3
Analyze the sub-explanations under the first possibility
Even if the power existed before, the brain is still needed. There are two explanations: either the transfer is a condition for the power to become effective, or it removes conflicting powers. Compare them.
The text uses 'فالأول' and 'وأما الثاني' to mark the two explanations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنما انتقل من القلب إلى الدماغ . [ والكبد (۱) ] فإما أن يقال : القوة النفسانية كانت موجودة في ذلك الروح قبل انتقاله إلى الدماغ ، أو ما كانت موجودة فيه أما القسم الثاني : وهو أنها ما كانت موجودة فيه قبل انتقالها إلى الدماغ ، فهو الذي اختاره في الأدوية القلبية وأما القسم الأول وهو أن هذه القوى كانت موجودة في ذلك الوقت . فلا نزاع في أن الأفعال النفسانية لا تصدر عنها إلا بعد حصولها في الدماغ . فالحاجة إلى الدماغ ، إما أن تكون لأجل حصول حالة وصفة ، أو لأجل زوال حالة وصفة . فالأول : وهو أن يقال (۳) : انتقالها إلى الدماغ شرط لا لحدوث تلك القوى في أنفسها ، بل لصيرورتها بحيث تصدر عنها أفعالها ، على معنى أن حصول تلك الأرواح الحاملة لتلك القوى [ في الدماغ " ] شرط لصدور آثارها عنها . وأما الثاني : فهو أن يقال : القوى الكثيرة كانت موجودة في ذلك الروح ، وتلك القوى متدافعة متمانعة ، فلما انتقل ذلك الروح إلى الدماغ ، بطل سائر القوى ، وبقيت هذه القوة سليمة عن المعارض ، فلا جرم صدر عنها الفعل .
It transferred from the heart to the brain. [ And the liver (1) ] So either it is said: the psychological power existed in that spirit before its transfer to the brain, or it did not exist in it. As for the second division: that it was not existing in it before its transfer to the brain, that is what he chose in al-Adwiya al-Qalbiyya. And as for the first division: that these powers were existing at that time. There is no dispute that psychological actions do not emanate from them except after their occurrence in the brain. So the need for the brain is either for the sake of acquiring a state and attribute, or for the sake of removing a state and attribute. The first: that it is said: their transfer to the brain is a condition not for the occurrence of those powers in themselves, but for them becoming such that their actions emanate from them, meaning that the occurrence of those spirits carrying those powers [in the brain] is a condition for the emanation of their effects from them. And as for the second: it is that it is said: many powers were existing in that spirit, and those powers are mutually repelling and mutually preventing; when that spirit transferred to the brain, all the other powers were nullified, and this power remained safe from opposition, so inevitably action emanated from it.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents a problem about the transfer of psychological powers from the heart to the brain. The author divides the issue into two possibilities: either the psychological power(s) existed in the spirit before its transfer to the brain, or they did not. He first mentions that the second possibility (that they were not existing) is what he chooses in his work 'al-Adwiya al-Qalbiyya'. For the first possibility, he notes consensus that psychological actions do not emanate until after the powers are in the brain, so the need for the brain is either to acquire a new state/attribute or to remove one. He then explains the first explanation: the transfer is a condition not for the creation of the powers themselves, but for them to become capable of producing actions; the presence of the spirits carrying those powers in the brain is necessary for their effects. The second explanation is that many conflicting powers existed in the spirit, and transfer to the brain nullifies the others, leaving one power able to act without opposition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate is about the location of the soul (whether it is in the heart or brain).
Correction: The debate is specifically about whether psychological powers exist in the spirit before transfer to the brain, and why the brain is necessary for their manifestation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that your psychological actions—your thoughts, decisions, intentions—may require the brain to either activate dormant powers or remove conflicting ones. Which of these two possibilities resonates more with your own experience of inner conflict? When you struggle to focus or act rightly, do you feel that the right power is dormant and needs activation, or that contrary powers within you must be silenced? Name one concrete situation today where you experienced such conflict.
?
This passage suggests that the spirit carries many competing powers, and the brain's role may be to nullify the others so that one power can act. What are the 'other powers' in your life that oppose the good actions you wish to perform? Are you actively seeking to weaken them, or do you let them remain? Commit to one specific step you will take today to remove a conflicting power so that a good action can emerge.
?
The author chooses the view that psychological powers are not present in the spirit before transfer to the brain—they arise upon transfer. If actions arise only through the brain's mediation, what does that imply about your accountability for thoughts and feelings that seem to come 'out of nowhere'? How does this understanding change the way you evaluate the origins of your own desires and intentions?

The Same Debate Applied to the Visual Power and Brain Spirit
Extends the heart-brain debate to the visual power and notes symmetry between Aristotle and Galen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the same rational problem about the timing of sensory power's existence applies to the brain spirit and the visual power, and why both Aristotle's and Galen's positions face symmetrical difficulties.

Core Idea
Both Aristotle and Galen face the same logical problem about when sensory power exists in the spirit.

This passage extends the earlier heart-brain debate to the visual power and the brain spirit. The author asks a specific question: when does the visual power exist in the brain spirit—upon reaching the crystalline lens, or was it already present but its manifestation depends on transfer? He then concludes that whatever difficulty arises for Aristotle's claim about the heart also arises for Galen's claim about the brain.

Guided Path

The crystalline (الجليدية) refers to the lens of the eye.
2

The passage compares Aristotle's view (heart as absolute chief) and Galen's view (brain as chief of senses). Understand the symmetry the author points out.
The author uses the phrase 'everything incumbent on Aristotle is also incumbent on Galen'.
3

The author then generalizes: everything that is applicable to Aristotle's claim that the heart is the absolute chief is also applicable to Galen's claim that the brain is the chief of the five external and internal senses. This implies that both views face symmetrical difficulties, so neither is inherently superior.

The Heart as Seat of Powers and Brain as Condition
Author states his view on location of powers and provides proof from genus-species logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's claim that psychic powers are inherently in the heart, with the brain serving only as a condition for their manifestation, and his proof from the logical relation between humanity and animality.

Core Idea
The heart holds the powers; the brain only lets them act.

In this passage, the author presents his own conclusion on the location of psychological powers, emphasizing that the heart is their seat while the brain is a necessary condition for their outward expression. He supports this with a logical argument based on the hierarchical relationship between species (human) and genus (animal).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation to capture the author's claim and proof.
Notice the author uses a proof from genus-species logic.
2
Identify the two key claims
Find where the author states that powers are in the heart and that the brain is a condition for manifestation, not for existence.
3
Follow the proof step by step
Trace the proof: soul attaches to heart -> humanity attained -> animality necessarily attained -> power of sensation and motion attained -> brain only needed for manifestation.
The key step is that humanity implies animality, and the differentia of animal is sensation and voluntary motion.
4
Consider the implication
Reflect on the conclusion: the brain does not create the powers; it only enables their actions to appear. This refutes the idea that the brain is the origin.
This is the author's preferred view against other opinions.
5
Reflect on the author's humility
Note the final sentence: complete knowledge belongs only to God, reminding us of the limits of human inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأقرب عندي : أن يقال : هذه القوى كانت موجودة في القلب . والدماغ شرط لظهور الأفعال عن تلك القوى . والدليل على صحة ما قلناه : أنه متى تعلقت النفس الناطقة بالقلب ، فقد حصلت الإنسانية ، ومتى حصلت الإنسانية فقد حصلت الحيوانية لا محالة ، لأن حصول النوع مع عدم الجنس محال ، والفصل المقوم لماهية الحيوان ، هو قوة الحس والحركة [ فـإذن كما تعلقت النفس بالقلب ، فقد حصلت قوة الحس والحركة ] الإرادية ، فلم يبق إلا أن يكون الدماغ شرطاً يحتاج إليه في ظهور الفعل عن تلك القوى . فهذا ما نقوله [ في هذا الباب ] والعلم بحقائق الأشياء على التمام والكمال ، ليس إلا الله تعالى .
And the closest to me is that it be said: these powers were present in the heart. And the brain is a condition for the manifestation of actions from those powers. And the proof for the correctness of what we have said: that whenever the rational soul attaches to the heart, humanity is attained, and whenever humanity is attained, animality is inevitably attained, because the occurrence of the species without the genus is impossible, and the differentia constituting the essence of animal is the power of sensation and voluntary motion [so then, just as the soul attaches to the heart, the power of sensation and voluntary motion is attained], so nothing remains except that the brain is a condition needed for the manifestation of action from those powers. This is what we say [in this chapter]. And knowledge of the realities of things in complete perfection is only for God Almighty.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage gives the author's own preferred view. He states that the psychological powers were present in the heart, and the brain is a condition (shart) for the manifestation (zuhūr) of actions from those powers, not for the existence of the powers themselves. He then provides a proof: when the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) attaches to the heart, humanity (al-insāniyya) is attained. Since humanity is a species of animality, it necessarily implies animality (al-ḥayawāniyya) because a species cannot exist without its genus. The defining differentia (al-faṣl) that constitutes the essence of animal is the power of sensation and voluntary motion. Therefore, as soon as the soul attaches to the heart, the power of sensation and voluntary motion is attained. So the brain is not needed for the existence of these powers—it is only needed for the manifestation of their actions. The author concludes by stating that this is his position, and that complete knowledge of realities belongs only to God.

الإنسانية
الحيوانية
القوة الحس والحركة
الدماغ
القلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The brain creates or originates the psychological powers.
Correction: The author argues that the powers are already present in the heart; the brain is only a condition for their actions to become manifest.

Checkpoint

B) Because humanity is a species of animality, and animality requires that power.
C) Because the brain transfers this power from the heart.
D) Because the soul creates the power in the brain first.

Key Takeaway
Powers exist in the heart; the brain merely facilitates their expression; this is proven by the necessary relationship between humanity and animality.

First Proof for the Createdness of the Soul: Unity or Multiplicity Dilemma
Razi presents the first proof for the soul's createdness: a dilemma on unity and multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof for the createdness of the rational soul, which argues that if souls were eternal, they would be either one or many, both of which are impossible.

Core Idea
If souls were eternal, they must be either one or many; both options lead to contradiction.

Razi begins presenting the arguments of those who affirm the creation of the rational soul. The first proof attacks the eternity of the soul by posing a dilemma: an eternal soul would either be a single entity or many. This passage lays out the initial division and begins to show why both options fail. Though the argument is cut short, the logic is already clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure of the argument: a conditional statement followed by a division into two possibilities.
The passage is incomplete; focus on the logic presented.
2
Identify the two divisions
The proof first divides into 'one or many', then further subdivides the 'one' option into 'remaining one' or 'becoming many'. Identify each division.
These divisions form the backbone of the proof.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why proving both divisions false would entail the soul's createdness. This is the core of the argument.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text

And those who advocate the creation of the soul argued with two arguments. The first argument is that they said: if the souls were eternal, they would be in pre-eternity either one or many. Both divisions are false, so the statement that they are eternal is false. And we say: it is impossible to say that they were one, because if they were one in pre-eternity, then at the time of their attachment to the bodies, either it is said that they remain in that unity, or it is said that they become multiple upon attachment to the bodies. Both divisions are false. As for the first: because on this assumption, it would necessitate that the ... (incomplete)
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the createdness of the soul. The advocates of creation argue that if souls were eternal, they would in pre-eternity be either one or many. Both possibilities are shown to be false. If they were one, then upon attachment to bodies, they would either remain one (which is impossible because each body has a distinct soul) or become many (which would imply change in an eternal entity). The argument is cut off, but it continues to reject both options.

Correction: The proof actually argues the opposite: if souls were eternal, they would have to be either one or many, and both lead to absurdity. It does not assert that souls are one; it refutes both possibilities to prove creation.

Key Takeaway
The first proof for the soul's createdness uses a dilemma: if eternal, souls must be either one or many; both options are impossible, so the soul must be created.

The Impossibility of Distinction by Accidents and Matter
Objection and refutation regarding distinction by accidents and matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that parts are distinguished by non-essential accidents or matter leads to absurdities such as actually infinite parts or infinite regress.

Core Idea
Matter cannot explain individuation without generating infinite regress or circularity.

This lesson examines the fourth objection against the author's position on the nature of material parts. The opponent proposes that distinction could be due to accidental features rather than essential ones. The author systematically dismantles this proposal by showing that any attempt to ground distinction in matter leads to either an infinite number of actual parts or an endless chain of dependencies. Both outcomes are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Start by reading the Arabic passage and the translation. Notice the opponent's claim and the author's immediate rejection of the idea that matter is the basis of distinction.
The opponent tries to avoid essential differences by resorting to accidents.
2
Follow the two possibilities for matter
The author presents two cases: either the matter of each part differs in quiddity or by a separable accident. Trace each case carefully.
These are the only logical possibilities if matter is the cause of distinction.
3
Understand the first consequence
If matter differs in quiddity, distinction already exists before division, implying the body is composed of actually infinite parts. Grasp why this is impossible.
Actually infinite parts conflict with the finite nature of bodies.
4
Understand the second consequence
If matter differs by a separable accident, that accident itself would need another matter, leading to infinite regress or circularity. Appreciate the force of this argument.
The regress is vicious because it never terminates in a stable explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الرابع : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن كل واحد منها يمتاز عن الآخر بسبب عارض غير لازم للماهية ؟

قوله : ( الاختلاف في العوارض إنما يكون بسبب المادة ) . قلنا : هذا محال . لأنه لو كان كذلك ، لكان امتياز هذا الجزء عن ذلك الجزء الآخر منه لأجل الامتياز في المادة . ولو كان كذلك ، لكانت مادة هذا الجزء إما أن تكون مخالفة لمادة الجزء الآخر إما في الماهية أو في العوارض . فإن كان الأول ، كان هذا الامتياز حاصلا قبل القسمة . والحال في كل مادة غير الحال فيما يخالفها . فيلزم أن يكون الجسم مؤلفاً من أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل . وهو محال .

وإن كان الثاني : وهو أن يقال إن مادة هذا الجزء تمتاز عن مادة الجزء الآخر بسبب عارض مفارق ، فحينئذ يلزم افتقار تلك المادة إلى مادة أخرى ، ولزم إما التسلسل وإما الدور وهما محالان .
The fourth objection: Why is it not permissible to say that each one of them is distinguished from the other due to an accident that is not necessary for the quiddity?

His statement: 'Difference in accidents is only due to matter.' We said: This is impossible. Because if it were so, then the distinction of this part from that other part would be due to the distinction in matter. And if it were so, then the matter of this part would either be different from the matter of the other part either in quiddity or in accidents. If the first, this distinction would have occurred before division. And the state of each matter is different from the state of what differs from it. So it would follow that the body is composed of actually infinite parts. That is impossible.

And if the second: that it is said that the matter of this part is distinguished from the matter of the other part due to a separable accident, then the dependence of that matter on another matter would follow, and either infinite regress or circularity would follow, both impossible.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth objection: the claim that distinction could be due to a non-essential accident. The opponent then asserts that differences in accidents are due to matter. The author argues that this is impossible. If matter were the cause, then each part's matter must be distinct either in quiddity or by accident. If in quiddity, then the distinction would already exist before any division, implying that the body is composed of actually infinite parts—absurd. If by a separable accident, then that accident would itself require another matter, leading to infinite regress or circularity. Thus the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
العوارض
المادة
الأجزاء غير المتناهية
التسلسل
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Matter can be the basis of individuation without causing an infinite regress or circularity.
Correction: The author shows that any attempt to use matter to distinguish parts leads to one of two impossibilities: either actually infinite parts (if matter differs essentially) or an infinite regress/circle (if matter differs by accident).

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the matter of two parts differs in quiddity?
A) The distinction would be before division, leading to actually infinite parts
B) Infinite regress of matters
C) Circularity
D) The parts would be identical

Key Takeaway
The attempt to explain distinction by matter or accidents inevitably collapses into impossible conclusions: either actually infinite parts or infinite regress/circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reach a logical dead end like infinite regress in your own reasoning, do you humbly accept the necessary conclusion or stubbornly cling to your assumption? Will you commit to following truth wherever it leads, even if it means abandoning a comfortable belief?
?
This argument shows that without a sufficient cause, distinctions lead to absurdity. How often do you make distinctions in your life—between right and wrong, true and false—without tracing them to their ultimate foundation? Are you willing to examine the root of every belief you hold?
?
If finite bodies cannot explain their own individuation, what does that imply about the one who created them? Does this logical conclusion increase your certainty in the necessity of a single, self-subsistent First Cause? If not, what is holding you back?

The Seventh Objection on Soul Pre-Eternity and Its Refutation
Razi refutes the seventh objection against the pre-eternity of souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh objection against the pre-eternity of souls and Razi's two-part refutation.

Core Idea
If souls need distinctions, the same logic denies both pre- and post-eternity.

In this passage, Razi addresses a clever objection that turns the philosophers' own argument against them. If souls cannot be pre-eternal because they lack distinguishing features, then they cannot be post-eternal either, especially in the case of fetal souls. The response hinges on whether self-awareness alone can provide distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Focus on the parity argument: the objector claims that the same reasoning that denies pre-eternity should also deny post-eternity. Pay attention to the example of fetal souls that have no acquired knowledge or morals.
The objector is using the philosophers' own criterion against them.
2
Examine the opponent's response
The opponent argues that even fetal souls have self-awareness of their specific identity, which suffices for distinction. Consider whether this is a valid reply.
3
Analyze Razi's two refutations
First refutation: if self-awareness works for post-eternity, why not for pre-eternity? Second refutation: according to the philosophers, knowledge of essence is identical to essence; thus self-awareness entails distinct quiddities, undermining the original proof against pre-eternity.
Notice how Razi uses the opponent's own doctrines against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض السابع : إن دل ما ذكرتم على أن النفوس لا يمكن كونها أزلية، فهو بعينه يمنع من كونها أبدية، مع أن مذهبكم إنها أبدية. وبيانه هو أن النفوس المفارقة لأبدانها، كل واحدة منها، تبقى ممتازة عن الغير، بسبب ما حصل لها من العلوم والأخلاق. فنقول: نفترض الكلام في النفوس التي فارقت أبدانها حال ما كانت تلك الأبدان أجنة في بطون الأمهات، فههنا لم يحصل لتلك النفوس من العلوم والأخلاق شيء. فوجب أن تحكموا، إما بأنها تفسد وتفنى، أو بحصول الامتياز من غير سبب. وكل ذلك عندكم باطل. فإن قالوا: كل واحدة منها قد حصل لها شعور بهويتها المعينة وذاتها المخصوصة. وهذا القدر يكفي في حصول الامتياز. فنقول: هذا باطل من وجهين: الأول: أن نقول: وإن جاز هذا، فلم لا يكفي هذا القدر في حصول الامتياز في جانب الأزل؟ الثاني: إن عندكم: علم النفس بذاتها المخصوصة عين ذاتها المخصوصة. وتقولون: إن هذه الصورة: العقل والعاقل والمعقول واحد. وإذا كان كذلك، فالقول بأن كل واحدة منها تمتاز عن الأخرى، بسبب شعورها بذاتها المخصوصة، يقتضي امتياز كل واحدة منها بنفس ذاتها المخصوصة، وهذا يقتضي أن تكون ماهية كل واحدة منها، مخالفة لماهية الأخرى. وإذا ثبت هذا، فقد بطل أصل هذا الدليل. فهذا تمام الكلام في الاعتراض على هذا الدليل.
Seventh objection: If what you have mentioned proves that souls cannot be eternal a parte ante, then it likewise prevents them from being eternal a parte post, whereas your doctrine holds that they are eternal a parte post. Its explanation is that the souls separated from their bodies, each one of them, remains distinguished from others due to the knowledge and moral qualities it has acquired. So we say: Let us consider the souls that have separated from their bodies when those bodies were fetuses in the wombs of mothers. Here those souls have acquired nothing of knowledge and moral qualities. Therefore, you must either judge that they become corrupted and perish, or that they obtain distinction without a cause. And both of these are false according to you. If they say: Each one of them has obtained awareness of its specific identity and its particular essence, and this suffices for distinction. Then we say: This is false from two aspects: First: We say: Even if this is allowed, why would this not suffice for distinction in the aspect of pre-eternity? Second: According to you, the soul's knowledge of its particular essence is identical to its particular essence, and you say that this form—the intellect, the intellectual being, and the intelligible—is one. If this is so, then the statement that each one is distinguished from another by its awareness of its particular essence entails that each one is distinguished by its own particular essence, and this entails that the quiddity of each one is different from the quiddity of the other. If this is established, then the foundation of this proof is invalidated. So this completes the discussion on the objection to this proof.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its refutation. The objector argues that if the soul cannot be pre-eternal (azali) based on the previous reasoning, then by the same logic it cannot be post-eternal (abadi) either, but the opponent (the philosophers) claim the soul is abadi. The objection focuses on souls that have just separated from fetal bodies: they have no acquired knowledge or moral habits, so how are they distinguished from each other? If they are not distinguished, they would be identical, which is impossible. The objector says the opponent must either admit that such souls perish or that they are distinguished without a cause, both of which are unacceptable. The opponent responds that even such souls have self-awareness of their specific identity, which suffices for distinction. The author then refutes this in two ways: (1) If self-awareness suffices for distinction in post-eternity, why would it not also suffice in pre-eternity, thus undermining the original argument against pre-eternity? (2) According to the opponent's own doctrine, the soul's knowledge of its essence is identical to the essence itself, and the intellect, intelligent, and intelligible are one. Therefore, if distinction is by self-awareness, then each soul's essence must be unique in quiddity. But then the original proof (that souls cannot be pre-eternal because they would require distinguishing factors) is invalidated, because if quiddities are inherently different, no external factor is needed.

Key Terms
الأزل (pre-eternity)
الأبد (post-eternity)
النفس (soul)
الامتياز (distinction)
الهوية (identity)
الماهية (quiddity)
العلم (knowledge)
الشعور (awareness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection only targets post-eternity, not pre-eternity.
Correction: The objection actually uses the same criterion to challenge both directions: if distinction is required, it applies equally to pre- and post-eternity.

Checkpoint
What is the first refutation the author gives against the opponent's claim that self-awareness suffices for distinction?
A) Self-awareness is impossible for fetal souls.
B) If self-awareness suffices for post-eternity, it should also suffice for pre-eternity.
C) The opponent's doctrine of identity of knower and known is contradictory.
D) Fetal souls have no consciousness.

Key Takeaway
Razi turns the philosophers' own doctrines against them, showing that self-awareness as a basis for distinction either fails to exclude pre-eternity or forces distinct quiddities that invalidate their original proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your sense of self is often tied to what you have acquired—knowledge, character, achievements. But this lesson shows that even the philosophers recognized a deeper, intrinsic identity. If everything you have acquired were stripped away, who would you be? How does that thought challenge the way you build your identity today?
?
Razi argues that if self-awareness is identical to essence, then your core self is fixed and unchanging. Have you ever felt that your 'true self' is hidden beneath layers of what you do and have? What would it mean for you to live more from that intrinsic identity rather than from external distinctions?
?
The objector forces a dilemma: either fetal souls perish or they are distinguished without cause. The philosophers sought a cause in self-awareness. In your own life, do you seek to establish your worth through external achievements and recognition, or do you trust that your worth is given by God, independent of your acquisitions? This lesson points to a deeper cause—what is that cause for you?
