The Second Argument on Souls' Temporality and its First Objection
Second argument and first objection on soul temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the temporality of souls and how the first objection challenges the notion of an 'inoperative' unattached soul.

Core Idea
An eternal soul must either inhabit a body or be idle—but 'idle' doesn't mean inactive.

The second argument against the eternity of souls employs a logical disjunction: if souls were eternal, they would have to be either attached to another body or unattached. The argument dismisses both options, concluding that souls must be temporal. However, an objection arises questioning the claim that an unattached soul would be 'inoperative,' opening a deeper discussion about the nature of perception and activity.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the logical disjunction and the conclusion.
2
Identify the two divisions
Find the two divisions: attached to other bodies (transmigration) or unattached. Understand why each is considered false by the argument.
The first division is false due to proofs against transmigration; the second leads to being 'inoperative'.
3
Analyze the objection
The objection investigates the two premises of the argument's second division. Pay attention to the definition of 'inoperative'.
The objector questions whether 'inoperative' means devoid of perception.
4
Examine the counterexample
Consider the example of fleas and mosquitoes: they have minimal perception and pleasure yet are not considered inoperative. How does this challenge the argument?
This shows that minimal activity suffices to avoid being 'inoperative'.
5
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on how this objection weakens the second argument. Does it completely refute it, or does it only question a premise?
The objection does not refute the entire argument but forces a more precise definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : قالوا : هذه النفوس، لو كانت موجودة في الأزل ، لكانت إما أن تكون متعلقة بأبدان أخرى ، أو كانت خالية عن التعلق [ بـأبدان أخرى (۳) ] والقسمان باطلان ، فبطل القول بكونها أزلية أما القسم الأول : وهو القول بأنها كانت متعلقة(٤) بأبدان أخرى ، فهذا باطل بالدلائل المذكورة في إبطال التناسخ وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أنها كانت خالية عن التعلق بأبدان أخرى ، فعلى هذا التقدير تكون معطلة . ولا معطل في الطبيعة والاعتراض عليه : وأما قولكم () : إنها لو لم تكن متعلقة بالأبدان ، لكانت معطلة ، ولا معطل في الطبيعة . فنقول : يجب البحث في كل واحدة من هاتين المقدمتين . فما المراد من قولكم : إنها تكون معطلة ؟ إن عنيتم أنها تكون خالية عن الإدراك والشعور ، فلا نسلم ، لأنها لما كانت موصوفة بالحياة، وجب أن يحصل لها إدراك وشعور ببعض الأشياء ، ولعلها تلتذ بذلك القدر من الإدراك ، وحينئذ لا تكون معطلة . ألا ترى أنا نجد البق والبعوض وحيوانات أخرى أصغر منها بكثير موجودة . ولا نقول : بأنها لما قلت انتفاعاتها ولـذاتها ، كانت معطلة . بل نقول : إنها لما فازت بذلك القدر الحقير من الخير والمنفعة ، خرجت عن أن تكون معطلة . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك ؟
The second argument: They said: If these souls existed in eternity, they would either be attached to other bodies or be devoid of attachment [to other bodies (3)]. Both divisions are false, so the claim that they are eternal is false. As for the first division – the claim that they were attached to other bodies – this is false due to the proofs mentioned in refuting transmigration. As for the second division – that they were devoid of attachment to other bodies – then on this assumption they would be inoperative. And there is no inoperative thing in nature. And the objection to it: As for your saying: If they were not attached to bodies, they would be inoperative, and there is no inoperative thing in nature. We say: It is necessary to investigate each of these two premises. What is meant by your saying: they are inoperative? If you mean that they are devoid of perception and awareness, we do not grant it, because since they are described with life, perception and awareness of some things must occur for them. Perhaps they take pleasure in that amount of perception, and then they are not inoperative. Do you not see that we find fleas, mosquitoes, and other animals much smaller than them existing, and we do not say that because their benefits and pleasures are few, they are inoperative. Rather, we say: Since they attain that meager amount of good and benefit, they are removed from being inoperative. So why would it not be possible that the matter is so?
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument for the temporality of souls using a disjunction. The argument states that if souls were eternal, they would either be attached to other bodies (which is false due to the refutation of transmigration) or unattached to any bodies (which would make them inoperative, contradicting the principle that there is nothing inoperative in nature). The objection challenges the second premise: it questions the claim that an unattached soul would be inoperative (i.e., without perception). The objector argues that since souls are alive, they must have some perception and pleasure even without bodies, citing the example of tiny creatures that have minimal pleasure but are not considered inoperative. Thus, an unattached soul could still have some awareness and is not necessarily inoperative.

Key Terms
نفس
أزل
تعلق
تناسخ
معطل
حياة
إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An unattached soul would have no function or activity at all.
Correction: The soul, being alive, must have some perception and pleasure even without a body, so it is not necessarily inoperative.

Checkpoint
What is the second division of the argument in the second proof?
A) Souls were attached to other bodies.
B) Souls were detached from bodies.
C) Souls were created with bodies.
D) Souls were non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The second argument uses a disjunction to prove souls are temporal, but the objection refines the definition of 'inoperative' to show that an unattached soul may still have perception, thus weakening the argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul were stripped of all bodily attachments and distractions, what minimal perception or awareness would remain? Are you content with that, or do you fear that your soul has become so dependent on the body that it would be 'inoperative' without it?
?
The objection uses the example of fleas and mosquitoes—tiny creatures with minimal pleasure. How does your own soul's activity compare when it is attached to worldly pursuits? Are you satisfied with a 'meager amount of good,' or do you strive for a higher awareness even in this life?
?
This argument forces you to consider that your soul could exist without a body. If you truly believed that right now, what would you do differently today that you are putting off because you think only the body matters?

Three Objections to the Second Proof for the Soul's Eternity
Three objections to the second proof for the soul's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections raised against the second argument for the soul's temporality and why they challenge the premise that no soul is inoperative.

Core Idea
Three objections attack the claim that unattached souls must be active.

Sanusi presents three additional objections to the second proof. Each targets a different aspect: alternative attachments, inconsistency with the opponents' own beliefs, and lack of proof for a key premise. These objections force a reexamination of the argument's foundations.

Guided Path
1
Read the three objections
Read the Arabic passage containing the second, third, and fourth objections. Notice how each is phrased as a question or challenge.
2
Identify the target of each objection
Determine which premise of the original proof each objection attacks. The commentary will help you see the logical structure.
3
Reflect on cumulative impact
Consider why these objections are considered damaging. They expose alternative possibilities, contradictions, and lack of evidence.
The fourth objection is particularly strong because it questions an unproven premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الثاني : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنها قبل الأبدان ، كانت متعلقة بجزء معين من السموات ، أو بجزء من أجزاء العناصر ، وكانت تنال الخيرات واللذات بسبب تلك الآلات ؟ . الاعتراض الثالث : أليس من مذهبكم : أن نفوس الأجنة ، إذا فارقت أبدانها ، فإنها تبقى أبد الآباد من غير أن يحصل لها لذة وخير ومنفعة ؟ وذلك يبطل قولكم : إنه لا معطل في الطبيعة الاعتراض الرابع : سلمنا أن تلك النفوس كانت معطلة . فلم قلتم : إنه لا معطل في الطبيعة ؟ فإن هذه المقدمة ذكروها ، وما رأيت أحداً منهم قواها بشبهة ، فضلا عن حجة . واعلم : أنه لم يصل إلينا كلام آخر في إثبات حدوث النفوس البشرية ويمكن ذكر دليلين آخرين فيه ، إلا أنهما يوجبان القول . بحدوث الأجسام . الوجه الأول : أن نقول : لا شك أن هذه النفوس قابلة للصفات الحادثة ، فإنها تصير عالمة بأشياء ، بعد ما أنها ما كانت عالمة بها ، وتصير موصوفة [ بأخلاق ، بعد أنها ما كانت موصوفة (١) ] بها . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن قابلية الصفات الحادثة ، يجب أن يكون من لوازم ذواتها . وقابلية الصفات الحادثة حادثة ، فيلزم أن يقال : إنها لا تخلو عن الحوادث ، وكل ما لا يخلو عن
The second objection: Why is it not allowed to say that before bodies, they were attached to a specific part of the heavens or a part of the elements, and they attained goods and pleasures by means of those instruments? The third objection: Is it not from your doctrine that the souls of fetuses, when they separate from their bodies, remain forever without obtaining pleasure, good, or benefit? That refutes your saying that there is nothing inoperative in nature. The fourth objection: We grant that those souls were inoperative. Why did you say that there is nothing inoperative in nature? For they mentioned this premise, and I have not seen anyone among them strengthen it with a semblance of proof, let alone a proof. And know: No other discourse has reached us on proving the temporality of human souls, but it is possible to mention two other proofs in it, except that they necessitate the statement of the temporality of bodies. The first aspect: We say: There is no doubt that these souls are receptive to temporal attributes, for they become knowing of things after they were not knowing of them, and they become described with [character traits after they were not described (1)] with them. If this is established, we say: Receptivity to temporal attributes must be among the necessities of their essences. And receptivity to temporal attributes is temporal, so it follows that they are not free from temporal things, and whatever is not free from
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage collects three objections aimed at undermining the second argument for the soul's temporality. The second objection suggests an alternative: souls could have been attached to celestial or elemental parts before bodies, thus not inoperative. The third objection points out a contradiction in the opponent's own belief: if fetal souls after death remain forever without pleasure, then the principle 'nothing inoperative in nature' is violated. The fourth objection simply argues that the premise 'no inoperative thing in nature' is unproven and unsupported. The passage then transitions to note that no further arguments for the soul's temporality have reached the author, but two additional proofs exist, albeit they imply the temporality of bodies. The first of these proofs is then started: the soul's receptivity to temporal attributes (like knowledge and character) shows that it cannot be free from temporal attributes, and thus must be temporal itself. The text cuts off mid-sentence.

أبدان
سموات
عناصر
تناسخ
معطل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All three objections are essentially the same argument.
Correction: Each objection targets a different aspect: alternative attachments, doctrinal inconsistency, and insufficient proof.

Checkpoint
Which objection directly attacks the premise that 'there is nothing inoperative in nature' by questioning its proof?
A) Second objection
B) Third objection
C) Fourth objection
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The three objections reveal that the second proof relies on unproven premises and faces alternative scenarios.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief without verifying its foundational premises? Which premise of your own worldview might be as unproven as the 'nothing inoperative' claim, and what would it take for you to examine it honestly?
?

?
If your own beliefs (like the state of fetal souls) appear to contradict a principle you hold, do you adjust the belief or reject the principle? Are you willing to face that tension in your own faith?

Two Further Proofs for the Temporality of Souls (First Proof Begins)
Beginning of the first additional proof for the soul's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the transition to new proofs and the beginning of the first proof based on the soul's receptivity to temporal attributes.

Core Idea
The soul's acquisition of new knowledge proves it cannot be free from temporal attributes.

After presenting objections, Sanusi mentions that two additional proofs exist, though they imply the temporality of bodies. He begins the first proof: the soul is receptive to temporal attributes (like knowledge and character), and this receptivity is an essential property, meaning the soul cannot be free from temporal things, hence it is temporal itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the transition statement
Read the opening sentence where Sanusi notes that no other discourse has reached him but two proofs can be mentioned.
These proofs require accepting the temporality of bodies.
2
Understand the premise about receptivity
Identify the claim: souls become knowing and characterized after not being so. This shows they are receptive to temporal attributes.
3
Follow the logical deduction
Trace the argument: receptivity is an essential property, receptivity itself is temporal, so the soul cannot be free from temporal things, therefore it is temporal.
The passage cuts off at the conclusion, but the logic is clear.

First Argument for the Pre-Eternity of Souls
The author presents the first argument for the pre-eternity of souls and notes a prior discussion on infinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first philosophical argument for the pre-eternity of souls and why an actual infinite number of souls is considered impossible.

Core Idea
If souls were created, there would be an infinite number of them now—an impossibility.

This lesson presents the first of two proofs used by philosophers to argue that human souls are pre-eternal. The argument draws on the infinity of celestial cycles and the impossibility of an actual infinite number. Pay close attention to how the author both states and then qualifies the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the argument is structured as a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the premises of the argument
The argument has several steps: celestial cycles are infinite, bodies are infinite, souls originate with bodies, so souls would be infinite. Find each step in the text.
3
Understand the impossibility
Why is an infinite number of souls impossible? The passage states that every number admits increase and decrease, so it must be finite. Reflect on why that is a problem.
This is the key crux of the argument.
4
Note the author's qualification
The author adds a note that this point about finite numbers has been discussed before. What does this tell you about the author's attitude toward the argument?
The author is not presenting this as a settled proof but as a known argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بقدم النفوس بوجهين:
الحجة الأولى: إنه ثبت في الفلسفة: أن الأدوار الفلكية غير متناهية فالأبدان البشرية غير متناهية. فلو كانت النفوس البشرية حادثة، لكان حدوثها، لأجل أن حدوث البدن شرط في فيضانها عن العلل العالية. فلما كانت الأبدان غير متناهية، وجب أن تكون النفوس البشرية غير متناهية. لكنه ثبت أن النفوس لا تقبل الفساد، فوجب أن يوجد الآن عدد لا نهاية له من النفوس. وذلك محال لأن كل عدد فإنه يحتمل الزيادة والنقصان وكل ما كان كذلك، فهو متناه.
واعلم: أن قولنا: إن كل عدد يحتمل الزيادة والنقصان، فإنه يجب أن يكون متناهيا: تقدم، بحثنا عنها في مسألة وتناهي الأبعاد، فلا فائدة في الإعادة.
And those who say the souls are pre-eternal (qidam) argued with two proofs: The first proof: It is established in philosophy that the celestial cycles (adwar falakiyya) are infinite, so human bodies are infinite. If human souls were originated (haditha), their origination would be because the origination of the body is a condition for their emanation (fayadan) from the high causes. So since bodies are infinite, it would be necessary that human souls be infinite. But it is established that souls do not accept corruption (fasad), so it would be necessary that there now exist an infinite number of souls. That is impossible because every number admits increase and decrease, and whatever is so is finite. And know: that our statement that every number that admits increase and decrease must be finite has been discussed before in the issue of the finitude of dimensions, so there is no benefit in repeating.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the pre-eternity of souls. The proponent claims: celestial cycles are infinite, so human bodies (which are generated in each cycle) are infinite. Souls are originated simultaneously with bodies, so infinite bodies imply infinite souls. Since souls are immortal, there must now be an infinite number of souls. But an infinite number is impossible because numbers are inherently finite (they can increase or decrease). Therefore, souls cannot be originated; they must be pre-eternal. The author then adds a note: the premise that every number admitting increase and decrease is finite has already been discussed in the context of the finitude of dimensions, so he does not repeat that discussion here.

Key Terms
النفوس (souls)
قدم (pre-eternity)
أدوار فلكية (celestial cycles)
فيضان (emanation)
علل عالية (high causes)
عدد لا نهاية له (infinite number)
متناه (finite)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument directly proves that souls are pre-eternal.
Correction: The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: it shows the impossibility of souls being originated, thus they must be pre-eternal. The author also notes a prior discussion on the finitude of numbers, showing this is not a settled proof.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why must souls be pre-eternal?
A) Because souls are simple
B) Because if souls were originated, there would be an infinite number of souls now
C) Because bodies are infinite
D) Because celestial cycles are finite

Key Takeaway
The first argument for the pre-eternity of souls relies on the impossibility of an actual infinite number of souls, given infinite celestial cycles.

?

?
The argument says an infinite number of souls is impossible, so your soul is one finite, unique existence. Does that uniqueness weigh on you? How would you live today if you truly believed your soul is not a product of time?
?
If souls are pre-eternal, then your existence has no beginning. Does that thought comfort you or unsettle you? What would change in your daily choices if you fully internalized that your soul is not bound by time?

Ibn Sina's First Proof Against Transmigration
Ibn Sina presents his first proof against transmigration via a causal argument based on the soul's origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's first argument against transmigration and why a single body cannot have two souls.

Core Idea
One body, one soul – that simple fact disproves transmigration.

Ibn Sina presents a tight logical argument against the doctrine of transmigration (al-tanasukh). He starts with two established premises about the soul's creation and its cause, then shows how transmigration would lead to an impossible consequence. This lesson unpacks his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read Ibn Sina's proof once to get the overall flow. Then read the translation to ensure understanding.
Note the causal structure: eternal cause → originated condition → effect.
2
Identify the two premises
What two facts does Ibn Sina assert as established? Find them in the text: souls are originated (haditha) and their cause is eternal (qadima).
These are the foundation of the argument.
3
Understand the causal logic
Why can an eternal cause only produce an effect through an originated condition? Because the effect is temporal, so there must be a temporal condition that triggers the causation. Here, that condition is the origination of a body.
This is a key principle in Ibn Sina's metaphysics.
4
See the absurd consequence
If transmigration were true, a body that originated would already have a newly emanated soul, and also a soul from a previous body would attach to it. That would mean one body has two souls.
Ibn Sina says this is impossible ('this is impossible').
5
Reflect on the impossibility
Everyone experiences themselves as having one soul, not two. So the consequence contradicts direct human experience, proving transmigration false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الشيخ الرئيس ( أبو علي بن سينا ، على فساد القول بالتناسخ بدليلين (۳) : الحجة الأولى : قال : إنه ثبت : أن النفوس البشرية حادثة ، وثبت أن علتها قديمة ، والموجود القديم لا يكون علة للشيء المحدث ، إلا إذا كان تأثيره فيه ، موقوفاً على شرط حادث وذلك الشرط الحادث وهو حدوث البدن . فعلى هذا إذا حدث بدن ، وجب أن يفيض عن واجب الصور نفس ، فلو تعلق بذلك البدن نفس على سبيل التناسخ وتعلقت به هذه النفوس الحادثة أيضاً ، لزم أن يتعلق بذلك البدن الواحد نفسان ، وهذا محال . لان كل أحد يجد نفسه نفساً واحدة لا نفسين.
The Chief Master (Abu Ali ibn Sina) argued against the corruption of the doctrine of transmigration with two proofs: The first proof: He said: It is established that human souls are originated (haditha), and it is established that their cause is eternal (qadima). And the eternal existent cannot be a cause for an originated thing unless its effect in it is dependent upon an originated condition, and that originated condition is the origination of the body. So accordingly, when a body originates, a soul must emanate from the Necessary of Forms. So if that body were attached to a soul by way of transmigration and also these originated souls were attached to it, it would necessitate that one single body be attached to two souls, and this is impossible. For everyone finds himself as one soul, not two souls.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's first proof against the doctrine of transmigration (al-tanasukh). The proof is structured as a reductio ad absurdum. First, Ibn Sina asserts two premises: (1) human souls are originated (haditha), i.e., they come into existence at some time; (2) the cause of souls is eternal (qadima). He then argues that an eternal cause cannot produce an effect except through an originated condition. In this case, the originated condition is the origination of a body. Therefore, whenever a body originates, a new soul must emanate from the Necessary Being (واجب الصور). Now, if that same body were also the carrier of a soul from transmigration, then the body would have two souls simultaneously. But this is impossible because every person experiences himself as having one soul, not two. Hence, transmigration cannot occur.

Key Terms
النفوس البشرية
حادثة
علة قديمة
واجب الصور
التناسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Transmigration could allow a soul to inhabit a new body without conflict because the soul is eternal.
Correction: Ibn Sina's argument assumes souls are originated, not eternal. Every new body already receives a newly created soul by necessity, so a transmigrating soul would cause two souls in one body, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise of Ibn Sina's first proof against transmigration?
A) The soul is eternal.
B) Every body originates from a previous body.
C) A body can have only one soul.
D) Souls are immaterial.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's first proof shows that because every new body already receives a newly created soul, transmigration would lead to the impossible situation of one body having two souls.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience yourself as one soul, not two. Does this certainty extend to your conviction that your soul is uniquely created for this specific body? How does that affect your understanding of your life's purpose?
?
If every body receives a new soul from the Necessary of Forms, then your soul is directly given to you at your body's creation. Does this make you feel personally accountable to the Giver? What would change if you lived with that awareness every moment?
?
Ibn Sina argues that transmigration is impossible because it would violate the soul-body unity you know in yourself. Are there areas in your life where you assume you can 'transfer' your responsibility or identity elsewhere? How does this proof demand that you own your soul's actions?

The First Objection: Circular Reasoning
Razi presents the first objection based on circular reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi identifies circular reasoning in Ibn Sina's proof against transmigration and why circularity invalidates the argument.

Core Idea
If two arguments depend on each other, neither can stand.

In his critique of Ibn Sina's first proof against transmigration, Razi exposes a logical flaw: Ibn Sina's argument for the soul's origination relies on disproving transmigration, while his argument against transmigration relies on the soul's origination. This creates a vicious circle that undermines the entire proof. This lesson focuses on the first objection and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the objector first denies the premise that the soul is originated.
The objection begins with 'We do not accept that the soul is originated.'
2
Trace the circular dependency
Identify two key claims: the proof for the soul's origination depends on refuting transmigration, and the proof against transmigration depends on the soul's origination. See how they form a cycle.
This is the core of the circular reasoning (al-dawr).
3
Understand why circularity is fatal
Reflect on why circular arguments are logically invalid – they assume what they are trying to prove, so no conclusion is established.
Remember that circular reasoning is a formal fallacy in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض عليه من وجوه : الأول : لا نسلم أن النفس حادثة . وذلك لأن الدليل الذي ذكرتم في إثبات حدوث النفس ، كان موقوفاً على إبطال التناسخ ، ثم إنكم بنيتم دليلكم في إبطال التناسخ على حدوث النفس . فيقع الدور ، وهو باطل . والاعتراض الثاني : سلمنا : أن النفس حادثة ، فلم قلتم : إن المؤثر في وجود النفس علة موجبة ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثر فيها فاعلا مختاراً ، فيخلق تلك النفوس والأرواح في أي وقت شاء وأراد؟ وعلى هذا التقدير [فلا يمكنكم أن
And the objection against it from several aspects: The first: We do not accept that the soul is originated. That is because the proof which you mentioned in establishing the origination of the soul was dependent upon refuting transmigration; then you built your proof in refuting transmigration upon the origination of the soul. So a circular argument occurs, and it is invalid. And the second objection: We accept that the soul is originated; but why do you say that the effector in the existence of the soul is a necessitating cause? And why is it not permissible that the effector in it be a free agent, creating those souls and spirits at whatever time He wishes and wills? And on this estimation, [you cannot... (incomplete)]
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents two objections to Ibn Sina's first proof against transmigration. The first objection attacks the premise that the soul is originated. The objector argues that Ibn Sina's proof for the origination of the soul itself relied on rejecting transmigration, while his proof against transmigration relied on the origination of the soul. This results in circular reasoning (al-dawr), which is invalid. The second objection concedes that the soul is originated but questions the nature of the cause. Ibn Sina assumed that the cause is a necessitating cause (illa mujiba) that inevitably produces an effect when the condition (the body) is present. The objector suggests that instead, the cause could be a free agent (fa'il mukhtar) who creates souls whenever He wishes, not necessarily with every body. Therefore, it is not required that a new soul emanate with every body, and thus the argument that two souls would conflict fails. The text cuts off before completing the consequence.

Key Terms
حدوث النفس
إبطال التناسخ
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that circular reasoning is acceptable if each step seems plausible.
Correction: Circular reasoning is a logical fallacy because it assumes the conclusion within the premises, providing no independent support.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection to Ibn Sina's proof regarding the soul's origination?
A) The soul is eternal.
B) The proof is circular.
C) The body is not the only condition.
D) The soul is material.

Key Takeaway
Circular reasoning invalidates a proof because it assumes what it aims to prove, offering no independent justification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief in your faith that, upon honest examination, turned out to rely on circular reasoning—where you assumed the very thing you were trying to prove? What step can you take today to break that circle and seek a solid foundation?
?
This passage shows a philosopher's argument dismantled by pointing out its hidden assumption. Is there an area in your life—your habits, your thoughts, your worship—where you have been building on an assumption you never questioned? Will you let this critique unsettle you enough to investigate?
?
Razi does not reject the soul's origination outright; he exposes flawed reasoning. When was the last time you allowed someone to challenge your thinking in a way that forced you to rebuild from the ground up? Are you humble enough to accept that your cherished beliefs might be supported by a fragile circle, not solid truth?

The Second Objection: Free Agency vs. Necessitating Cause
Razi presents the second objection based on free agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's objection that the cause of the soul could be a free agent rather than a necessitating cause, undermining Ibn Sina's proof against transmigration.

Core Idea
If God creates souls freely, not every body necessarily receives a new soul.

In the second objection, Razi grants the origination of the soul but challenges the nature of its cause. Ibn Sina assumed a necessitating cause that inevitably produces a soul when the body is ready. Razi counters that the cause could be a free agent who creates souls at will. This breaks the necessary link between body and soul, weakening the argument against transmigration. Note: the original text cuts off, but the main point is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note that the objector first accepts the soul's origination but then questions the type of cause.
The text ends mid-sentence, but the core challenge is complete.
2
Differentiate cause types
Understand the distinction between a necessitating cause (illa mujiba) that operates inevitably and a free agent (fa'il mukhtar) that acts by choice.
This distinction is crucial in Islamic theology.
3
Consider the implications
If God is a free agent, He can create souls whenever He wishes, not necessarily when a body is formed. This undermines the argument that two souls cannot coexist in one body.
The broken text likely would have completed the implication.

Three Categories of Entities Subject to Corruption
The author classifies three kinds of entities: accidents, forms, and immaterial possible beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of entities that are subject to corruption or are possible, and why the author distinguishes them based on composition from matter and form.

Core Idea
Corruption and possibility are not uniform—things differ based on their composition.

In this passage, the author presents a foundational classification in natural philosophy. He separates entities into three kinds based on whether they are subject to corruption and whether they are composed of matter and form. This classification is crucial for understanding later discussions on the nature of change and permanence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own structure come through.
Notice the use of enumeration: 'One of them... and the second... and the third...'
2
Identify the three categories
List the three categories: accidents, forms, and the third group (intellects, celestial souls, rational souls, prime matter). Note the defining feature of each.
The first two are said to be 'receptive to non-existence' (i.e., corruptible), while the third are 'possible entities'.
3
Understand the reasoning for forms
The author gives a brief argument for why forms are not composed of matter and form: otherwise an infinite regress would result. Consider why this argument is necessary.
This links to the broader debate about composition and corruption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إحداها : الأعراض . مثل : السواد والبياض . فإنها قابلة للعدم (١) وليست مركبة من المادة والصورة . وثانيها : الصورة قابلة للعدم . وليست مركبة من المادة والصورة (٢) وإلا لزم أن تحصل للصورة صورة أخرى إلى غير نهاية . وثالثها : إن العقول والنفوس الفلكية ، والنفوس الناطقة ، والهيولى : كلها ممكنات ، وليس لشيء منها مادة
One of them: accidents, such as blackness and whiteness. Indeed, they are receptive to non‑existence (1) and are not composed of matter and form. And the second: form is receptive to non‑existence and is not composed of matter and form (2); otherwise it would necessitate that form acquire another form ad infinitum. And the third: indeed, the intellects, the celestial souls, the rational souls, and prime matter (hayūlā) are all possible entities, and none of them has matter.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a classification. The author enumerates three types of entities that are either subject to corruption (the first two) or are possible beings (the third). He begins with accidents such as blackness and whiteness, stating that they are receptive to non-existence and are not composed of matter and form. Second, he mentions form itself, which is also receptive to non-existence and not composed of matter and form; otherwise there would be an infinite regress of forms. Third, he lists intellects, celestial souls, rational souls, and prime matter (hayūlā) as all being possible but having no matter. This classification sets up the subsequent discussion on whether corruption necessarily implies composition from matter and form.

Key Terms
أعراض (aʿrāḍ, accidents)
صورة (ṣūra, form)
عقول (ʿuqūl, intellects)
نفوس فلكية (nufūs falakiyya, celestial souls)
نفوس ناطقة (nufūs nāṭiqa, rational souls)
هيولى (hayūlā, prime matter)
إمكان (imkān, potentiality/contingency)
مادة (mādda, matter)
عدم (ʿadam, non-existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents and forms have no substrate at all because they are not composed of matter and form.
Correction: The text only states they are not composed of matter and form, but they still inhere in a substrate (matter) as will be explained later. The passage does not deny their existence in a subject.

Checkpoint

A) Accidents only
B) Forms only
C) Intellects and souls only
D) All three categories

Key Takeaway
The classification of entities into accidents, forms, and immaterial possible beings shows that corruption and possibility are not solely determined by composition from matter and form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson classifies you (as a human) as having a rational soul (نفس ناطقة) which is immaterial and possible. If your soul is not made of matter, what does that mean for your attachment to material possessions and your body? Are you living as if your soul will perish with your body? What would change today if you truly believed your soul is immaterial and awaiting a perpetual existence?

How can you shift your self‑definition to what is more lasting?
?
The passage says form is not composed of matter and form, otherwise there would be infinite regress. Consider your own intellectual pursuits: do you ever fall into infinite regress by seeking endless justifications or definitions, never settling on a first principle? Where in your life do you need to accept a foundational truth instead of demanding further proof?

Answer to the First Objection: The Locus of Potentialities
Answer to the first objection: forms and accidents have matter as locus of potentialities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why forms and accidents have matter as the locus of their potentialities, and how the author distinguishes corruptible things from eternal entities.

Core Idea
Potentialities of corruptible things reside in matter, not in themselves.

In this passage, the author directly answers an objection challenging his earlier examples. He clarifies that forms and accidents inhere in matter, so the potentialities (imkānāt) for change and corruption are grounded in that matter. The discussion then extends to eternal beings, where potentialities subsist in themselves. This lesson walks through his reasoning and shows why the distinction is crucial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument flow through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: objection answered, then example, then principle, then distinction.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms forms (ṣuwar), accidents (aʿrāḍ), matter (mādda), potentialities (imkānāt), and locus (maḥall). Understand how they relate.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Focus on the pitcher example
The author uses the example of a pitcher made from clay. Explain why the potentiality for the pitcher's form resides in the clay, not in the pitcher itself.
This illustrates the core principle.
4
Understand the principle for corruptible things
The author states: 'Every thing that admits corruption must have matter.' Ask yourself why this follows from the example.
Corruption implies loss of form, so the potentiality for that loss must be in the substrate.
5
Grasp the distinction for eternal things
The author separates eternal entities (intellects, celestial souls, prime matter) which have potentialities subsisting in themselves. Compare this to corruptible things.
This distinction resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب: أما السؤال الأول: وهو النقيض بالصور المذكورة. فجوابه: أن نقول: الصور والأعراض قائمة بالمواد، فيكون محل إمكاناتها هو تلك المادة. ألا ترى أن صورة الكوز، قائمة بالطين فإنك تحكم بأن هذا الطين يمكن اتخاذ الكوز (1) منه. فثبت: أن محل إمكانات هذه الصور والأعراض تلك المواد. وهذا يدل على صحة قولنا: إن كل ما يقبل الفساد، فلا بد له من مادة. وأما إمكانات العقول والنفوس الفلكية والهيولى. فنقول: إن هذه الأشياء باقية أبداً وأزلاً، فلا يمنع قيام (2) إمكاناتها بها. أما الشيء إذا عدم بعد وجوده فإمكان عدمه، عند عدمه، وهو غير حاصل عند عدمه فيمتنع أن يكون إمكانه قائماً به، فوجب قيامه بغيره. فظهر الفرق حاصل
And the answer: As for the first question, which is the objection by means of the mentioned forms. Its answer is that we say: Forms and accidents inhere in matters, so the locus of their potentialities (imkānāt) is that matter. Do you not see that the form of a pitcher inheres in clay, for you judge that this clay can be made into a pitcher (1)? So it is established that the locus of the potentialities of these forms and accidents is those matters. And this indicates the correctness of our statement: Every thing that admits corruption must have matter. As for the potentialities of the intellects, celestial souls, and prime matter, we say: These things are eternal and everlasting, so the subsistence (2) of their potentialities in them is not prevented. As for a thing that ceases to exist after its existence, its potentiality for non‑existence at the time of its non‑existence is not actual, so it is impossible for its potentiality to subsist in it, so its subsistence in something else is necessary. So the difference is apparent.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the first objection, which had challenged the given examples. The author begins by stating the answer. He argues that forms and accidents inhere in matter, so the locus of their potentialities is that matter. He gives an example: the form of a pitcher inheres in clay; we say that clay can be made into a pitcher. Thus the potentiality resides in the clay (matter). This proves the principle that whatever admits corruption must have matter. He then distinguishes two cases: eternal things like intellects, celestial souls, and prime matter itself – because they are eternal, their potentialities subsist in themselves. For a thing that ceases to exist, its potentiality for non‑existence cannot subsist in it when it no longer exists, so that potentiality must subsist in something else, namely matter. Therefore the difference between corruptible and eternal things is clear.

Key Terms
صور (ṣuwar, forms)
أعراض (aʿrāḍ, accidents)
إمكانات (imkānāt, potentialities)
محل (maḥall, locus)
هيولى (hayūlā, prime matter)
فساد (fasād, corruption)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the author is saying all things that admit corruption have matter, without exception.
Correction: The author explicitly distinguishes eternal things (intellects, celestial souls, prime matter) which do not need an external substrate for their potentialities.

Checkpoint

A) The forms themselves
B) The accidents themselves
C) Matter
D) The intellect

Key Takeaway
The potentiality for corruption in a thing must reside in its matter, not in the thing itself, whereas eternal things sustain their own potentialities.

The Nature of Potentiality: Objection and Answer
The author refutes the objection that potentiality does not require a substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why potentiality (imkān) requires a substrate regardless of whether it is considered an existing attribute or not.

Core Idea
Potentiality needs a subject even if it's not an existing attribute.

This lesson presents a key objection from the opponent who argues that potentiality is not an existing attribute and thus does not need a locus. The author responds that the proof does not depend on potentiality being existent; even as a non‑existent concept, it must inhere in something. This subtle argument defends the necessity of prime matter (hayūlā) for corruptible beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: objection then answer.
The passage contains a clear objection and a detailed response.
2
Identify the objection
The opponent claims that potentiality is not an existing attribute, so it doesn't need a locus.
3
Follow the author's reasoning
The author argues that even if potentiality is non‑existent, its concept is not self‑subsistent; it must belong to something.
Pay attention to the phrase 'concept not independent by itself'.
4
Understand the conclusion
Therefore, if the soul were corruptible, it would be composed of prime matter and form.
This conclusion follows from the necessity of a substrate.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
This argument upholds the necessity of a substrate for change and defends the traditional view of composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني: لا نسلم أن قبول الفساد، لا بد له من محل، فإن هذا بناء على أن الإمكان والقبول صفة موجودة، ولا بد لها من محل. وقد سبق بيان الإمكان لا يمكن أن يكون صفة موجودة.
وأما السؤال الثاني: وهو قوله: (إن هذا الكلام مبني على أن الإمكان صفة موجودة مفتقرة إلى المحل، فنقول: لا حاجة في تقرير هذا الدليل إلى إثبات أن الإمكان صفة موجودة. بل نقول: إمكان الفساد، سواء كان موجوداً أو معدوماً، فهو حاصل عند حصول الفساد والمفهوم من هذا الإمكان، سواء كان موجوداً أو معدوماً، مفهوم غير مستقل بنفسه، فلا بد من فرض شيء آخر يحكم العقل بجعل ذلك الإمكان صفة له، وذلك الشيء لا بد وأن يكون حاصلا عند حصول ذلك الإمكان، ولما امتنع (3) أن يكون الذي عدم باقياً عند عدمه، وثبت: أن إمكان الفساد حاصل عند حصول ذلك الفساد، وجب القطع بأن محل ذلك الإمكان شيء آخر. والذي هو محل إمكان ذلك الشيء هو الهيولي. فثبت: أن النفس لو كانت قابلة للفساد، فسواء قلنا: إن هذا القبول صفة موجودة، أو معدومة. فإنه يجب كون النفس مركبة من الهيولى والصورة.
The second question: We do not grant that the reception of corruption requires a locus, for this is based on the claim that potentiality (imkān) and reception (qubūl) are existing attributes and require a locus. And it has already been explained that potentiality cannot be an existing attribute.
And as for the second question, which is his statement: 'This speech is based on the claim that potentiality is an existing attribute in need of a locus.' We say: There is no need in establishing this proof to prove that potentiality is an existing attribute. Rather we say: The potentiality for corruption, whether it exists or is non‑existent, is actual at the occurrence of corruption. And the concept of this potentiality, whether it exists or is non‑existent, is a concept not independent by itself. So there must be a supposition of something else that the intellect judges to make that potentiality an attribute of it. And that thing must be actual at the occurrence of that potentiality. And since it is impossible (3) that which ceased to exist remains at its non‑existence, and it is established that the potentiality for corruption is actual at the occurrence of that corruption, it is necessary to decide that the locus of that potentiality is something else. And that which is the locus of the potentiality of that thing is prime matter (hayūlā). So it is established that if the soul were receptive to corruption, whether we say this reception is an existing attribute or a non‑existent one, then the soul must be composed of prime matter and form.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its answer. The objector denies that reception of corruption requires a locus, arguing that potentiality (imkān) is not an existing attribute and therefore does not need a subject. The author responds that the proof does not depend on potentiality being an existing attribute. Whether existent or non‑existent, the concept of potentiality is not self‑subsistent; it requires something else to be its subject. The intellect judges that potentiality is an attribute of something. That subject must exist when the potentiality is realized. Since the corrupted thing no longer exists at the time of corruption, its potentiality for corruption cannot inhere in it; it must inhere in something else, namely prime matter (hayūlā). Therefore, if the soul were corruptible, it would be composed of prime matter and form, regardless of whether reception is considered an existing attribute or not.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkān, potentiality)
قبول (qubūl, reception)
صفة موجودة (ṣifa mawjūda, existing attribute)
محل (maḥall, locus)
هيولى (hayūlā, prime matter)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If potentiality is not an existing attribute, it does not need a subject.
Correction: The author argues that even if potentiality is non‑existent, its concept is not independent and therefore requires a substrate.

Checkpoint

A) Potentiality must be an existing attribute to require a locus
B) Potentiality is self‑subsistent and needs no locus
C) Whether existent or non‑existent, potentiality is a concept that requires a subject
D) Potentiality is a divine attribute that transcends matter

Key Takeaway
Potentiality, whether existent or non‑existent, is not self‑subsistent and therefore requires a substrate, which in cases of corruption is prime matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author argues that the corrupted thing cannot be the subject of its own potentiality at the moment of corruption. Consider a time when you lost something you valued. Did you still feel potential for change in that loss? Where did that potential reside? How does this lesson invite you to entrust your potentials to the Eternal rather than to the ephemeral?
?
If the soul were corruptible, it would be composite. This argument strengthens the case for the soul's immateriality and immortality. Does this conviction shape how you spend your time today? What one concrete change can you make this week to prioritize your soul's eternal welfare over temporary gains?

Third Objection: Origination Implies Composition
Objection and partial answer on origination and composition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the soul's immateriality based on the analogy with corruption, and the beginning of the response that identifies the body as the required matter.

Core Idea
Origination, like corruption, seems to imply material composition, but the answer points to the body as the substrate.

The opponent presses further: if souls are originated, they must share the same structure as other originated things. Just as potential for corruption implies matter and form, potential for origination should do the same. This would demolish the argument for the soul's immateriality. Let's follow the logic and see where the answer begins.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note that the answer is cut off.
The objection is complete; the answer only starts.
2
Analyze the analogy
Identify the two potentialities: one for corruption, one for origination. The objector claims they are parallel.
If one implies composition, so should the other.
3
Examine the implication
Understand why this would invalidate the proof of the soul's immateriality.
The soul would be composite, not simple.
4
Explore the partial answer
The answer begins by granting that the potentiality needs matter, but identifies that matter as the body, not prime matter.
The answer is incomplete; we don't see how it fully resolves.
5
Reflect on the incompleteness
Consider why it's important not to speculate beyond the given text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث: النفوس عندكم حادثة. وكما أن كل ما يقبل الفساد، فإن إمكان فساده حاصل قبل (3) وجوده، فكذلك ما يكون حادثاً، يكون إمكان حدوثه حاصلاً قبل وجوده، فإن لزم من أحد الإمكانين كونه مركباً من المادة والصورة، لزم من الإمكان الثاني، كونه كذلك، فكان يجب أن يلزم من كون النفس محدثة، كونها مركبة من الهيولى والصورة. وحينئذ يبطل دليلكم.
وأما السؤال الثالث: وهو قوله: (إذا كانت النفس محدثة، فالإمكان السابق على حدوثها، وجب أن يفتقر إلى المادة، فجوابه: إن ذلك الإمكان مفتقر إلى مادة، ومادته هي البدن، والبدن باق حال ذلك الحدوث، فلم
The third question: The souls, according to you, are originated (ḥāditha). And just as every thing that admits corruption, its potentiality for corruption is actual before (3) its existence, similarly whatever is originated has its potentiality for origination actual before its existence. So if from one of the two potentialities it follows that it is composed of matter and form, then from the second potentiality it follows that it is likewise. So it would be necessary to conclude from the soul being originated that it is composed of prime matter and form. And then your proof is invalidated.
And as for the third question, which is his statement: 'If the soul is originated, then the potentiality prior to its origination must be in need of matter.' Its answer: That potentiality is in need of matter, and its matter is the body, and the body remains at the time of that origination, so it did not...
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and the beginning of its answer. The objector argues that the soul is originated (ḥāditha). He draws an analogy: just as with corruptible things the potentiality for corruption exists before the thing exists, so with originated things the potentiality for origination exists before origination. If one potentiality implies composition from matter and form (as argued for corruption), then the other should also imply composition, leading to the conclusion that the soul is composed of prime matter and form, which would invalidate the opponent's proof. The answer begins: the potentiality for origination does need matter, but its matter is the body, which exists at the time of origination. The answer cuts off at 'so it did not…', leaving the rest incomplete.

حادثة (ḥāditha, originated)
إمكان حدوث (imkān ḥudūth, potentiality of origination)

Key Takeaway
The third objection uses a parity argument: if corruption implies matter and form, so should origination, but the answer starts by distinguishing the needed matter as the body.

Why Possibilities of Corruption Inhere in Matter
Arguing that possibilities of corruptibles must inhere in matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of corruption must inhere in a material substrate and how this principle distinguishes perishable from eternal entities.

Core Idea
The possibility of a thing's corruption needs a holder in matter when the thing is gone.

Sanusi argues that forms and purposes inhere in matter, so their possibilities must also inhere in matter. He uses the example of a pitcher and clay to illustrate this, then addresses the special case of eternal beings and distinguishes corruptible things. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
2
Identify the two categories
Notice how the passage divides things into eternal (intellects, souls, prime matter) and perishable (things that cease to exist). Each has a different relationship to possibility.
3
Examine the pitcher-clay example
The form of a pitcher exists in clay, so the possibility of making a pitcher from that clay inheres in the clay. What does this show about the locus of possibility?
The example establishes that possibility can inhere in a material substrate.
4
Understand the argument for corruptibles
The passage states that when a thing no longer exists, the possibility of its non-existence cannot be in it (since it is absent). Therefore, that possibility must inhere in something else – namely, matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصور والأغراض قائمة بالمواد ، فيكون محل إمكاناتها هو تلك المادة . ألا ترى أن صورة الكوز ، قائمة بالطين فإنك تحكم بأن هذا الطين يمكن اتخاذ الكوز منه . فثبت : أن محل إمكانات هذه الصور والأعراض تلك المواد . وهذا يدل على صحة قولنا : إن كل ما يقبل الفساد، فلا بد له من مادة . وأما إمكانات العقول والنفوس الفلكية والهيولى . فنقول : إن هذه الأشياء باقية أبداً وأزلاً ، فلا يمنع قيام إمكاناتها بها . أما الشيء إذا عدم بعد وجوده فإمكان عدمه ، عند عدمه ، وهو غير حاصل عند عدمه فيمتنع أن يكون إمكانه قائماً به ، فوجب قیامه بغيره . فظهر الفرق حاصل
Forms and purposes inhere in matter, so the locus of their possibilities is that matter. Do you not see that the form of a pitcher inheres in clay, for you judge that from this clay a pitcher can be taken? So it is established that the locus of the possibilities of these forms and accidents is those materials. And this indicates the correctness of our statement: Everything that accepts corruption must have a matter. As for the possibilities of intellects, celestial souls, and prime matter (hayula), we say: These things are everlasting and eternal, so there is nothing preventing the inherence of their possibilities in them. As for a thing that ceases to exist after its existence, the possibility of its non-existence at the time of its non-existence is not present at its non-existence, so it is impossible that its possibility inheres in it, so its inherence in something else is necessitated. So the difference appears and is established.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents a key kalam argument that anything subject to corruption must have a material substrate. The author begins by stating that forms and purposes (الصور والأغراض) inhere in matter, so the locus (محل) of their possibilities is that matter. He illustrates with the example of a pitcher's form existing in clay: you judge that a pitcher can be made from this clay. Thus, the locus of possibilities of these forms and accidents is those materials. This supports the general principle: everything that accepts corruption must have a matter. Next, he addresses the possibilities of eternal entities—intellects, celestial souls, and prime matter (هيولى)—which are everlasting, so their possibilities can inhere in themselves. In contrast, for a thing that ceases to exist, the possibility of its non-existence at the time of non-existence is not actually present (حاصل) in it, so it cannot inhere in the thing itself; rather, it must inhere in something else—namely, matter. The difference is thus clarified: perishable things require an external material substrate for their possibility of corruption, while eternal entities do not.

Key Terms
إمكان
مادة
فساد
هيولى
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the possibility of a thing's corruption always inheres in the thing itself, even after it ceases to exist.
Correction: The passage explains that when the thing no longer exists, the possibility of its non-existence cannot be present in it, so it must inhere in matter.

Checkpoint

A) Because it already exists.
B) Because at the time of its non-existence, that possibility is not present in it.
C) Because possibilities are eternal.
D) Because the thing is composed of matter and form.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of corruption in perishable things must inhere in matter, not in the thing itself, because when the thing ceases to exist, the possibility cannot remain in it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the possibility of your corruption does not inhere in you but in the matter that composes your body, what does that mean for your attachment to your physical existence? Will you invest your hope in what is eternal in you?

You know that the possibility of your own non-existence is not in you at the moment of death. Since it is in something else (the matter), how does that change your view of death—does it free you to seek what truly lasts?

First Argument: Intuitive Assignment of Sensations to Organs
First general argument against soul perceiving particulars, with objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first general argument against the soul perceiving particulars, which relies on the intuitive knowledge that each sensory modality is inherently linked to a specific organ, and the initial objection that the soul uses these organs as instruments.

Core Idea
Intuition assigns each sense to a specific organ; if the soul perceived directly, those assignments would be invalid.

This passage presents the first of four general arguments that the rational soul does not perceive particulars. The argument appeals to common intuitive knowledge: everyone knows vision occurs in the eye, hearing in the ear, and so on. If the soul itself were the perceiver, these specific assignments to organs would be false. An objection is then raised suggesting that the soul uses organs as instruments, but the author's reply is cut off. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the flow of the argument, even though some parts are garbled.
The beginning is heavily garbled, but the core argument becomes clear mid-passage.
2
Identify Avicenna's view
Note that the author attributes to Avicenna the position that the rational soul only perceives universals, while particular perceptions are distributed over the senses.
3
Understand the first argument from intuition
The argument states that by primordial intuition (badīha), people know that seeing is in the eye, hearing in the ear, etc. If the soul were the perceiver, these assignments would be meaningless.
4
Recognize the objection
An opponent objects: why not say the perceiving power is not resident in the organs but uses them as instruments, so when the soul turns to the eye it sees?
5
Note the incomplete reply
The author begins to reply ('We said: The soul, if it turns its attention to...') but the text cuts off. Consider what the reply might be.
The reply likely involves a critique of the instrumental model.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث سكر
في
المباشرة
بيان أن النفيس الداخلة معركة الكليات والجينيات معا. وأتحاميل لجميع الإتصال بنفسها، وإن كانت بالكرة موقوفة على استعمال الكليات
ظاهر كلام الشيخ الرئيس : مشعر بأن النفس الناطقة ، لا تقوى إلا على إدراك الكليات والمجردات وأما الإدراكات الجزئية ، فإنها موزعة على الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة
واحتجوا على أن النفس لا تدرك الجزئيات بوجوه عامة ووجوه خاصة
أما الوجوه العامة فأربعة : الأول : إن العقلاء ببدائة عقولهم يعلمون أن رؤية المرئيات حاصلة في البصر لا غيره ، وسماع الأصوات حاصل في الأذن لا في غيرها ، بل نقول : كما أن البديهة حاكمة بأن اللسان غير مبصر ، والعين غير ذائق ، فهي أيضاً حاكمة بأن اللسان ذائق ، والعين مبصرة . ولو قلنا : إن الموصوف بإدراك هذه المحسوسات هو النفس ، لزم بطلان هذه الاختصاصات المعلومة .
فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : القوة المدركة ، وإن كانت غير موجودة في هذه الأعضاء ، لكنها آلات لها . فإذا وقع للنفس التفات إلى العين أبصرت أو إلى الأذن : سمعت ؟ قلنا : النفس إذا التفتت إلى
The Third Chapter: Sukr [؟] On Direct [Perception]. Demonstration that the inner soul [؟] perceives both universals and particulars together. And [؟]... although if complete [؟] it depends on the use of universals. The apparent meaning of the speech of the Chief Master indicates that the rational soul is only capable of perceiving universals and abstract things. As for particular perceptions, they are distributed over the external and internal senses. And they argued that the soul does not perceive particulars with general and specific arguments. As for the general arguments, they are four: First: That rational people by the primacy of their intellects know that vision of visible things occurs in the eye not elsewhere, and hearing of sounds occurs in the ear not elsewhere; rather we say: just as intuition judges that the tongue does not see and the eye does not taste, it also judges that the tongue tastes and the eye sees. And if we say that the described perceiver of these sensed things is the soul, then the invalidation of these known specific assignments would follow. Then they said: Why is it not permissible to say that the perceiving power, although it does not exist in these organs, yet they are instruments for it? So if the soul turns its attention to the eye, it sees, or to the ear, it hears? We said: The soul, if it turns its attention to...
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage begins a discussion on whether the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) perceives particulars or only universals. The author first attributes to Avicenna (al-Shaykh al-Ra'īs) the view that the soul only apprehends universals and abstracts, while particular perceptions are distributed among external and internal senses. Then the author presents the first of four general arguments that the soul does not perceive particulars. The argument appeals to intuitive knowledge (al-badīha): everyone knows by primordial intuition that vision is in the eye, hearing in the ear, etc. If the soul itself were the perceiver of these sensibles, then these specific assignments to organs would be invalid. Next, an objection is raised: why not say that the perceiving power is not located in the organs but uses them as instruments? When the soul turns to the eye, it sees; when it turns to the ear, it hears. The author begins his reply with 'We said: The soul, if it turns its attention to...' but the text cuts off. Thus, the passage presents the first argument and the start of a dialectical exchange.

Correction: The passage argues that by intuition, each sensation is inherently linked to a specific organ; if the soul perceived directly, these assignments would be invalid.

Second General Argument: Medical Experiments and Localization
Presents the second general argument based on medical experience showing localization of functions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how medical experiments provide evidence that perceptual faculties are physical by showing that brain damage impairs specific functions.

Core Idea
Brain damage localizes mental functions, proving they are physical.

This passage introduces the second general argument. It uses empirical medical evidence: when specific parts of the brain are damaged, corresponding mental abilities fail. This strongly suggests that these faculties are not purely spiritual but depend on physical organs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: first a general claim, then examples for external senses and internal perceptions, then a concluding conditional.
Pay attention to the three brain ventricles and their associated functions.
2
Identify the claim and evidence
The claim is that medical experiments show organ damage impairs function. The evidence: for external senses it's obvious; for internal perceptions, specific brain damage affects specific faculties.
3
Examine the three internal examples
Note how damage to the front ventricle corrupts imagination, middle ventricle corrupts thought, rear ventricle corrupts memory. This mapping is crucial for the argument.
These three are enumerated in the passage.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is implied: if these faculties were non-physical (like the soul), such localization would not occur. Therefore, they must be physical.
The passage ends with a conditional: 'Were it not that these faculties are physical, this would not be the case.'
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this argument uses empirical observation to support a metaphysical claim about the nature of perceptual powers. It shows that rational and empirical evidence can work together in kalam.
This connects to broader discussions about the soul-body relationship.

The Soul's Ability to Move the Body
Defends the soul's role as mover of the body and concludes the refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the soul must be able to move the body for its role as manager to be meaningful.

Core Idea
A manager who cannot act is no manager at all.

This lesson presents the final refutation in a series of objections against the soul's causal role. The tenth objection claims the soul is not the mover of the body. The answer cuts to the core: if the soul cannot move the body, then calling it a 'manager' becomes empty. We'll see how this argument defends the soul's causal efficacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note that this is the final response to the tenth objection.
2
Understand the objection and the definition
Identify the objection: the soul is not the mover. Then see how the answer defines 'management' as moving the body according to purposes.
The definition is key to the argument.
3
Analyze the consequence
If the soul cannot move the body, then calling it a manager is just an empty phrase. Consider why this would be problematic.
This shows the necessity of causal power for meaningful agency.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The answer concludes that all these objections are extremely weak. Ask yourself why this final point is so effective.

والجواب عما ذكروه عاشراً من أن القادر على تحريك البدن ، ليس هو النفس : أن نقول : إنه لا معنى لتدبير البدن وللتصرف فيه إلا تحريكه على وفق الأغراض والمصالح، فإن لم تقدر النفس على تحريك هذا البدن ، لم يبق لقولنا : النفس مديرة للبدن ومتصرفة فيه : إلا اللفظ الخالي عن الفائدة . فثبت بهذه البيانات : أن هذه الوجوه التي عولوا عليها في هذا الهاب : في غاية الضعف والسقوط . والله أعلم .
And the answer to what they mentioned tenthly: that the one able to move the body is not the soul. We say: There is no meaning to 'management of the body' and 'disposal in it' except moving it in accordance with purposes and interests. If the soul is not able to move this body, then nothing remains for our saying 'the soul is the manager of the body and the disposer in it' except an empty phrase. So it is established by these explanations that these aspects upon which they relied in this matter are in utmost weakness and collapse. And God knows best.

Commentary
This passage answers the tenth objection, which claimed that the soul is not the mover of the body. The answer defines what it means for the soul to manage the body: it is precisely the ability to move it according to purposes and interests. If the soul could not move the body, then calling it 'manager' would be meaningless—just empty words. The answer thus defends the soul's causal role. The passage concludes by stating that all the objections (the seventh through tenth) are extremely weak and collapse. This is the final refutation of the series.
