Three Initial Proofs That a Body Cannot Cause the Rational Soul
The author presents the first three of six proofs that the cause of the rational soul cannot be a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational arguments proving that a body cannot be the efficient cause of the rational soul, based on the similarity of bodies, the principle of non-reciprocity, and the weakness of bodies.

Core Idea
A body's limitations make it impossible to be the cause of the rational soul.

The author begins by establishing that the rational soul, whether eternal or originated, requires an originator. He then examines the possibility that this originator could be a body. In this lesson, we focus on the first three proofs that rule out bodies as efficient causes. Each proof attacks a different aspect of corporeality—their uniform quiddity, their passive nature, and their ontological weakness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read the opening lines that establish the soul's need for an originator and list the three possibilities (body, accident, or neither).
Notice that the author sets up a disjunction to be eliminated.
2
Examine Proof 1: Similarity of quiddities
Bodies are all alike in their complete essence. If one body could cause something, then every body would cause the same thing. This is false.
This proof relies on the premise of quidditative uniformity.
3
Examine Proof 2: Receptivity vs. agency
A single thing cannot be both receptive and agent. Bodies are receptive, so they cannot be agents (causes).
This is a philosophical principle attributed to the philosophers.
4
Examine Proof 3: Weakness of bodies
Bodies are weaker in existence because they depend on place and direction; immaterial substances are independent. The weaker cannot cause the stronger.
This argument compares ontological strength.
5
Reflect on the three proofs
Consider how each proof targets a different aspect of corporeality and collectively suggests that bodies cannot be causes of immaterial souls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثبت أنها ممكنة لذواتها. فسواء قلنا: إنها قديمة أو محدثة. فإنه لا بد لها من مؤثر وموجد، وذلك الموجد إما أن يكون متحيزاً، أو حالاً في المتحيز [أو لا متحيزاً ولا حالاً في المتحيز] لا جائز أن يكون متحيزاً لوجوه: الأول: إنا بينا: أن المتحيزات متماثلة في تمام الماهية. فلو كان واحد منها علة لشيء، لكان كل واحد منها علة لمثل ذلك الشيء. وذلك باطل. والثاني: إن الفلاسفة يقولون: إن الشيء الواحد، لا يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً معاً، والأجسام قابلة، فوجب أن لا تكون فاعلة. والثالث: إن المتحيز أضعف وجوداً من الجوهر المجرد لأن المتحيز محتاج إلى المكان والجهة، والمجرد غني عنهما. والأضعف لا يكون علة للأقوى. والرابع: إن النفوس البشرية عالمة بالحقائق، قادرة على الأفعال. والجسم من حيث إنه جسم، ليس كذلك. فالنفوس أشرف من الأجسام، والأشرف لا يكون معلولاً للأخس. والخامس: إن الأجسام إنما تؤثر بمشاركة الوضع. والمراد من هذا الوضع: أن المؤثر الجسماني يؤثر في القريب [منه] أولاً. ثم يؤثر ثانياً فيها اتصل بذلك القريب. ثم يؤثر ثالثاً فيها اتصل بذلك الثاني. وهذا هو المراد من قولنا: إن المؤثر الجسماني إنما يؤثر بواسطة الوضع. والدليل على أن الأمر كذلك: [هو أنه لو لم يكن الأمر كذلك] لكان تأثير هذا المؤثر الجسماني في القريب والبعيد سواء. ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك علمنا: أنه إنما يؤثر بمشاركة الوضع. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: الذي يمكن وصفه بالبعد والقرب من الجسم ليس إلا الجسم. ولما كان قبول الأثر من الجسم مشروطاً بكون القابل قريباً منه، فما يمتنع وصفه بالقرب والبعد، وجب أن لا يكون للجسم فيه أثر. لكن النفس جوهر مجرد، فيمتنع كونه معلولاً للجسم. والسادس: إن الاستقراء يدل على أن المجانسة والمشاكلة [بين العلة وبين المعلول معتبرة، والمشاركات المجانسة مفقودة] بين الجسم وبين النفوس المجردة، فامتنع كون الجسم علة. واعلم: أن أكثر هذه الوجوه يدل على أن النفس يمتنع كونها معلولة للأعراض الجسمانية. وإذا بطل هذان القسمان، ثبت: أن النفس الناطقة معلولة لجوهر مجرد عن الجسمية.
It has been established that they (the souls) are possible in themselves. So whether we say they are eternal or originated, they necessarily require an influencer and an originator. That originator is either a body, or an accident in a body, or neither a body nor an accident in a body. It is not permissible for it to be a body for several reasons: First: We have shown that bodies are similar in complete quiddity. So if one of them were a cause of something, then every one of them would be a cause of the like of that thing. That is false. Second: The philosophers say that one thing cannot be both receptive and agent simultaneously, and bodies are receptive, so it is necessary that they are not agent. Third: A body is weaker in existence than an immaterial substance because a body is in need of place and direction, while the immaterial is independent of them. The weaker cannot be the cause of the stronger. Fourth: Human souls are knowing of realities and capable of actions. A body, insofar as it is a body, is not like that. So souls are nobler than bodies, and the nobler cannot be an effect of the baser. Fifth: Bodies only have effect by participation of position (al-wadʿ). The meaning of this position is that the corporeal influencer first affects what is near to it, then secondly affects things connected to that near thing, then thirdly affects things connected to that second. This is what is meant by our saying: the corporeal influencer only acts by means of position. The proof that the matter is so is that if it were not so, the effect of this corporeal influencer on the near and the far would be equal. Since the matter is not so, we know that it only affects by participation of position. And if this is established, then we say: That which can be described as far and near to a body is nothing but a body. Since the reception of effect from a body is conditional upon the receiver being near it, whatever cannot be described as near and far must necessarily have no effect from the body. But the soul is an immaterial substance, so it is impossible for it to be an effect of a body. Sixth: Induction indicates that similarity and resemblance between cause and effect are considered, and commonalities of similarity are absent between a body and immaterial souls, so it is impossible for a body to be a cause. Know: Most of these proofs indicate that the soul cannot be an effect of bodily accidents. And when these two divisions are invalidated, it is established that the rational soul is an effect of a substance free from corporeality.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents six arguments (وجوه) proving that the efficient cause of the rational soul cannot be a body, and concludes that it must be an immaterial substance. The reasoning follows a typical kalam method of disproving alternatives. The first argument uses the principle that bodies are all alike in quiddity; if one could cause something, all would cause the same, which is false. The second invokes the philosophical principle that one thing cannot be both receptive and active; bodies are receptive, so they cannot be agents. The third compares ontological strength: bodies are weaker because they depend on place and direction, so they cannot cause the stronger soul. The fourth argues from nobility: souls are knowing and powerful, thus nobler than bodies, so they cannot be effects of baser bodies. The fifth is a spatial argument: bodies affect only what is near; the soul is not spatial, so a body cannot affect it. The sixth uses induction to assert that cause and effect must share similarity, which is absent between body and soul. The author adds that most of these proofs also show the soul cannot be an effect of bodily accidents. Since the cause cannot be a body or an accident in a body, it must be a substance free from corporeality.

Key Terms
نفس ناطقة (rational soul)
متحيز (body)
جوهر مجرد (immaterial substance)
علة (cause)
ماهية (quiddity)
فاعل (agent)
قابل (receptive)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since bodies cause physical effects, they can also cause immaterial things like the soul.
Correction: Bodies are limited to affecting only things that are spatial and similar to them. The rational soul is immaterial and not spatial, so a body cannot be its efficient cause.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why cannot a body be the cause of the rational soul?
A) Because bodies are too weak.
B) Because all bodies are alike; if one caused, all would cause the same thing.
C) Because bodies are receptive, not active.
D) Because bodies depend on place.

Key Takeaway
Three rational arguments—based on the uniformity of bodies, the principle of non-reciprocity, and the ontological weakness of bodies—show that a body cannot be the efficient cause of the rational soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If no body can be the cause of your soul, what does that tell you about where your true value and identity come from? Are you living as if your soul is defined by your body, or by its immaterial Cause?
?
The proofs show that bodies are weak and dependent. Your soul, being from an immaterial Cause, is stronger. Yet you often worry about bodily concerns. How can this truth shift your priorities today?
?
Since the weaker cannot cause the stronger, and your soul is stronger than any body, is it not foolish to let bodily desires enslave your soul? What specific bodily habit do you need to stop treating as your master?

Three Final Proofs and the Conclusion: The Soul's Cause Must Be Immaterial
The author presents the last three proofs and the final conclusion that the rational soul's cause is an immaterial substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three additional proofs that a body cannot cause the soul, and the conclusion that the soul's cause must be an immaterial substance.

Core Idea
The soul's nobility, non-spatiality, and dissimilarity to bodies force its cause to be immaterial.

Having presented three initial proofs, the author now gives three more reasons why a body cannot be the cause of the rational soul. These arguments focus on the soul's superiority over bodies, the spatial nature of bodily causation, and the need for similarity between cause and effect. After ruling out bodies and their accidents, the author concludes that the cause must be a substance free from matter.

Guided Path
1
Review the context
The author has already ruled out that the cause is a body using three proofs. Now he provides three more.
These proofs are complementary and reinforce the conclusion.
2
Examine Proof 4: Nobility argument
Souls are nobler than bodies because they know and act; the nobler cannot be an effect of the baser.
This argument appeals to the principle that the more perfect cannot come from the less perfect.
3
Examine Proof 5: Spatial causation argument
Bodies cause only through spatial proximity; the soul is not spatial, so a body cannot affect it.
This is the longest proof, explaining the concept of 'position' (wadʿ).
4
Examine Proof 6: Induction from similarity
Induction shows that cause and effect share similarity; no similarity between body and soul, so body cannot be cause.
This is an empirical argument.
5
Read the conclusion
The author concludes that since the cause cannot be a body or an accident in a body, it must be an immaterial substance.
This is the final result of the six proofs.

ثبت أنها ممكنة لذواتها. فسواء قلنا: إنها قديمة أو محدثة. فإنه لا بد لها من مؤثر وموجد، وذلك الموجد إما أن يكون متحيزاً، أو حالاً في المتحيز [أو لا متحيزاً ولا حالاً في المتحيز] لا جائز أن يكون متحيزاً لوجوه: الأول: إنا بينا: أن المتحيزات متماثلة في تمام الماهية. فلو كان واحد منها علة لشيء، لكان كل واحد منها علة لمثل ذلك الشيء. وذلك باطل. والثاني: إن الفلاسفة يقولون: إن الشيء الواحد، لا يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً معاً، والأجسام قابلة، فوجب أن لا تكون فاعلة. والثالث: إن المتحيز أضعف وجوداً من الجوهر المجرد لأن المتحيز محتاج إلى المكان والجهة، والمجرد غني عنهما. والأضعف لا يكون علة للأقوى. والرابع: إن النفوس البشرية عالمة بالحقائق، قادرة على الأفعال. والجسم من حيث إنه جسم، ليس كذلك. فالنفوس أشرف من الأجسام، والأشرف لا يكون معلولاً للأخس. والخامس: إن الأجسام إنما تؤثر بمشاركة الوضع. والمراد من هذا الوضع: أن المؤثر الجسماني يؤثر في القريب [منه] أولاً. ثم يؤثر ثانياً فيها اتصل بذلك القريب. ثم يؤثر ثالثاً فيها اتصل بذلك الثاني. وهذا هو المراد من قولنا: إن المؤثر الجسماني إنما يؤثر بواسطة الوضع. والدليل على أن الأمر كذلك: [هو أنه لو لم يكن الأمر كذلك] لكان تأثير هذا المؤثر الجسماني في القريب والبعيد سواء. ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك علمنا: أنه إنما يؤثر بمشاركة الوضع. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: الذي يمكن وصفه بالبعد والقرب من الجسم ليس إلا الجسم. ولما كان قبول الأثر من الجسم مشروطاً بكون القابل قريباً منه، فما يمتنع وصفه بالقرب والبعد، وجب أن لا يكون للجسم فيه أثر. لكن النفس جوهر مجرد، فيمتنع كونه معلولاً للجسم. والسادس: إن الاستقراء يدل على أن المجانسة والمشاكلة [بين العلة وبين المعلول معتبرة، والمشاركات المجانسة مفقودة] بين الجسم وبين النفوس المجردة، فامتنع كون الجسم علة. واعلم: أن أكثر هذه الوجوه يدل على أن النفس يمتنع كونها معلولة للأعراض الجسمانية. وإذا بطل هذان القسمان، ثبت: أن النفس الناطقة معلولة لجوهر مجرد عن الجسمية.
It has been established that they (the souls) are possible in themselves. So whether we say they are eternal or originated, they necessarily require an influencer and an originator. That originator is either a body, or an accident in a body, or neither a body nor an accident in a body. It is not permissible for it to be a body for several reasons: First: We have shown that bodies are similar in complete quiddity. So if one of them were a cause of something, then every one of them would be a cause of the like of that thing. That is false. Second: The philosophers say that one thing cannot be both receptive and agent simultaneously, and bodies are receptive, so it is necessary that they are not agent. Third: A body is weaker in existence than an immaterial substance because a body is in need of place and direction, while the immaterial is independent of them. The weaker cannot be the cause of the stronger. Fourth: Human souls are knowing of realities and capable of actions. A body, insofar as it is a body, is not like that. So souls are nobler than bodies, and the nobler cannot be an effect of the baser. Fifth: Bodies only have effect by participation of position (al-wadʿ). The meaning of this position is that the corporeal influencer first affects what is near to it, then secondly affects things connected to that near thing, then thirdly affects things connected to that second. This is what is meant by our saying: the corporeal influencer only acts by means of position. The proof that the matter is so is that if it were not so, the effect of this corporeal influencer on the near and the far would be equal. Since the matter is not so, we know that it only affects by participation of position. And if this is established, then we say: That which can be described as far and near to a body is nothing but a body. Since the reception of effect from a body is conditional upon the receiver being near it, whatever cannot be described as near and far must necessarily have no effect from the body. But the soul is an immaterial substance, so it is impossible for it to be an effect of a body. Sixth: Induction indicates that similarity and resemblance between cause and effect are considered, and commonalities of similarity are absent between a body and immaterial souls, so it is impossible for a body to be a cause. Know: Most of these proofs indicate that the soul cannot be an effect of bodily accidents. And when these two divisions are invalidated, it is established that the rational soul is an effect of a substance free from corporeality.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents six arguments (وجوه) proving that the efficient cause of the rational soul cannot be a body, and concludes that it must be an immaterial substance. The reasoning follows a typical kalam method of disproving alternatives. The first argument uses the principle that bodies are all alike in quiddity; if one could cause something, all would cause the same, which is false. The second invokes the philosophical principle that one thing cannot be both receptive and active; bodies are receptive, so they cannot be agents. The third compares ontological strength: bodies are weaker because they depend on place and direction, so they cannot cause the stronger soul. The fourth argues from nobility: souls are knowing and powerful, thus nobler than bodies, so they cannot be effects of baser bodies. The fifth is a spatial argument: bodies affect only what is near; the soul is not spatial, so a body cannot affect it. The sixth uses induction to assert that cause and effect must share similarity, which is absent between body and soul. The author adds that most of these proofs also show the soul cannot be an effect of bodily accidents. Since the cause cannot be a body or an accident in a body, it must be a substance free from corporeality.

Key Terms
نفس ناطقة (rational soul)
متحيز (body)
جوهر مجرد (immaterial substance)
وضع (position)
مجانس (similar)
مشاكلة (resemblance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul might be caused by a combination of bodies or accidents.
Correction: The proofs show that no body or accident can be the cause; therefore, the cause must be a separate immaterial substance.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth proof, why can a body not affect the rational soul?
A) Because the soul is nobler.
B) Because bodies only affect what is near them, and the soul is not spatial.
C) Because cause and effect must be similar.
D) Because bodies are weaker.

Key Takeaway
Three arguments—from nobility, spatial causation, and induction—complete the demonstration that no body can cause the soul, establishing that its cause is an immaterial substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that the soul is nobler than the body and cannot be its effect. If the soul is your true self, why do you still spend most of your energy serving the body's demands while neglecting the soul's nourishment? What one change will you make today to honor the soul's nobility?
?
The fifth proof shows that your soul cannot be 'near' or 'far' from anything physical. Yet you often feel distant from Allah because of your location or circumstances. When you feel that way, will you remember that distance is only an illusion for the soul, and turn back to Him immediately?
?
If every cause must resemble its effect, and your soul is immaterial and knowing, then its Cause must be all-knowing and immaterial. Are you truly seeking that Cause with the same intensity you seek material causes for your comfort? What will you sacrifice today to draw closer to that Cause?

Proof That the Soul's Cause Is the Active Intellect
The author identifies the immaterial cause as a separate intellect and proves it is the Active Intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rational soul's immaterial cause must be an intellect rather than another soul, and how the author identifies it as the Active Intellect.

Core Idea
The soul's cause cannot use a bodily instrument, so it must be an intellect.

In this passage, the author continues the investigation into the immaterial cause of the rational soul. He first establishes a key distinction between two types of immaterial beings: souls (which act through bodily instruments) and intellects (which do not). By showing that the soul's cause cannot use a bodily instrument, he rules out the possibility that it is a soul, leading to the conclusion that it must be an intellect—specifically, the Active Intellect. This argument is foundational for understanding the Neoplatonic emanation scheme.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of immaterial beings
Read the opening division: immaterial beings are either souls (acting through bodily instruments) or intellects (acting without them). Grasp why this distinction matters.
Souls are not completely independent; intellects are.
2
Follow the argument against a soul cause
The author argues that a bodily instrument only makes sense for a spatial body. Since the rational soul is not a body, its cause cannot use a bodily instrument, so it cannot be a soul.
This step eliminates one possibility.
3
Identify the cause as an intellect
Having ruled out souls, the cause must be an intellect. The author specifies it as the last celestial intellect managing the sublunar world, i.e., the Active Intellect.
4
Consider the initial proof (cut off)
The text begins to argue why these souls cannot be direct effects of the Necessary Existence or other intellects. Note that the argument is incomplete, but appreciate its direction.
The review notes the text is cut off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: إن هذا الموجود المجرد قسمان: النفس - وهو الذي يفعل بواسطة آلة جسمانية - والعقل - وهو الذي لا يتوقف تأثيره في الغير على اختيار آلة جسمانية - والأول باطل. لأنا بينا: أن كون الآلة الجسمانية معتبرة، إنما يعقل في حق الجسم، الذي يحصل لتلك الآلة الجسمانية. قرب منه تارة، وبعد منه أخرى. ولما لم تكن النفس الناطقة جسماً، علمنا: أنه لا يمكن [أن يقال]: إن موجدها، أوجدها بواسطة آلة جسمانية. فثبت: أن موجدها ليس من جنس النفوس، بل من جنس العقول. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إن ذلك العقل هو العقل الأخير المدير لما تحت كرة القمر، وهو العقل الفعال [والدليل عليه: أنه لا يمكن أن تكون هذه النفوس معلولات واجب الوجود. لما ثبت أنه فرد منزه من جميع جهات الكثرة، فيمتنع أن يصدر عنه معلولات كثيرة، ولا يمكن أيضاً أن يكون من معلولات شيء من العقول المديرة للأفلاك التسعة وإلا فقد صدر...]
Then we say: This immaterial entity is of two kinds: the soul – which acts by means of a bodily instrument – and the intellect – which does not require the instrumentality of a bodily instrument. The first is invalid. Because we have shown that the consideration of a bodily instrument is only conceivable in relation to a body, which has proximity and distance from that bodily instrument at times. Since the rational soul is not a body, we know that it cannot be said that its originator originated it by means of a bodily instrument. Therefore it is established that its originator is not of the kind of souls, but of the kind of intellects. And when this is established, we say: That intellect is the last intellect, the manager of what is under the sphere of the moon, and it is the Active Intellect. [The proof for it: that these souls cannot be effects of the Necessary Existence, because it has been established that He is one, free from all aspects of multiplicity, so it is impossible that many effects should proceed from Him; nor can they be effects of any of the managing intellects of the nine spheres, otherwise there would have proceeded...]
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage continues the argument by dividing immaterial substances into two kinds: souls (which act through bodily instruments) and intellects (which do not). Since the cause of the rational soul cannot use a bodily instrument (because the soul itself is immaterial, and the concept of a bodily instrument only makes sense for spatial beings), the cause cannot be a soul. Therefore, it must be an intellect. The author then identifies this intellect as the last celestial intellect, the manager of the sublunar world, which is the Active Intellect (العقل الفعال). A brief proof is given: the souls cannot be direct effects of the Necessary Existence because He is one and cannot produce multiple effects directly; nor can they be effects of any of the other celestial spheres' intellects, otherwise multiple effects would proceed from a single cause (the argument is cut off but assumes that would lead to unacceptable multiplicity). The context suggests a Neoplatonic emanation scheme.

Key Terms
نفس (soul)
عقل (intellect)
آلة جسمانية (bodily instrument)
عقل فعال (Active Intellect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Active Intellect is a soul because it is immaterial and causes souls.
Correction: The author distinguishes souls as acting through bodily instruments; the Active Intellect does not, so it is an intellect, not a soul.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the possibility that the rational soul's cause is another soul?
A) Because souls are not immaterial.
B) Because souls act through bodily instruments, and the rational soul cannot be caused by something using a bodily instrument.
C) Because souls are weaker than intellects.
D) Because souls are multiple, while the cause must be one.

Key Takeaway
The rational soul's immaterial cause cannot be a soul because its cause would need a bodily instrument, which is impossible for a non-spatial being; therefore, it must be an intellect, namely the Active Intellect.

The Cause of the Soul: The Active Intellect
Classification of separate substances and proof for the active intellect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the cause of the rational soul must be an intellect (the active intellect) rather than another soul or the Necessary Existent directly.

Core Idea
The soul's immateriality demands that its cause be an intellect, not a soul.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah tackles a crucial question: what is the cause of the rational soul? The author classifies separate substances into two types—souls and intellects—and then eliminates possibilities until only one remains. By following the argument, you will see how Neoplatonic causality and the principle 'from one only one' lead to the active intellect as the creator of souls.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing to analyze. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the repeated phrase about the Necessary Existent being free from multiplicity—this is a key principle.
2
Understand the two types of separate substances
Identify the two divisions: the soul (acts through a bodily instrument) and the intellect (acts without a bodily instrument). Understand why the soul cannot be the cause of the rational soul.
The rational soul itself is not a body, so its cause cannot use a bodily instrument.
3
Follow the proof for the active intellect
The author eliminates the Necessary Existent and the celestial intellects as direct causes, leaving only the last intellect governing the sublunar realm. See how the principle 'from one only one' is used repeatedly.
The proof relies on the idea that multiple effects cannot proceed from a single, simple cause.
4
Reflect on the philosophical implications
Consider how this argument ties together cosmology, psychology, and causation. The active intellect serves as the intermediary between the eternal and the temporal souls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : إن هذا الموجود المجرد قسمان : النفس - وهو الذي يفعل بواسطة آلة جسمانية والعقل - وهو الذي لا يتوقف تأثيره في الغير على اختيار آلة جسمانية - والأول باطل . لأنا بينا : أن كون الآلة الجسمانية معتبرة ، إنما يعقل في حق الجسم، الذي يحصل لتلك الآلة الجسمانية . قرب منه تارة ، وبعد منه أخرى . ولما لم تكن النفس الناطقة جسماً ، علمنا : أنه لا يمكن أن يقال : إن موجدها أوجدها بواسطة آلة جسمانية . فثبت : أن موجدها ليس من جنس النفوس ، بل من جنس العقول . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن ذلك العقل هو العقل الأخير المدير لما تحت كرة القمر ، وهو العقل الفعال والدليل عليه : أنه لا يمكن أن تكون هذه النفوس معلولات واجب الوجود . لما ثبت أنه فرد منزه من جميع جهات الكثرة ، فيمتنع أن يصدر عنه معلولات واجب الوجود . لما ثبت أنه فرد منزه عن جهات الكثرة ، فيمتنع أن يصدر عنه معلولات كثيرة ، ولا يمكن أيضاً أن يكون من معلولات شيء من العقول المديرة للأفلاك التسعة وإلا فقد صدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد. ولما بطل الكل ، ثبت : أن الموجد لهذه النفوس ، ليس إلا العقل الأخير ، المدبر لما تحت كرة القمر . وهو العقل الفعال
Then we say: this existent abstract (al-mawjud al-mujarrad) is of two divisions: the soul — which acts by means of a bodily instrument, and the intellect — whose effect on another does not depend on choosing a bodily instrument. The first is false. For we have shown that considering the bodily instrument is only conceivable in the case of a body, which obtains near to that bodily instrument sometimes and far from it other times. And since the rational soul is not a body, we know that it cannot be said that its creator created it by means of a bodily instrument. So it is established that its creator is not of the genus of souls, but of the genus of intellects. And when this is established, we say: that intellect is the last intellect governing what is under the sphere of the moon, and it is the active intellect (al-'aql al-fa''al). The proof for it is that it is impossible for these souls to be effects of the Necessary Existent. For it is established that He is an individual free from all aspects of multiplicity, so it is impossible that many effects proceed from Him. Nor is it possible that they be from the effects of any of the intellects governing the nine spheres, for then more than one would proceed from the one. And when all is invalidated, it is established that the creator of these souls is nothing but the last intellect, the governor of what is under the sphere of the moon. And it is the active intellect.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage classifies separate substances into two types: souls (which use bodily instruments) and intellects (which do not). The author argues that the cause of the rational soul cannot be a soul, because a soul's action requires a bodily instrument, but the soul itself is not a body. Hence the cause must be an intellect. Then a proof is given that this intellect is the active intellect (al-'aql al-fa''al) based on the principle that from one only one proceeds, and the Necessary Existent is one, so He cannot directly cause many souls; the celestial intellects also cannot because each is one; therefore the last intellect (the active intellect) is the cause.

Key Terms
جوهر مجرد
النفس
العقل
آلة جسمانية
العقل الفعال
الواحد
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rational soul could be caused by a higher soul or directly by God.
Correction: The author shows that a soul uses a bodily instrument, incompatible with the immaterial soul. God is too simple to directly produce many souls; the active intellect is the necessary intermediary.

Checkpoint
Why can't the cause of the rational soul be a soul?
A) Because souls are not immaterial
B) Because souls act through bodily instruments, but the rational soul is not a body
C) Because souls are multiple
D) Because souls are caused by bodies

Key Takeaway
The cause of the rational soul must be an intellect—specifically the active intellect—because souls cannot cause immaterial souls, and God and the celestial intellects are too simple to directly produce many souls.

?
The argument shows that God's oneness necessitates a chain of intermediaries. Do you fully accept that God's wisdom includes such a hierarchy, or do you secretly think you can bypass it? How does this affect your reliance on the means He has established?
?
The active intellect is the immediate cause of your soul. Since it governs the sublunar world, your soul is connected to a cosmic order. Does this make you feel a sense of responsibility toward the world? What is one way you can act as a steward of this sublunar realm, honoring the intellect that gave you existence?

Critique of the Emanation Principle and the Role of Celestial Souls
The author critiques the emanation principle and allows celestial souls to influence human souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects the emanation principle and how he allows celestial souls to be causes of human souls, including his explanation of 'perfect natures'.

Core Idea
The principle 'from one only one' is weak; celestial souls can be true causes of human souls.

In this passage, the author critically examines the foundational principle of emanationism—that from one only one can proceed. Having already demonstrated its weakness, he opens the door to a richer causal framework: the souls that govern the celestial spheres and stars may directly bring about human rational souls. This shift allows for diversity among human souls based on their celestial origins, and it also clarifies the concept of 'perfect natures' used by talisman practitioners.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the author's critical tone at the beginning.
2
Identify the criticism of the emanation principle
Find where the author states that the previous method is based on 'from one only one proceeds' and that it is weak and corrupt.
This is a key moment – the author rejects a major philosophical principle.
3
See the alternative causal model
After rejecting the principle, the author asserts that celestial souls can be effective causes of human souls. Note the examples of Saturn and Jupiter.
This opens the door to multiple causes.
4
Understand the diversity of human souls
The author explains that different human souls may originate from different celestial souls, including fixed stars like Sirius.
This explains variation in human souls.
5
Grasp the concept of 'perfect natures'
Read the author's explanation of why talisman makers call these celestial souls 'perfect natures': the cause has complete perfection, while the effect has a weak trace.
This connects causality to notions of perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا غاية الكلام في تقرير هذه الطريقة. وهي مبنية على أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد . وقد عرفت ضعفه وفساده. وإذا ثبت هذا ، ظهر أنه لا يمتنع أن تكون الأرواح المدبرة للأجسام الفلكية ، والأجرام الكوكبية ، هي المؤثرة في وجود هذه النفوس الناطقة. وإذا قلنا : إن هذه الأرواح البشرية أنواع مختلفة ، لم يمتنع أن يكون بعضها من معلولات نفس فلك زحل ، وبعضها من معلولات نفس فلك المشتري ، وعلى هذا الترتيب فقس . ولا يمتنع أيضاً أن يكون طائفة من معلولات الروح المدبرة لـ ه الشعري اليمانية ، وطائفة ثانية من معلولات الروح المدبرة لكوكب آخر من الكواكب الثابتة . واعلم : أن كل كمال يحصل للحيوان فهو أثر من آثار تلك العلة فيكون كمال تلك الحالة حاصلاً لتلك العلة ، ويكون الحاصل منه عند المعلول كالأثر الضعيف ، بالنسبة إلى اعتبار ذلك الكمال فلهذا السبب سمي أصحاب الطلسمات تلك الأرواح التي هي المبدأ لهذه الطائفة من النفوس البشرية ، بالطباع التام . لأن تلك الصفة في ذلك الشيء ، الذي هو الأصل تامة كاملة. وفي هذه النتائج والفروع قليلة ناقصة . وذلك الجوهر
This is the final word in presenting this method. It is based on the principle that from the one only one proceeds. And you have known its weakness and corruption. And when this is established, it appears that it is not impossible for the souls governing celestial bodies and planetary bodies to be the effective agents in the existence of these rational souls. And if we say that these human souls are different types, it is not impossible for some of them to be effects of the soul of the sphere of Saturn, and some effects of the soul of the sphere of Jupiter, and on this order analogize. Nor is it impossible for a group to be effects of the soul governing the star Sirius of Yemen, and a second group effects of the soul governing another of the fixed stars. Know that every perfection that occurs in the animal is an effect of those causes, so the perfection of that state is present in that cause, and what is obtained from it in the effect is like a weak trace relative to the consideration of that perfection. For this reason the makers of talismans (ashab al-tilismat) called those souls which are the principle of this group of human souls 'the perfect natures' (al-taba'i' al-tamm). Because that attribute in that thing which is the origin is complete and perfect, while in these results and branches it is deficient and incomplete. And that substance...
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage begins with a critical note: the previous method (that the soul's cause is the active intellect) was based on the principle that from one only one proceeds, which the author considers weak and corrupt. Therefore, it is not impossible that the souls governing celestial bodies and planets are the effective causes of human souls. The author then allows for variety: different human souls could be caused by different celestial souls (e.g., Saturn, Jupiter, fixed stars). He also connects this to the concept of 'perfect natures' used by talisman practitioners, explaining that the perfection in the cause is complete, while its manifestation in the effect is weak and incomplete. This lesson shows the author's openness to causal multiplicity and his criticism of strict emanationism.

مؤثرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the principle 'from one only one' is universally accepted and necessary for emanationist theories.
Correction: The author explicitly calls this principle weak and corrupt, and builds an alternative causal framework that allows multiplicity.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The principle 'from one only one' is flawed, allowing for celestial souls to directly cause diverse human souls, with the cause having perfect perfection and the effect a weak trace.

What concrete step will you take today to turn from the shadow to the sun?

Are there any rigid beliefs you hold about how Allah (سبحانه وتعالى) works that might actually limit your understanding of His power and wisdom? Will you dare to question a comfortable assumption today and seek a broader perspective?

How can you honor the distinctiveness of every soul you meet today, seeing each as a unique direct creation of Allah?

Different Celestial Causes for Human Souls
Al-Razi explains the possibility that different celestial souls cause different human souls, with an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that different human souls may originate from different celestial souls, and the analogy he uses to explain this relationship.

Core Idea
Different human souls may have different celestial origins, like children from different fathers.

Al-Razi continues his exploration of the causal link between celestial and human souls. He now considers whether all human souls stem from the same celestial source or from different ones. Through examples and an analogy with fatherhood, he shows the logical possibility of multiple celestial causes, while also reporting the terminology used by talisman practitioners.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify examples
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Al-Razi lists specific celestial bodies (Saturn, Jupiter, Sirius) as possible sources for different human souls.
This is a hypothetical exploration, not a definitive claim.
2
Understand the logical structure
Al-Razi argues: if human souls are of different types, then it is not impossible that they have different causes. Recognize this as a conditional possibility.
The key word is 'لا يمتنع' (it is not impossible).
3
Examine the cause-effect relationship
Al-Razi explains that the perfection in the cause is complete, while in the effect it is only a weak trace. This is why talisman masters call the celestial souls 'perfect nature'.
The term 'الطباع التام' is used by talisman practitioners.
4
Reflect on the analogy of the father
The cause is like a father to its effects (human souls). Just as a father cares more for his own children, each celestial soul has more concern for its own effects.
This analogy helps illustrate the special connection.

وإذا قلنا: إن هذه الأرواح البشرية أنواع مختلفة، لم يمتنع أن يكون بعضها من معلولات نفس فلك زحل، وبعضها من معلولات نفس فلك المشتري، وعلى هذا الترتيب فقس. ولا يمتنع أيضاً أن يكون طائفة من معلولات الروح المدبرة للشعري اليمانية، وطائفة ثانية من معلولات الروح المدبرة لكوكب آخر من الكواكب الثابتة. واعلم: أن كل كمال يحصل للحيوان فهو أثر من آثار تلك العلة فيكون كمال تلك الحالة حاصلاً لتلك العلة، ويكون الحاصل منه عند المعلول كالأثر الضعيف، بالنسبة إلى اعتبار ذلك الكمال فلهذا السبب سمي أصحاب الطلسمات تلك الأرواح التي هي المبدأ لهذه الطائفة من النفوس البشرية، بالطباع التام. لأن تلك الصفة في ذلك الشيء، الذي هو الأصل تامة كاملة. وفي هذه النتائج والفروع قليلة ناقصة. وذلك الجوهر الذي هو العلة يكون بالنسبة إلى هذه النتائج كالأب بالنسبة إلى الأولاد، والأصل بالنسبة إلى الفرع وكما أن الأب انجذابه إلى أولاده أكثر من انجذابه إلى أولاد غيره. فكذلك لا يمتنع أن يكون اهتمام حال كل روح من الأرواح الفلكية بالنفوس البشرية، التي هي كالأولاد، أتم من اهتمامها بنتائج سائر الأرواح. ولهذا السبب يقول أصحاب الطلسمات: أخبرني طباعي التام بكذا، وأرشدني إلى كذا.
And if we say: these human souls are different types, it is not impossible that some of them are effects of the soul of the sphere of Saturn, and some are effects of the soul of the sphere of Jupiter, and analogize accordingly. And it is also not impossible that a group are effects of the soul governing Sirius the Yemenite, and a second group are effects of the soul governing another of the fixed stars. Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage extends the earlier discussion by exploring the possibility that different human souls may have different celestial causes. Al-Razi begins by positing that if human souls are of different types, it is not impossible that some are effects of the soul of Saturn's sphere, some of Jupiter's sphere, and so on. He also allows that groups may be effects of the soul governing the fixed star Sirius (al-Shiʿrā al-Yamāniyya) or other fixed stars. He then explains the relationship between cause and effect: every perfection (kamāl) in an animal is an effect of its cause, and the perfection in the cause is full, while in the effect it is only a weak trace. For this reason, talisman practitioners call these originating souls 'perfect nature' (al-ṭibāʿ al-tāmm), because the attribute is perfect in the origin but deficient in the results and branches. Al-Razi then draws an analogy: the cause is like a father to children, or a root to a branch. Just as a father's inclination towards his own children is stronger than towards others, similarly each celestial soul's concern for the human souls that are its effects (like children) is more complete than for the effects of other souls. This is why talisman masters claim that their 'perfect nature' informs them and guides them. The passage is purely exploratory and hypothetical, as the author is not asserting the truth of this view but presenting it as a possible consequence of the earlier argument.

Razi's Probabilistic Proof of Celestial Causality
Razi presents an analogy and probabilistic proof for celestial causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's argument that celestial souls are the causes of human souls and its probabilistic nature.

Core Idea
Celestial souls may be the 'fathers' of human souls, but the proof is only probable.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents an analogy to argue that celestial souls are the causes of human souls. He compares the cause-effect relationship to a father's bond with his children. However, he cautions that this argument only yields probability unless supported by mystical experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall flow.
Notice how Razi uses analogy and then qualifies it.
2
Analyze the father-children analogy
Compare the relationship between cause and effect to a father and his children. Consider why Razi uses this analogy.
3
Understand the epistemic status
Razi explains that the argument is only probable unless strengthened by mystical unveiling. Ask yourself how one might confirm it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذي هو العلة يكون بالنسبة إلى هذه النتائج كالأب بالنسبة إلى الأولاد، والأصل بالنسبة إلى الفرع. وكما أن الأب انجذابه إلى أولاده أكثر من انجذابه إلى أولاد غيره، فكذلك لا يمتنع أن يكون اهتمام حال كل روح من الأرواح الفلكية بالنفوس البشرية، التي هي كالأولاد، أتم من اهتمامها بنتائج سائر الأرواح.

ولهذا السبب يقول أصحاب الطلسمات: أخبرني طباعي التام بكذا، وأرشدني إلى كذا.

واعلم أن الحق أن هذا النوع من الكلام ليس فيه إلا بيان مجرد الاحتمال. ثم إن اتفق لأرباب المكاشفات وأصحاب المشاهدات تجارب تقوي هذا الاحتمال: قوي الاعتقاد فيه. وإلا فهي باقية في بقعة الإمكان.

واعلم أن هذا البرهان الذي ذكرناه في أن العلل الموجبة لذوات النفوس لا بد وأن تكون شيئاً من العقول الفلكية أو النفوس الفلكية فهو بعينه برهان على أن السبب لحدوث العلوم والأخلاق في جواهر النفوس البشرية يجب أن يكون شيئاً من العقول الفلكية أو النفوس الفلكية. ولما كان منهاج الكلام فيه واحداً، لا جرم اقتصرنا على المذكور، وتركنا الإطناب فيه. والله أعلم.
That which is the cause is in relation to these results like the father in relation to children, and the origin in relation to the branch. And just as the father's attraction to his children is greater than his attraction to the children of others, so likewise it is not impossible that the concern of each soul of the celestial souls for the human souls, which are like children, is more complete than its concern for the results of the other souls. And for this reason the masters of talismans say: 'My complete nature informed me of such-and-such, and guided me to such-and-such.' And know that the truth is that this type of discourse contains nothing except an expression of bare probability. Then if it happens that for the people of unveilings and the people of witnessed experiences, experiments strengthen this probability, conviction in it becomes strong. Otherwise, it remains in the place of possibility. And know that this proof which we mentioned concerning that the necessary causes of the essences of souls must be something from the celestial intellects or the celestial souls, it is itself a proof that the cause for the occurrence of knowledge and ethics in the substances of human souls must be something from the celestial intellects or the celestial souls. And since the method of discourse concerning it is one, we without doubt restrict ourselves to what was mentioned and omit elaboration upon it. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical proof that the causes of human souls and their attributes are celestial. The author begins with an analogy: the cause (the 'illa) is related to its effects (al-nata'ij) as a father is to his children, and as an origin (al-asl) is to its branch (al-far'). Just as a father has a stronger attraction to his own children than to others' children, similarly it is not impossible that each celestial soul (ruh min al-arwah al-falakiyya) has a more complete concern for human souls, which are like its children, than for the results of other souls. The author then notes that the practitioners of talismans (ashab al-talasimat) base their claims on this principle: 'My complete nature informed me of such-and-such.' However, the author clarifies that this entire type of argument only expresses bare probability (mujarrad al-ihtimal). If the mystics (arbab al-mukashafat) and visionaries (ashab al-mushahadat) have experiences that strengthen this probability, then belief becomes strong; otherwise, it remains merely possible. Finally, the author states that the same proof he used for the causes of the essences of souls (dhawat al-nufus) applies equally to the cause of the occurrence of knowledge (al-'ulum) and ethics (al-akhlaq) in the substances of human souls: they must be from celestial intellects or celestial souls. The method being the same, he restricts himself to what was already mentioned and does not elaborate further.

Misconception: The proof is certain and decisive.
Correction: The author explicitly states that this discourse expresses only bare probability (mujarrad al-ihtimal) and remains in the realm of possibility unless strengthened by mystical experience.

First Argument Against Supplication: Contingents Depend on God
Presents the first argument against supplication: all contingents depend on God, so prayer cannot add or remove effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against supplication based on the dependency of all contingents on the Necessary Existent, and why it challenges the efficacy of dua.

Core Idea
Supplication is either superfluous or futile because all effects depend entirely on God.

This passage opens a chapter on the efficacy of supplication. The author presents the first of several arguments from those who deny that supplication can influence events. The argument draws on a foundational principle of Islamic theology: all contingent beings are entirely dependent on the Necessary Existent (God). We will read the passage, unpack its reasoning, and see how it challenges the common understanding of prayer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Some words in the Arabic are unclear due to OCR, but the logical argument is still clear.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice that the argument starts with the claim that all contingent things (al-mumkinat) are entirely dependent on the Essence of Existence (i.e., God). This is the foundation of the argument.
This premise is central to Ash'ari causal theory.
3
Understand the logic of necessary concomitants
The argument then splits into two possibilities: either the desired effect is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of God's essence, or it is not. Follow each branch to see what follows.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that supplication is either unnecessary (if the effect is already necessitated) or useless (if it is not). Ask yourself why this conclusion follows from the premises.
This is the first challenge; later lessons will present responses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع سكر[؟] بيان أن اشتغال النفوس البشرية بالدعاء والتخري[؟]. هل يعقل أن يكون فيه فاتحة أم لا؟ بوجوه : من الناس من أنكر هذه التأثيرات ، واحتج على صحة ذلك الإنكار الحجة الأولى : إن الممكنات بأسرها منتهية إلى ذات الوجود ، فإن اقتضى شيء من لوازم ذات واجب الوجود، حدوث ذلك الشيء . وجب أن يحصل ، سواء وجد ذلك الدعاء ، أو لم يوجد . وإن لم يوجد في لوازم ذات واجب الوجود ما يوجب حصول ذلك الأثر ، لم ينتفع بالدعاء أصلا
The seventh chapter: [؟] [سكر]. Explanation that the occupation of human souls with supplication and [؟] – is it rationally conceivable that there is an opening (or benefit?) or not? With aspects: Among people are those who denied these effects and argued for the correctness of that denial. The first argument: All contingent things are entirely dependent on the Essence of Existence. If something from the necessary concomitants of the Essence of the Necessary Existent necessitates the occurrence of that thing, then it must occur, whether that supplication exists or not. And if there is not found among the necessary concomitants of the Essence of the Necessary Existent that which necessitates the occurrence of that effect, then supplication is of no benefit at all.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This is a passage that introduces a chapter and presents the first of several arguments against the efficacy of supplication (dua). The author begins by stating the chapter heading, which contains some unclear wording, and then frames the question: can human engagement in supplication have any beneficial effect? He then mentions that some people deny these effects and argue for that denial. The first argument is based on the complete dependency of all contingent beings (al-mumkinat) on the Essence of the Necessary Existent (i.e., God). The argument goes as follows: If an effect is something that is necessarily required by God's essential attributes (the necessary concomitants of His essence), then that effect must occur regardless of whether supplication exists or not. Conversely, if the effect is not among those necessary concomitants, then supplication cannot bring it about because there is no cause for it in God's essence. Thus, either supplication is superfluous (since the effect will happen anyway) or it is futile (since it cannot cause the effect). This challenges the common belief that prayer can influence events.

Key Terms
ممكنات (contingents)
واجب الوجود (Necessary Existent)
لوازم (necessary concomitants)
دعاء (supplication)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument proves that supplication is completely worthless in Islam and that prayer has no role in the life of a believer.
Correction: This is a philosophical challenge, not a rejection of prayer. The argument forces theologians to explain how supplication can be effective within the framework of God's absolute sovereignty. Later responses show that God can decree that certain effects occur through supplication as a means.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why is supplication either superfluous or useless?
A) Because God is all-powerful and does not need supplication.
B) Because effects are either necessitated by God's essence or not, and supplication cannot alter that.
C) Because supplication is a form of worship without effect.
D) Because human souls are too weak to influence anything.

Key Takeaway
The first argument against supplication asserts that since all contingent effects are fully determined by God's essence, supplication cannot add any new effect nor remove an effect that is not divinely determined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you raise your hands in supplication, do you truly believe that your prayer is a means that God has decreed to bring about an effect, or do you feel that the outcome is already fixed and your prayer does nothing? Examine your heart and commit to praying with the conviction that God has made prayer a cause within His decree.
?
If the argument presented were true, would you still pray? Why or why not? Let your answer reveal what you truly believe about God's relationship with His creation. Write down a specific prayer you regularly make and reflect on whether your hope is in God alone or in the act of praying itself.
?
The passage states that if an effect is necessary, it will occur whether you pray or not. How does that challenge your motivation? Instead of weakening your prayer, can you use this as a reason to rely completely on God's will? Decide today to pray with greater humility and trust, acknowledging that only God gives effect.

Third Argument: Celestial Causes vs. Human Influence
Presents the third argument from celestial causation against supplication efficacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against supplication, which claims that only celestial causes produce events, making human influence impossible.

Core Idea
Human souls cannot influence the celestial causes of events.

Sanusi now presents the third argument against the efficacy of supplication. It is based on a cosmological model where celestial spheres are the direct causes of all earthly events. The argument challenges whether human souls, which are weak and sublunary, could possibly influence these higher causes. This passage forces us to examine the assumptions about causality that underlie the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style and the conditional 'if we were to say'.
2
Identify the premise
Find the opening statement: what are the principles of events according to this argument? The answer is 'the high celestial causes.'
This premise is crucial; it sets up the conclusion.
3
Examine the conditional argument
The author presents a hypothetical: if we say supplication changes events from non-existence to existence, what follows? The conclusion is that we attribute influence to human souls over celestial causes, which is deemed 'very far-fetched.'
Why is it far-fetched? Because human souls are weak and the causes are high.
4
Reflect on the cosmological assumption
Consider that this argument depends on an Aristotelian model where celestial spheres cause all sublunary events. Without that model, the argument loses force.
This helps you see how theology interacts with the science of its time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن مبادىء حدوث الحوادث في هذا العالم ، ليس إلا العلل العالية الفلكية . فلو قلنا : إن قبل الدعاء ما كانت تلك الحوادث : توجد . وبعد الدعاء : تدخل في الوجود . لكنا قد أثبتنا للنفوس البشرية على ضعفها نوعاً من التأثير في العلل العالية ) وذلك بعيد جداً (۱)
The third argument: The principles of the occurrence of events in this world are nothing but the high celestial causes. If we were to say that before the supplication those events did not exist, and after the supplication they come into existence, then we would have attributed to human souls, despite their weakness, a kind of influence over the high causes – and that is very far-fetched.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the efficacy of supplication. It is rooted in an Aristotelian cosmology where the celestial spheres (the high causes) are the ultimate efficient causes of all sublunary events. The argument asserts that since these celestial causes are far above human souls, it is implausible that a mere human supplication could affect them. If we were to claim that supplication causes events to occur, we would be attributing to weak human souls an influence over the high celestial causes, which is considered highly improbable. Thus, supplication cannot change the course of events determined by these high causes.

Key Terms
مبادىء حدوث الحوادث
العلل العالية الفلكية
النفوس البشرية

Misconception: The argument is invalid because it relies on an outdated Aristotelian cosmology.
Correction: The argument reflects the cosmological assumptions of its time; its logical structure remains valid within that framework, and the author is not claiming absolute truth but rather reasoning within a shared model.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes feel that your supplications are not heard because you are too insignificant? The author of this passage dismisses human influence as 'very far-fetched'. But what if the real issue is not your weakness but the distance you have created between yourself and the One who controls all causes? Think of a time you prayed fervently for something and felt it went unanswered. Did that experience make you pray less? This lesson forces you to decide: will you let a worldview that excludes divine intervention determine your prayer life, or will you cling to the belief that your Creator hears and can change anything? Write down one 'impossible' request that you have given up on, and commit to making it part of your daily supplication for the next 30 days. What would it take for you to truly believe that your prayer matters?
?
The argument here says that if supplication worked, it would mean human souls influence celestial causes—something the author finds absurd. But in your heart, do you hold that same absurdity? Have you ever acted as if your prayer was just a ritual with no real effect? Reflect on your current prayer habits: Are they filled with genuine hope and expectation, or have they become empty words? If you truly believed that your supplication could alter the course of events, what would you start praying for that you currently avoid? What stops you from believing that? This week, choose one specific area where you feel powerless and pray with absolute certainty that Allah is listening. What change would that make in your life?

The Fourth Argument: High Causes and the Futility of Supplication
Dichotomy of causation and conclusion for necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against supplication based on the deterministic nature of celestial causes.

Core Idea
If high causes act by necessity, supplication cannot change them.

This lesson presents a concise argument against the efficacy of supplication. It sets up a dichotomy: celestial causes operate either by necessity or by choice. The argument then concludes that in the first case, supplication is futile. But what about the second case? This passage leaves that open, inviting further reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage Carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's straightforward style come through.
Notice the footnote marker and bracket—these are part of the source text.
2
Identify the Two Modes of Causation
The argument presents two possibilities: necessity/nature or intention/choice. Find where each is mentioned.
3
Consider the Conclusion for Necessity
Why does the argument conclude that supplication is useless if causes act by necessity? Think about the nature of necessity: fixed and unresponsive.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن الأسباب العالية. إما أن يكون تأثيرها بالإيجاب والطبع (٢) ] أو بالقصد والاختيار. فإن كان الأول ، لم يكن في الدعاء فائدة .
The fourth argument: The high causes – either their influence is by necessity and nature, or by intention and choice. If it is the first, then there is no benefit in supplication.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against the efficacy of supplication. It begins by stating that the high celestial causes act in one of two ways: either by necessity and nature (i.e., deterministically and naturally) or by intention and choice (volitionally). The argument then draws a conclusion only for the first case: if the high causes act by necessity and nature, then supplication is useless because natural processes are fixed and unresponsive. The argument appears to be incomplete in this excerpt; it does not state the conclusion for the second case (if they act by choice), perhaps implying that supplication might then be directed to them, but that is not elaborated here.

Key Terms
الأسباب العالية
الإيجاب والطبع
القصد والاختيار
دعاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Supplication is always useless because celestial causes are necessary.
Correction: The argument only addresses the case of necessity; the possibility of volitional causes leaves room for supplication.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, supplication is useless if the high causes act in what manner?
A) By intention and choice.
B) By necessity and nature.
C) By both.
D) By neither.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument challenges supplication by positing that if celestial causes operate by necessity, supplication cannot affect them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make duʿāʾ, do you truly believe that it can change what has been decreed, or do you sometimes feel it is merely a ritual? How does this argument challenge or confirm your practice?
?

?
The argument only considered the possibility of necessity. Are you willing to embrace the second possibility—that high causes act by intention and choice—and let that shape how you approach duʿāʾ? What concrete change will you make in your supplication habits starting today?

The Third Argument: Celestial Causality
Third argument: events depend on celestial causes, not human prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against supplication efficacy, which is based on the idea that events depend on high celestial causes beyond human influence.

Core Idea
Supplication cannot change events because human souls are too weak to affect celestial causes.

The author presents a third objection to the idea that supplication can bring about events. This argument is rooted in a deterministic view of causality, where all events originate from celestial causes. The lesson unpacks this reasoning and its implications for the efficacy of human prayer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the concise argument structure.
The passage is a single logical chain.
2
Identify the premise
The argument begins with a statement about the principles of events. What are they? Identify the claim that events depend solely on celestial causes.
3
Understand the implication
If supplication were to cause events, what would that imply about human souls? The author says it would affirm a kind of influence over celestial causes, which is deemed far-fetched.
Think about the relative power of human souls vs. celestial causes.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that such influence is very unlikely. Ask yourself: does this argument assume a purely naturalistic causality, or is there room for divine intervention?
Later passages may address this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: إن مبادىء حدوث الحوادث في هذا العالم ليس إلا العلل العالية الفلكية. فلو قلنا: إن قبل الدعاء ما كانت تلك الحوادث توجد وبعد الدعاء تدخل في الوجود لكنا قد أثبتنا للنفوس البشرية على ضعفها نوعاً من التأثير في العلل العالية وذلك بعيد جداً.
The third argument: The principles of the occurrence of events in this world are nothing but the high celestial causes. If we were to say that before supplication those events did not exist and after supplication they come into existence, we would have affirmed for human souls, despite their weakness, a kind of influence over the high causes, and that is very far-fetched.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection. It asserts that all events in the world originate from celestial causes. These causes are powerful and beyond human reach. If we claimed that supplication changes events, we would be attributing to the weak human soul an ability to influence these high celestial causes. Such a claim is unreasonable, implying that supplication cannot have any real effect.

Key Terms
العلل العالية الفلكية
النفوس البشرية
التأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, what would claiming that supplication causes events imply?
A) That human souls are stronger than celestial causes.
B) That human souls can influence celestial causes.
C) That celestial causes depend on human actions.
D) That events have no causes.

Key Takeaway
The third argument holds that supplication cannot influence events because events are determined by celestial causes beyond human power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This argument labels your soul's ability to affect change as 'far-fetched.' Where in your life have you settled for resignation rather than supplication, and how will you challenge that starting today?

The Fourth Argument: Higher Causes and the Futility of Supplication
Presents the fourth argument: higher causes either act necessarily or by choice, rendering supplication futile.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against the efficacy of supplication based on the nature of higher causes, and how it presents a dilemma that seems to render supplication useless.

Core Idea
Higher causes either act necessarily or by choice; in either case, supplication cannot change the outcome.

This lesson examines a sophisticated theological objection that questions the utility of supplication. The argument presents a dilemma: if the celestial causes that govern events operate by necessity or by perfect choice, then human requests cannot alter their predetermined actions. Understanding this argument is key to appreciating later responses that defend the value of supplication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative structure come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is structured as a clear disjunction (either...or).
2
Identify the two scenarios
The argument presents two possible modes of operation for higher causes: acting necessarily (by compulsion/nature) or acting by intention and choice. Find where each is introduced.
The Arabic phrases are 'بالإيجاب والطبع' and 'بالقصد والاختيار'.
3
Analyse the implications
For each scenario, the argument draws a consequence regarding supplication. In the first, supplication has no benefit. In the second, the argument goes further: the higher causes have perfect knowledge, so if something is beneficial, they already do it; if not, requesting it is ignorance.
Ask yourself why supplication would be useless in both cases.
4
Reflect on the assumptions
The argument assumes that higher causes are either deterministic or perfectly wise. Critically examine these assumptions and consider how a theologian might respond.
This sets the stage for the refutation of this objection in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة: إن الأسباب العالية إما أن يكون تأثيرها بالإيجاب والطبع أو بالقصد والاختيار. فإن كان الأول لم يكن في الدعاء فائدة. وإن كان الثاني فعلم تلك الأسباب العالية الفعالة العالمة بمصالح هذا العالم أكمل وأتم وأوفى من علم البشر. فإن كان الأصلح حصول ذلك الشيء فتلك العلل العالية لا تبخل بها البتة. وإن كان الأصلح عدم ذلك الشيء كان طلب تحصيله بالدعاء جهلا. وحكمة العلل العالية تمنعها عن الإقدام على فعل ما لا ينبغي.
The fourth argument: The high causes, either their effect is by compulsion and nature, or by intention and choice. If the first, then there is no benefit in supplication. If the second, then the knowledge of those high causes, which are effective and cognizant of the interests of this world, is more perfect, more complete, and more comprehensive than human knowledge. So if what is most beneficial is the occurrence of that thing, then those high causes do not withhold it at all. And if what is most beneficial is the non-occurrence of that thing, then seeking to obtain it through supplication is ignorance. The wisdom of the high causes prevents them from proceeding to do what ought not to be done.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection, structured as a disjunction. It divides the possible modes of operation of the high celestial causes into two: either they act by necessity (natural compulsion) or by choice and intention. In the first case, supplication cannot influence them. In the second case, these causes have perfect knowledge of what is truly beneficial for the world. If they know that an event is beneficial, they already bring it about; if they know it is not beneficial, seeking it through supplication is foolish. Thus, supplication is useless in both scenarios.

Key Terms
الأسباب العالية
الإيجاب والطبع
القصد والاختيار
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the argument rejects supplication because it assumes higher causes are indifferent or malevolent.
Correction: The argument actually attributes perfect knowledge and wisdom to the higher causes, but concludes that this makes supplication useless because they already act optimally.

Checkpoint
In the fourth argument, if the high causes act by intention and choice, what does the argument say about the possibility of obtaining something that is not truly beneficial?
A) Supplication can persuade them to grant it.
B) Supplication is ignorance because they know what is best.
C) They might grant it out of mercy.
D) It is possible but unlikely.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument presents a dilemma that seems to eliminate any benefit from supplication, setting the stage for a theological defense of its value.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have made du'a for something you deeply desired. If the high causes already know what is best and never withhold true good, why do you still feel the need to plead? Does your supplication reveal a lack of trust in divine wisdom or a genuine act of worship? What might that say about your heart's attachment to your own plans?
?
Consider a prayer that seemed to go unanswered. This argument suggests that not receiving what you asked for might be because it was not truly beneficial for you. Are you willing to accept that your limited knowledge might have misjudged what is best? How does that challenge your understanding of God's love and wisdom?
?
If you knew with certainty that every outcome is determined by perfect wisdom, would you still pray with the same urgency? Or would your prayers become merely formal? Confront your own motivation: do you pray to change things or to submit to the One who knows all?

The Futility of Contending with Celestial Causes
Presents the fifth argument: supplication is like fighting against celestial causes, which is futile

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument against supplication based on the futility of contending with celestial causes.

Core Idea
Supplication is either useless or counterproductive against celestial causes.

This lesson presents the fifth argument from the text, which uses a disjunction to show that supplication is futile. Either celestial causes are already bringing about the desired event, making supplication unnecessary, or they are not, making supplication a futile struggle. The argument concludes that resisting celestial causes only brings negative consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Observe the conditional structure.
The passage uses two 'if' clauses to frame the argument.
2
Identify the two cases
Note the two possibilities: the celestial cause is either doing the action or refraining from it. See how each case leads to a negative conclusion about supplication.
3
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that in either case, supplication is futile. The second case even leads to harm, as the weak gains nothing but disdain.
This argument challenges the common assumption that supplication always has positive effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة: إن الأسباب العالية الفلكية إن كانت فاعلة لذلك الفعل لم يكن في هذا الدعاء فائدة. وإن كانت ممتنعة من ذلك الفعل كان اشتغال هذا الإنسان بطلب تحصيل ذلك الفعل يجري مجرى المنازعة مع الأسباب الفلكية. والضعيف إذا قاوم القوي لم يستفد منه إلا المقت والرد والحرمان.
The fifth argument: If the high celestial causes are the doers of that action, then there is no benefit in this supplication. And if they are refraining from that action, then this human’s occupation with seeking to obtain that action runs in the course of contending with the celestial causes. And the weak, when he resists the strong, gains nothing except disdain, rejection, and deprivation.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection, again using a disjunction. It first considers two possibilities: either the celestial causes are actively bringing about the event, or they are refraining from it. In the first case, supplication adds nothing. In the second case, trying to obtain the event through supplication is like opposing the powerful celestial causes. Since humans are weak compared to these causes, such opposition only leads to negative consequences, not success. Thus, supplication is either useless or counterproductive.

Key Terms
الأسباب العالية الفلكية
المنازعة
الضعيف
القوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Supplication is always beneficial and can change any outcome.
Correction: This argument shows that supplication is either irrelevant (if the cause is already acting) or harmful (if the cause is refraining), so it is not always beneficial.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, what is the outcome if a weak human contends with strong celestial causes?
A) He may succeed if he tries hard enough.
B) He gains nothing except disdain, rejection, and deprivation.
C) He can persuade them through repeated supplication.
D) He will be rewarded for his effort.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument concludes that contending with celestial causes through supplication is futile and only brings negative results.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you truly believe it can change what the 'celestial causes' have decreed, or do you sometimes feel it's futile? What does that reveal about your trust in Allah's power over all causes?
?
Have you ever stopped praying for something because you felt you were 'fighting against the stars'? How does this lesson challenge that feeling of hopelessness?
?
The lesson says the weak gain nothing but disdain when resisting the strong. Is your supplication a form of resistance against Allah's decree, or is it an act of submission and recognition that He alone can change the outcomes? Be honest with yourself.
